## S. 7

7
The following script was created with the intention of 
collecting the contents of the lectures of History of Architec­
ture I and II that took place in the winter semester of 2021 
and in the spring semester of 2022.
The structure, form and presentation of the texts are 
linked to the content of the lectures. The aim was to reduce 
the indispensable work of reading texts and sources, which 
are in any case highly recommended.
The following script is structured according to the course 
schedule and divided by lecture topic. The script is conceived 
as part of the lectures but may move beyond the content 
presented in class and it is not exhaustive of all the argu­
ments and topics presented during the teaching. Please note 
that for a successful exam preparation the script should be 
used together with the lectures’ PowerPoints published on 
the course’s website and your notes. 
General Notes

## S. 9

9
Architecture History and Theory I and II
THEME AND APPROACH
The course explores two intertwined stories about Euro­
pean architecture of the period ca. 1500–1850. It tells these 
two stories by discussing buildings and texts. 
The first story is about architects and authors who seek 
to define sound rules and principles for architectural design. 
It is in essence a history of attempts to provide architectural 
design with a sound basis, both in practice and in theory. In 
the period and region that concerns us here, this quest 
unfolds initially as an attempt to design and build architec­
ture in imitation of the Ancients. This story is important, 
because it has defined to an important extent how we here 
today understand what architecture is, what an architect 
should know and do, and how buildings should be designed. 
In other words, the history of the recovery, definition and ap­
plication of the principles of ‘classical’ architecture is, in the 
Western tradition, one of the – if not the – most important 
ways of defining architecture as an art form. In this tradi­
tion, architecture is determined by how it is designed and 
made; that is, all objects generated by the right principles 
are ‘architecture’; architects are those who master these 
principles.
In this story, some key questions and problems keep 
reoccurring: what exactly is ‘ancient architecture’? Where did 
it originate? How can we know it? What are our sources? 
What are its main components? How can we codify, commu­
nicate and teach its principles? What is the value of ancient

## S. 10

10
examples for the present? How do historical circumstances 
affect how we use classical principles? How does ‘classical’ 
architecture relate to other forms of building and design, like 
the gothic, the vernacular or the non-European? 
The course traces an historical arc with regard to these 
questions, going from the free interpretation of Antiquity in 
the 15th century over the attempts to normalize the principles 
of architecture by means of the five orders and the ensuing 
debates about the authority of Vitruvius and ancient ruins, 
to the increasingly complex understanding of Antiquity itself 
and the development of design systems that distance them­
selves from ancient precedents, or incorporate them in new 
constellations.
Crucial to this story are its limits. These limits can be 
considered internal and external to the problem at hand. 
Looking into the various attempt to define the principles of 
architecture shows how these attempts always ran into 
certain limitations, which essentially revolve around the 
problem of authority: who has the capacity to define, impose 
and verify these principles of architecture? Is it the individu­
al architect, the builder, their patron, society, the individual 
user? These internal limits become an important and explic­
it theme of architectural theory and practice from the second 
half of the 17th century, but they are present in, and intrinsic 
to, all attempts to formulate an architectural position. They 
also point to the external limits of any attempt to establish 
the principles of architecture. If, for instance, architecture is 
defined foremost as a matter of architects, many other

## S. 11

11
Architecture History and Theory I and II
actors involved in the built environment are excluded. And 
even if the builder, the patron, society or the individual user 
society are considered, it is worth wondering who or what is 
meant by those categories. Similarly, defining architecture 
implies excluding non-architecture. If architecture is a mat­
ter of public buildings and certain types of houses, such as 
palaces, where does that leave other components of the built 
environment?
These limits point to the second story of the course: how 
architecture expresses cultural meaning, and is endowed with 
it. As Vitruvius’ dedication to Augustus illustrates, from its 
inception architecture is used to express values and ideas 
that are important for certain actors society, generally those 
in power: imperial or royal authority, religious beliefs, social 
hierarchy, the functioning of the state, …. The most radical 
and fundamental figure of thought to express this nexus 
between architecture and the values of society is the origin 
myth. This type of myth presents architecture as an intrinsic 
part of society: no society without architecture, and no 
architecture without society. This double bind legitimizes the 
existence of architecture, and immediately describes its 
function: as the material expression of the very structure of 
society itself. This idea is foundational to the classicist 
notion of architecture explored in our first story, and gives 
rise to notions such as decorum, beauty or character – 
means or instruments to connect buildings to their cultural 
context. These notions hold out the promise that a well-de­
signed building will fulfill its role in society, and be appreci­
ated by all involved.

## S. 12

12
As we know, things are not that simple; in fact, our 
discussion of for instance character demonstrates how deli­
cate the relationship between architects, their patrons, build­
ings and society is. But there is more to the problem. As our 
discussion of the propagation of neo-classicism as an official 
style for modern government buildings in the US suggests, 
proposing a close bond between architecture and society 
implies specific ideas about what society is, how it should 
function, and who it welcomes. And conversely, as for in­
stance the House of Loreto demonstrates, buildings can 
attract meaning and attention regardless of whether they 
adhere to design principles. Their significance is determined 
by events, stories and myths that engender all kinds of 
behavior and actions in and around buildings that are not in 
the least concerned with beauty, proportion or the five or­
ders. In more general terms, much of what happens in the 
built environment is only marginally concerned with architec­
ture; looking at the history of the built environment through 
the lens of architecture, as we do here, leaves much unseen.
Still, much can be gleaned from architecture about the 
forces and ideas that shape a society. As part of his con­
demnation of the classical tradition, Victor Hugo already 
argued that architecture primarily exists as the expression of 
human thought, and derives its features from how thought 
shapes a society. Even if we don’t have to agree with Hugo’s 
position, his perspective on architecture draws attention to 
its context. Buildings do not express meaning on their own, 
but as elements of an environment, or in dialogue with 
images and texts found in books, plates or, indeed, build­
ings.

## S. 13

13
Architecture History and Theory I and II
By exploring these two stories, the course aims to famil­
iarize you with some key works of European architectural 
history. It also wants to show how these works reflect those 
stories. Most of all, the course hope to offer a springboard 
for your own exploration of architectural history, by high­
lighting examples, themes, questions and problems that can 
help you to probe the architecture of the past and the pres­
ent, in your own surroundings, Europe and the world at 
large.

## S. 17

17
17
1. Vitruvius
INTRODUCTION 
One of the main themes of the course is the relationship 
between architecture and books. In fact, the history and the 
theory of architecture cannot be separated. The reason for 
this is both very plain and very complicated: architecture 
simply does not exist or, better yet, it does not exist as such. 
Architecture does not grow on trees or from the earth. Ar­
chitecture is not discovered or carried inside of us. It does 
not manifest itself in the interplay of light and space—unless 
someone tells us that it does. Architecture exists because 
someone, somewhere, has decided to call it so. 
As a result, architecture does not exist without a theoret­
ical reflection, at a given moment and place. Then, to frame 
a history of architecture, we also need to understand what 
people think architecture is. This is the main concern of this 
course, and it is the subject of both buildings and books. In 
fact, it is mainly in books that we discover what people 
think about architecture. 
Possibly the most important written work in the history 
of architectural thinking is De Architectura Libri Decem, the 
Ten Books of Architecture by Vitruvius. This treatise is 
about the construction of the subject of architecture. Written 
around 30–20 BC in a complex Latin enriched with Greek 
vocabulary (a fact that has led to many passages being 
open to interpretation), Vitruvius’s Ten Books has survived 
over the centuries, copied and recopied by hand. Even 
though it provides the broadest knowledge of Roman archi­
tecture that is available to us, however, it also has opened 
many questions, which Vitruvius fails to answer and with 
which architects and historians have been confronted for 
many generations. The language, aims, and nature of Vitru­
vius’s writing comes from later interpretations and supposi­
tions. The characterization of Vitruvius himself also derives 
mostly from his text.

## S. 18

18
TO KNOW
Who Vitruvius was, how and why he wrote the Ten Books, 
and the structure of the Ten Books (cf. also slides).
The four themes discussed here, and how to explain them 
on the basis of the selected passages of the Ten Books.
The six design principles, their relationships, and their 
meaning.
Why Vitruvius’s text is important.
How architecture requires theory to exist, and how to exp­
lain this using the example of Vitruvius.

## S. 19

19
1. Vitruvius
The Carytids in Fra Giocondo's 1511 edition

## S. 20

20
WHO WAS VITRUVIUS
We don’t really know very much about Vitruvius. Most of 
what we know comes from the treatise itself. Vitruvius was 
born in Rome, in the Campania region, and belonged to a 
middle class of salaried technicians. In the highly stratified 
system of the Roman Republic, Vitruvius was charged with 
the manufacture and maintenance of machines of war. He 
participated in the final campaigns of Julius Caesar and 
after leaving the army became involved in the maintenance 
and works on the water system, under Agrippa, in 33 BC.
Vitruvius wrote the Ten Books after his retirement and 
under the protection of Octavia, the sister of Augustus, in 
the first years of the Principate (27 BC–285 AD). He proba­
bly worked on the project for decades, collecting material 
throughout his life. Vitruvius wrote at a time of great politi­
cal and economic upheaval, when the Republic was just 
being transformed into an empire with an emperor at its 
head. As the empire grew, power became more centralized in 
Rome. There was probably an ambition to give guidance to 
this process of construction and transformation in the capi­
tal and elsewhere, guidance based on safe Greek principles. 
As such, his writing was probably meant for a more general 
than a specific audience, one of prospective clients, patrons, 
and political figures.
READING VITRUVIUS
The treatise is grouped into ten sections, so that it is 
easy to navigate. Vitruvius wrote it in a manuscript form,

## S. 21

21
with each scroll corresponding to one book, and all collected 
in a box. The specific time and place in which Vitruvius 
writes is relevant to the contents of his work. In fact, most 
of his sources are Greek. Again, this further suggests the 
need for basic, fundamental principles, to address the uncer­
tain state of Roman architecture at the time. For example, 
the origin of the three orders (Doric, Ionic, and Corinthian) 
is Greek, while the Roman practice of building used other 
orders as well (Tuscan and Composite), but Vitruvius focus­
es on the Greek orders. He does not describe what was being 
practiced in the Rome in which he lived, and so his text has 
a flavor of conservativism and tradition, as if he as looking 
back. This created all sorts of confusion in later centuries, 
where architects tried to match the remains of Roman archi­
tecture with what was described in the Ten Books. In most 
instances, what Vitruvius describes does not match the reali­
ty of the time in which he was writing.
In the Ten Books, Vitruvius often mentions how compli­
cated and difficult it was to compile such a synthetic body of 
work, with all of architecture systematized in it. He arranges 
his subject matter according to the way he defines the range 
that architecture covers: 
The divisions of architecture itself are three: construc­
tion [i.e., building], gnomonics (the making of sundials), 
and mechanics. Construction in turn is divided into two 
parts, one of which is the placement of city walls and 
public works in public places; the other is the erection 
of private buildings. The allocations of public works are 
three, of which the first is defense, the second religion, 
and the third service. The architecture of defense is 
the set of principles devised so that walls, towers, and 
gates will be permanently effective in warding off enemy 
1. Vitruvius

## S. 22

22
attacks. That of religion is the establishment of sanctu­
aries for the immortal gods and of temples; that of ser­
vice is the design of public places for common use, such 
as ports, fora, porticoes, baths, theaters, promenades, 
and the other installations which are appointed in pub­
lic places for the same purposes. [Book I, chapter 3]
Each of these categories becomes the subject of one of 
the Ten Books. After two books of general introduction, 
Vitruvius deals with various building types: religious build­
ings (III and IV), public buildings (V), private buildings (VI 
and most of VII), infrastructure (VIII), gnomonics (IX) and 
mechanics, mainly machines of war (X). The titles are as 
follows:
I. First Principles and the Layout of Cities
II. Building Materials
III. Temples
IV. Corinthian, Doric and Tuscan Temples
V. Public Buildings
VI. Private Buildings
VII. Finishing
VIII. Water
IX. Sundials and Clocks
X. Machines.
THE MAJOR THEMES OF THE TEN BOOKS 
Understanding what Vitruvius wrote in his Ten Books will 
help to understand why he is so important in the history of

## S. 23

23
Western architecture. We approach his work by looking at 
four themes:
1. What is an architect
2. The principles of architecture
3. Architecture and society
4. Architecture and empire
1. WHAT IS AN ARCHITECT
This section discusses Vitruvius, Ten Books of Architec­
ture, Book I, chapter 1. The versatility of the figure of the 
architect is first theme highlighted in Vitruvius’s book. This 
ideal architect is described in different ways. The first sen­
tence of the first book introduces what the architect should 
know:
The architect’s expertise is enhanced by many disci­
plines and various sorts of specialized knowledge; all the 
works executed using these other skills are evaluated 
by his seasoned judgment. This expertise is born both 
of practice (fabrica) and of reasoning (ratiocinatio). 
Practice is the constant, repeated exercise of the hands 
by which the work is brought to completion in whatever 
medium is required for the proposed design. Reasoning, 
however, is what can demonstrate and explain the pro­
portions of completed works skillfully and systematically. 
Vitruvius is suggesting that to define an architect, we 
should be able to identify a specific knowledge profile. This 
profile involves skills—practices that one can learn and can 
be taught, but which in turn should always be evaluated by 
1. Vitruvius

## S. 24

24
judgment. This judgment is the personal take, the opinion of 
the individual. The architect then is presented as a hybrid: 
someone who knows how to apply certain skills, but who is 
also able to judge when and how these skills can or should 
be applied. This profile is achieved by two activities. The 
first is practice, or fabrica, the acquisition of knowledge that 
has to do with the manual manipulation of matter through 
repeatedly working with the hands; it is the art of making 
things. The second is reasoning, or ratiocinatio, which is the 
ability to explain what is being done. To be an architect, 
you need to have manual skills to master a craft, but only if 
you can conceptualize those skills, explain them, and justify 
them. This is what distinguishes the architect from the mere 
craftsman and grants the architect an intellectual status.
In the following passage Vitruvius addresses the ways in 
which we can access these different forms of production:
In all things, but especially in architecture, there are 
two inherent categories: the signified and the signifi­
er. The signified is the proposed subject of discussion; 
it is signified by a reasoned demonstration carried 
out according to established principles of knowledge.
Vitruvius makes an important distinction between signi­
fied and signifier. The first is the subject of a discussion: 
what you do. This is coupled with a level of reflection and 
reasoned demonstration: the signifier. That is to say, you 
should be able to explain what you are doing and why. 
According to Vitruvius, this dialectic between action and 
reflection is typical of architects and is always present. To 
acquire this sort of knowledge, it is important to learn

## S. 25

25
various disciplines. An educated architect is an experienced 
draftsman, well versed in geometry, familiar with history, a 
diligent student of philosophy, knows music, has some 
acquaintance with medicine, understands the rulings of legal 
experts, and has a clear grasp of astronomy and the ways of 
Heaven. 
Vitruvius proceeds to explain why we need to know all 
these subjects: knowledge of each helps to perform the work 
of an architect. History, for example, is relevant ‘because 
architects often include ornaments in their work, and ought 
to be able to supply anyone who asks with an explanation 
why they have introduced certain motifs.’ Vitruvius then 
proposes the example of the caryatids, loadbearing columns 
sculpted in the shape of women. The etymology of the col­
umn type, Vitruvius explains, is based on the matrons of 
Caryae, a Greek city that was punished for siding with the 
Persians during the war. After all men were killed, the wom­
en were forced to parade in an act of shame. Caryatids 
therefore literally ‘carry the weight’ of their guilt as columns. 
Hence, to properly use caryatids, architects should know the 
history of Caryae. Similarly, music provides principles of 
proportions, but it’s also practical, for example in establish­
ing the right tension of a catapult. Philosophy inspires an 
architect to do good. In all these instances Vitruvius pres­
ents different ‘uses’ of knowledge, so that the architect can 
apply this knowledge directly in his (in this case it is always 
his) activity. 
At the same time, all this knowledge provides the entry 
point into a sort of meta-knowledge, an understanding of 
the very structure of knowledge itself. If one is able to under­
1. Vitruvius

## S. 26

26
stand different disciplines, it will become apparent that they 
all behave in a similar way. According to Vitruvius, there is 
coherent body of knowledge that articulates itself in specific 
sciences. The implication is that the architect has access to 
this body of knowledge, and at the same time, the arts are 
distinguished by the fact that people in different disciplines 
adapt these general principles to their specific subject, to the 
signified of their profession. Vitruvius distinguishes the work 
and the reasoning behind it; while it may be impossible to 
master all the different kinds of work, the reasoning is 
shared across various fields of action. For example, music 
and medicine both partake in this body of knowledge, so you 
will see principles that are shared between the two, such as 
proportionality, balance, etc. The architect is able to under­
stand the relationship between all these disciplines.
According to Vitruvius, then, an architect knows a lot; he 
has a high level of general learning, balancing between 
different forms of knowledge, both theoretical and practical. 
In proposing this profile, Vitruvius is concerned with the sta­
tus of the architect as an intellectual. This conception of the 
architect as a ‘generalist’ who is familiar with a wide range 
of different knowledge is one which we still hold today.
2. THE PRINCIPLES OF ARCHITECTURE
This section discusses Vitruvius, Ten Books of Architec­
ture, Book I, chapter 2; Book III, chapter 1; and Book VI, 
chapter 20.

## S. 27

27
According to Vitruvius, there are six principles of archi­
tecture:
Architecture consists of ordering (ordinatio), which is 
called taxis in Greek, and of design (dispositio)—the 
Greeks call this diathesis—and shapeliness (eurythmia) 
and symmetry (symmetria) and correctness (decor) and 
allocation (distributio), which is called oikonomia in Greek.
1. Ordinatio
Ordering (ordinatio) is the proportion to scale of the 
work’s individual components taken separately, as 
well as their correspondence to an overall proportional 
scheme of symmetry. It is achieved through quantity, 
which in Greek is called posotes. Quantity, in turn, is the 
establishment of modules taken from the elements of 
the work itself and the agreeable execution of the work 
as a whole on the basis of the elements’ individual parts.
Vitruvius describes ordinatio as a form of mathematical 
proportion, a system adopted to make sure that the size of 
each element is related to the size of the others and that 
there is a correspondence between the parts and the whole. 
It is a quantitative form of proportion, which means that it 
is essentially a measure. This quantity is established by 
modules, which are units defining the measure of each part 
of the building. These modules can be found somewhere in 
the work itself. For instance, in a temple the module is the 
section of a column.
1. Vitruvius

## S. 28

28
2. Dispositio 
Next, design (dispositio) is the apt placement of things, 
and the elegant effect obtained by their arrangement 
according to the nature of the work. The species of 
design, which are called ideai in Greek, are these: ich­
nography (plan), orthography (elevation), and scenog­
raphy. Ichnography is the skillful use, to scale, of com­
pass and rule, by means of which the on-site layout of 
the design is achieved. Next, orthography is a frontal 
image, one drawn to scale, rendered according to the 
layout for the future work. As for scenography, it is the 
shaded rendering of the front and the receding sides as 
the latter converge on a point. These species are pro­
duced by analysis and invention. Analysis is devoted 
concern and vigilant attention to the pleasing execution 
of a design. Next, invention is the unraveling of obscure 
problems, arriving, through energetic flexibility, at a 
new set of principles. These are the terms for design.
Vitruvius presents dispositio, or design, as the way you 
place things so that you can obtain an elegant ensemble. In 
that sense, it is again a kind of proportion, though not 
determined by how a building is perceived. Vitruvius specifies 
that there are three techniques that can be used to explore 
dispositio: drawing plans (ichnographia), elevations (or­
thographia), and a sort of perspective view of the outside of 
the building (scenographia). 
3. Symmetria
Symmetry is the proportioned correspondence of the el­
ements of the work itself, a response, in any given part,

## S. 29

29
of the separate parts to the appearance of the entire 
figure as a whole. Just as in the human body there is a 
harmonious quality of shapeliness expressed in terms of 
the cubit, foot, palm, digit, and other small units, so it 
is in completing works of architecture. For instance, in 
temples, this symmetry derives from the diameter of the 
columns, or from the triglyph, or from the lower radius 
of the column; in a ballista, it derives from the hole 
that the Greeks call peritreton, in boats from the [spac­
ing of the] oarlock, which the Greeks call the diapegma 
likewise for all the other types of work, the reckoning of 
symmetries is to be found among their component parts.
Symmetria is the correspondence of the parts of the work 
according to a proportional system. The choice of words 
here suggests that there is a relationship between symmetry 
and ordering. Vitruvius provides some examples of symmet­
rical objects. In particular, the body provides units (feet, 
hands, digits) that can be adopted in describing its propor­
tional system. Therefore, in each body one can find elements 
or distances that govern the measuring system of the whole 
thing. 
4. Eurythmia 
For Vitruvius, shapeliness, or eurythmia, is an attractive 
appearance and a coherent aspect in the composition of the 
elements. Like dispositio, it depends on how a building is 
seen or viewed. It is achieved when the elements of the 
project are proportionate—height to width, length to 
breadth—and every element corresponds in its dimensions to 
the total measure of the whole. If something has a visual co­
herence and harmony, it has shapeliness. 
1. Vitruvius

## S. 30

30
5. Decor
Next, correctness (decor) is the refined appearance of a 
project that has been composed of proven elements and 
with authority. It is achieved with respect to function, 
which is called thematismos in Greek, or tradition, or 
nature. Correctness of function occurs when temples 
dedicated to Jupiter the Thunderer and Heaven or the 
Sun and Moon are made open-air shrines, beneath their 
patron deity, because we see the appearance and effect 
of these divinities in the light of the outdoor world. 
Temples of Minerva, Mars, and Hercules will be Doric, 
because temples for these gods, on account of their 
courage in battle, should be set up without a trace of 
embellishment. Temples done in the Corinthian style for 
Venus, Proserpina, or the Fountain Spirits (nymphs) are 
those that will seem to possess the most fitting quali­
ties, because, given the delicacy of these goddesses, the 
works executed in their honor seem best to augment a 
suitable quality of correctness when they are made more 
slender, ornamental, and are decorated with leaves and 
volutes. If temples are constructed in the Ionic style for 
Juno, Diana, Father Liber, and other gods of this type, 
the principle of the ‘mean’ will apply, because their par­
ticular disposition will strike a balance between the stern 
lines of the Doric and the delicacy of the Corinthian.
Decor has to do with the way in which a project relates 
to rules. These rules are not produced by the project itself, 
like in symmetria, nor do they depend on how a building is 
composed or viewed. The outside world—the ideas, princi­
ples, and expectations formulated by society—figures into a 
project through decor. Vitruvius posits that there are ‘univer­
sal’ rules the building has to obey; these depend on ‘author­
ity’ or ‘proven elements’. In other words, Vitruvius recogniz­

## S. 31

31
es the existence of some form of authority outside of the 
realm of architecture itself. There are three ways in which 
these universal rules apply to a building: function, tradition, 
and nature. A building has to answer to a purpose (func­
tion), follow certain ways of doing (tradition) and fit within 
its context (nature). 
Function, however, does not necessarily mean what we 
intend today. For Vitruvius, it refers to what we call the 
three orders, or rather ‘genera’, genres, that relate to tem­
ples dedicated to different kinds of gods. Vitruvius defines 
the extremes of the Doric and the Corinthian by distinguish­
ing their build (sturdy versus slender) and especially their 
embellishment (none for the Doric, while refined ornament 
for the Corinthian). The Ionic is the mean between the two. 
While these distinctions are to some degree gendered, it is 
only the Corinthian that is reserved exclusively for the god­
desses. Relating appearance to meaning in this way consti­
tutes decor and can thus be understood as a form of func­
tion.
Tradition tells us how to do things in the way they are 
supposed to be done, according to the customs of living and 
building. Vitruvius explains this principle by referring to two 
different aspects of architecture: consistency in the layout of 
the house, where the same degree of refinement should be 
sustained throughout, and consistency in the composition of 
ornament: 
Correctness of tradition will be expressed if, when 
buildings have magnificent interiors, their vestibules 
have been made equally harmonious and elegant, for 
1. Vitruvius

## S. 32

32
if interiors were outfitted elegantly, but had entrances 
deficient in dignity and respectability they would lack 
correctness. Likewise, if Doric entablatures are sculpt­
ed with dentils in the cornices, or triglyphs show up 
atop cushion capitals and Ionic entablatures, so that 
characteristics from one set of principles have been car­
ried over into another type of work, the appearance of 
the result will be jarring, because the work was estab­
lished according to a different sequence of conventions.
Finally, nature and natural correctness tells us how to 
build according to our surroundings. A temple dedicated to 
the healing powers of water should be built next to a source 
of water. This also means that we should use the building 
materials that are found and available in the site as a way 
to be efficient and adapt to the context. Vitruvius writes,
Natural correctness occurs as follows: if, from the outset, 
temple sites are chosen in the most healthful regions, 
well supplied with suitable sources of water, but espe­
cially for the building of shrines to Asclepius, Health, 
and those gods by whose medicines the sick seem to be 
healed in the greatest numbers. When patients have been 
transferred from a pestilent to a healthful place and are 
afforded the use of waters from healthful springs, they 
will recover more quickly, and so it will be arranged that 
from the very nature of the place the divinity in question 
will receive a greater and greater reputation along with 
the dignity of divine rank. Likewise, natural correctness 
will obtain if the light source for bedrooms and libraries 
comes from the east, whereas the source for baths and 
winter quarters comes from the west in winter, while in 
the case of picture galleries and whatever areas need 
a constant level of illumination it should come from 
the north, because that region of the sky is neither

## S. 33

33
made bright nor dark by the course of the sun, but re­
mains dependable and unchanging throughout the day.
6. Distributio 
Allocation is the efficient management of resources 
and site and the frugal, principled supervision of work­
ing expenses. This will be observed if from the outset 
the architect forbears to require things that cannot be 
found at all or only procured at great expense. After 
all, not every place has an abundant supply of pit sand 
or rubble or fir, or deal planks, or marble. Different 
resources occur in different places, and their transport 
elsewhere is difficult and expensive. Where there is no 
pit sand, river sand or washed seashore sand should 
be used instead; if there is a shortage of fir or of deal 
planks, use cypress, poplar, elm or pitch pine. Oth­
er problems should be resolved in a similar fashion.
The other level of allocation obtains when buildings 
are designed differently according to the habits of 
the heads of families, or the amount of money avail­
able, or to suit their prestige as public speakers. Ur­
ban dwellings ought to be set up in one way, and 
rustic holdings, where harvests must be gathered, in 
another; the homes of moneylenders, certainly other­
wise, and still otherwise the homes of those who are 
fortunate and sophisticated. For those powerful men 
by whose counsel the republic is governed, dwellings 
should be designed to accommodate their activities, 
and in every case the allocation of buildings should 
be appropriate to every different type of person.
Distributio, or allocation, is about building according to 
the means at your disposal. If the client is wealthy, you will 
be able to build a big house, and if they are not, the house 
1. Vitruvius

## S. 34

34
will be smaller. Architects should be able to efficiently use 
the means at their disposal, so that the building is appropri­
ate to the person that will use it. Then, you will also build 
according to the resources of the building site, which closely 
relates to decor. In general, allocation suggests that the 
means always correspond to the desired end result. 
Relationship between the six principles
We can now see that these six principles, ordinatio, dispo­
sitio, eurythmia, symmetria, decor, and distribution, are actu­
ally related. Ordinatio and symmetria both have to do with 
the quantitative mathematical proportions of buildings. 
Dispositio and eurythmia have to do with the visual harmony 
perceived in a building. Distributio and decor have to do with 
the means at one’s disposal and the way in which one 
should meet expectations.
To relate these concepts, we can adopt aspects of design 
practice and the results of that action in the project. Ordina­
tio is an action, the choice of a module that in turn works 
as a proportional system. Its outcome is symmetria. Similar­
ly, if the building’s parts are disposed correctly to one an­
other (ordinatio), we obtain eurythmia. If we use the resourc­
es at our disposal according to what is available and 
expected (distributio), we will obtain correctness, decor, 
where a building is consistent, correct in its use of the or­
ders, and commensurate with the social status of the inhabi­
tant (note here that the pairing of distributio/decor defines 
distinct sets of rules for public and private buildings). These 
are three principles, made of an action and its result: math­

## S. 35

35
ematical proportion, visual proportion, and context.
The principle of symmetria
The principle discussed most persistently throughout the 
Ten Books is symmetria. In the opening passage of the first 
book on temples (Book III), which is one of the most im­
portant of the entire work, Vitruvius argues that because 
temples exist forever, it is particularly important to achieve 
perfect proportions. The model for a perfect proportional 
system is the human body. As in the body, each part of a 
temple has to relate well to the rest. Actually, the human 
body is such a perfect system of proportion because it can 
be inscribed in both a circle and a square:
Similarly, indeed, the elements of holy temples should 
have dimensions for each individual part that agree 
with the full magnitude of the work. So, too, for ex­
ample, the center and midpoint of the human body 
is, naturally, the navel. For if a person is imagined 
lying back with outstretched arms and feet within a 
circle whose center is at the navel, the fingers and toes 
will trace the circumference of this circle as they move 
about. But to whatever extent a circular scheme may 
be present in the body, a square design may also be 
discerned there. For if we measure from the soles of 
the feet to the crown of the head, and this measure­
ment is compared with that of the outstretched hands, 
one discovers that this breadth equals the height, 
just as in areas which have been squared off by use 
of the set square. The manner in which architectural 
orders are used is also crucially defined by the social 
context. Appropriateness of application is important.
1. Vitruvius

## S. 36

36
What we see here is a description of one of the most 
famous representations of the human body, the Vitruvian 
man. This figure fits within Vitruvius’s idea of symmetria 
and is linked to the architecture of the temple, the building 
that should be governed by proportion in all its parts.
As we have seen, however, Vitruvius provides two differ­
ent ways of approaching proportion. The first is from the 
inside out, where a module establishes the dimensions of all 
parts of the building (symmetria). The other operates from 
the outside, where the composition of the building and its 
parts is determined by how it is perceived (eurythmia). 
Vitruvius recognizes in his Ten Books that these two kinds 
of principles are not the same, and he demonstrates this 
when looking at houses, in Book VI: 
Nothing should be of greater concern to the architect 
than that, in the proportions of each individual ele­
ment, buildings have an exact correspondence among 
their sets of principles. Thus, once the principle of the 
symmetries has been established and the dimensions 
have been developed by reasoning, then it is the special 
skill of a gifted architect to provide for the nature of the 
site, or the building’s appearance, or its function, and 
make adjustments by subtractions or additions, should 
something need to be subtracted from or added to the 
proportional system, so that it will seem to have been de­
signed correctly with nothing wanting in its appearance.
Vitruvius is suggesting that, in a way, there is an order 
for adopting principles of proportion. After introducing the 
principles of symmetria, these principles have to be reworked

## S. 37

37
to adapt to the specific conditions of the site (eurythmia). 
He explains how this works by referring to optical phenome­
na. In a way, we don’t see things the way they are. There 
are factors that affect the way we perceive the world. For 
example, when the oars of a boat are lowered into the water, 
they no longer seem straight. This is also true for architec­
ture, he says. A house in a city looks different from the 
same house in the countryside. The good architect should be 
able to deal with these conditions and adapt the principles 
of proportions to the context, because the context deter­
mines how we perceive things.
Vitruvius thus approaches design from two different 
angles, one mathematical and one optical. There is another 
fundamental distinction at work as well, which we may call 
typological, between the architecture of temples and that of 
the private dwelling. The temple is an example of architec­
ture that is determined by perfect symmetria and a correct 
use of the established ‘genera’, whereas the house should be 
adapted to the status of the inhabitant, have an internal 
coherence, and adopt its composition much more to the 
circumstances of its surroundings. In modern words, the 
house is more contextual. This aspect will be developed 
further in the next two themes.
Summary
• 
The six principles (ordinatio, dispositio, eurythmia, 
symmetria, décor, and distributio) do not structure the 
Ten Books, but are present throughout the treatise in 
different forms. 
1. Vitruvius

## S. 38

38
• 
The most persistent notion is symmetria; it governs 
the design not just of buildings but of every kind of 
architecture, including machines. 
• 
But Vitruvius is also aware that designing according 
to an abstract proportional system (already based on 
fractions of whole numbers, and therefore limited) 
does not guarantee the pleasing appearance of a 
building, nor an appropriate disposition of its ele­
ments. Hence the importance of eurythmia, visual 
harmony. 
• 
Decor pervades the treatise in a different way: in the 
application of the ‘genera’ (orders) but also in regard 
to the private dwelling. Decor raises the issue of 
authority: who or what determines what is correct in 
architecture.
• 
These three issues—proportion, visual harmony, and 
decor/authority—will remain at the center of architec­
tural theory until well into the 19th century. Many of 
the discussions we will encounter throughout the 
course can be related back to these terms.
3. ARCHITECTURE AND SOCIETY
This section discusses Vitruvius, Ten Books of Architec­
ture, Book II, chapter 1.
Book II treats the materials of architecture. To explain 
how the availability of materials determined how people 
built, Vitruvius presents to us the origins of architecture. 
The narrative technique of adopting origin stories as a way 
to discuss different disciplinary fields was common in antiq­
uity. Because Vitruvius situates the origins of architecture at

## S. 39

39
the very origin of society, the importance of the story far 
exceeds the aim to introduce the various building materials; 
the story he tells establishes a close connection between the 
very existence of architecture and society itself.
According to Vitruvius, it all began with fire. During a 
storm, lightning struck a tree and set it on fire. Primitive 
men found that it was warm and, after perfecting how to 
keep it alive, they began to communicate with each other 
around the fire, first with sounds then with words and sen­
tences, and thus language emerged. Hence, the first basis 
for gathering people together as a society was fire. In addi­
tion, Vitruvius argues that, by sitting together by the fire, 
men started considering how they situate themselves in the 
universe around them; they also became aware that they 
could craft things. In imitation of animals, they began to 
construct forms for dwelling. By watching each other, men 
improved their skills, because according to Vitruvius, men 
are imitative; they learn from each other. They experimented 
with various primitive construction techniques.
At this point in the story, Vitruvius makes an important 
digression. To prove that his version of the events is true, he 
takes his reader on a tour of the Roman empire and discuss­
es the construction techniques found in various regions. He 
also refers to the primitive huts preserved on the Capitoline 
Hill in Rome, that were identified as the dwellings of the 
city’s founders. The implications are clear: it is in Rome that 
architecture evolved the farthest from its primitive origins; 
and all architecture shares such similar primitive origins. 
Thus, Vitruvius’s story is universal.
1. Vitruvius

## S. 40

40
Vitruvius then returns to the origin story. Certain men 
became experts in building and further specialized to prac­
tice only construction. These men looked at their own bodies 
and discovered symmetry and proportion. Finally, by apply­
ing human proportions to building, men ‘invented’ architec­
ture. Therefore, it is by applying reasoning (rationatio) to 
practice (fabrica) that architecture can exist. 
This origin story is an important part of Vitruvius’s book 
for a number of reasons. It explains how the various princi­
ples that he proposed in the first book came into being: that 
building requires craft and reasoning, that architecture is 
based in proportion (symmetria), and that construction is 
adapted to the materials provided in a certain region. More 
fundamentally, Vitruvius established a very close connection 
between the emergence of society and the birth of architec­
ture. In fact, for Vitruvius, architecture and society originat­
ed together. There is no society without architecture and, as 
a consequence, no architecture without society. In Vitruvi­
us’s story, architecture emerges naturally from the very 
development of society, and he also suggests that this 
course of events is true and universal.
These claims tell us a lot about the function of the origin 
story in Vitruvius’s text. The origin story is a device or a 
technique to portray his theory of architecture as something 
natural. In other words, if we believe Vitruvius’s origin story, 
we will also accept his theory of architecture, and consider 
and define architecture according to his view; this view, in 
turn, is ‘naturalized’. With the story, Vitruvius tries to ‘nat­
uralize’ architecture, to make it part of human evolution. 
Architecture for Vitruvius is completely intertwined with the

## S. 41

41
very fabric of human society. 
Summary
• 
According to Vitruvius, architecture emerged in paral­
lel with human society. 
• 
Vitruvius says that architecture evolved from primitive 
constructions that imitated nature and animals to 
sophisticated buildings based on symmetria. 
• 
The origin story, as the introduction to the book on 
materials, emphasizes the close connection between 
material, region, building techniques, typologies, and 
even style. 
• 
Vitruvius uses a historical account to legitimize his 
origin story. 
• 
Vitruvius’s origin account explains and naturalizes 
key theoretical notions of his treatise: fabrica/ratioci­
natio; symmetria/tradition; specialization. 
• 
From the Renaissance onward, the origin myth would 
become one of the most powerful and ubiquitous 
devices to legitimize and develop design theories.
4. ARCHITECTURE AND EMPIRE
This section discusses Vitruvius, Ten Books of Architec­
ture, Dedication to Augustus; Book 1, chapter 3; and Book 
VI, chapter 1.
As an intrinsic part of society, architecture can be used 
as an instrument of power. Indeed, while Vitruvius tries to 
build a universal definition of architecture, the world in 
1. Vitruvius

## S. 42

42
which his treatise was generated, where architecture acquired 
meaning and sense for him, was a very specific one: the 
world of the Romans. There is in fact a very close connec­
tion between the practice of architecture and the Roman 
empire.
To begin with, Vitruvius dedicates his book to the emper­
or Augustus, as a way to build an association between 
architecture and the power and mission of an empire. In oth­
er words, a great empire is made of great architecture, and 
architecture, in turn, is the instrument of an empire. In his 
dedication, Vitruvius identifies architecture as one of the 
building blocks of the empire, just like the army, law, or 
religion. As a consequence, he writes, an emperor should 
know how to build; that is, he should be able to judge what 
is good, so as to direct constructions worthy of his name. 
This ability is all the more important because these build­
ings will also be the future monuments of his rule. Since 
Augustus established peace, the time had come to allow 
architecture to flourish. Architecture, says Vitruvius, helps to 
build the empire in the present and preserve its reputation in 
the future; the greatness of architecture reflects the success 
of the emperor. The close connection between architecture 
and empire is reflected in how Vitruvius defines architecture 
(as mentioned earlier): buildings (but also cities), infra­
structure, machines (tools of war), and sundials (instru­
ments to impose a uniform measure of time across the 
empire). 
The same close connection becomes visible in a more 
surprising context, that of the private house. In Book VI, 
Vitruvius sets out to discuss how the architecture (actually

## S. 43

43
‘symmetries’) of dwellings changes according to their ‘lati­
tude’, whether they are positioned more to the north than to 
the south. Vitruvius explains how the climate affects the 
constitution of people. Basing his thought on current medi­
cal theories (especially the physician and philosopher Ga­
len), he points out that people living in the dark, cold, moist 
North will be different from those situated in the sunny, 
warm, and dry South. This is visible in their bodies, he says: 
northerners are tall, have deep voices, pale skins, and blue 
eyes, and are strong but not very smart. Those in the south 
are small, have dark skin and curly hair, and are weak but 
cunning. Vitruvius uses this observation to explain the 
superiority of Rome: as it is situated in the middle, Romans 
are smarter than the northerners and stronger than the 
southerners, and therefore destined to rule the world.
Summary
• 
This last passage is perhaps one of the most as­
tounding parts of the book, because a consideration 
of how the architecture of dwellings should be adapted 
to climate leads to a legitimation of the Roman em­
pire. It is almost like a very long zoom, which is 
ultimately framed by the very structure of the cosmos, 
which governs the climate of the earth. This ‘zoom’ 
again illustrates how closely connected architecture 
and society are in Vitruvius’s view, and how this 
connection is exemplified by the Roman empire. This 
connection gives particular meaning to architecture, 
as it becomes the expression of the state of a particu­
lar society.
1. Vitruvius

## S. 44

44
• 
The connection between architecture and empire, and 
especially its articulation in the dedication to Augus­
tus, will remain present in the Western tradition. It 
will be referenced explicitly, for instance in Raphael’s 
letter to Leo X (see chapter 3), and in the Cours 
d’architecture by François Blondel (see chapter 15). 
• 
Finally, these passages suggest how closely connected 
the very definition of architecture as a discipline is to 
the idea of power and empire.

## S. 45

45
CONCLUSION
Vitruvius’s treatise on architecture makes visible how the 
subject of architecture can be constructed by means of an 
elaborate argument that touches on a wide range of topics. 
The way Vitruvius selects and arranges these topics would 
be of lasting influence from the Renaissance onwards. The 
choices he makes become the template for the architectural 
treatise, a type of text that shows what architecture is by 
explaining how it is made. He defines ‘architecture’ by ex­
plaining the principles, actions, and instruments that should 
be used to make it, and by classifying the results of this 
process into different types of objects, each with their own 
characteristics (such as the various building types). In fact, 
he says, only objects made according to the principles laid 
down in his treatise are architecture. Finally, the specific 
topics and themes that Vitruvius treats will have a lasting 
influence on Western architecture.

47
2.
Brunelleschi 
and the 15th
Century

## S. 49

49
2. Brunelleschi and the 15th Century
INTRODUCTION 
In the first stages of the Renaissance, known as the Early 
Renaissance, a cultural and artistic process developed 
through a synthesis of the medieval Florentine tradition with 
a renewed interest in antiquity. This cultural upheaval was 
centered in Florence and is very much connected to the 
figure of Filippo Brunelleschi. The subsequent shift from 
Florence as a cultural center of artistic production to Rome 
identifies the beginning of the so-called High Renaissance. 
The person who represents this passage is Donato Bra­
mante, whose work shaped the entire 16th century and her­
alded a new interest in Roman antiquity. The cultural pro­
cesses of the Renaissance had an everlasting effect on the 
centuries that followed, especially in the quest for antiquity 
and for the interpretation of ancient models through new 
architectural theories and buildings.

## S. 50

50
The context that allowed the Renaissance to flourish and 
how it differed from other renaissances.
Names of important artists, humanists, and architects of 
the Renaissance, together with their works.
How Brunelleschi referred to a local tradition and to argue 
how this differed from the reception of his architecture.
Names of treatises of the 15th century and to argue about 
the attitude to Vitruvian culture.
The change in attitude towards antiquity in the high 
Renaissance.
Projects by Bramante and their important features.
TO KNOW

## S. 51

51
2. Brunelleschi and the 15th Century
Francesco di Giorgio Martini, Trattati di architettura, 1480/1490.

## S. 52

52
THE EARLY REINASSANCE 
For the history of architecture, the beginning of the 
Renaissance, or the Early Renaissance period, is generally 
considered to be the year 1420, when Filippo Brunelleschi 
(1377–1446) begun the construction for the dome of Santa 
Maria del Fiore in Florence, in the Tuscany region it the 
Italian peninsula. Beyond this historical convention, different 
aspects shaped the Florentine Quattrocento. A period of 
political stability and peace, followed by large-scale econom­
ic growth, led to the development of a new urban and artis­
tic culture. The arts were also able to flourish through a 
competitive climate of city-states throughout the Italian pen­
insula, all striving for cultural supremacy. The important 
intercession of patrons investing in art allowed personalities 
such as Brunelleschi to flourish. One of the first consequenc­
es of these trends was a general rise in education, especially 
in the humanities. 
Philosophical curricula were developed in the first human­
istic schools, where a wide interest in classical knowledge 
and antiquity led to a deeper study of ancient writings, such 
as the work of Vitruvius. Many of these writings were of 
course already known, but they had never been treated with 
this level of attention. The processes of copying and study­
ing classical texts in turn led to the diffusion of this materi­
al, resulting in new and more rigorous forms of classical 
knowledge and a greater appreciation for antiquity. This 
ultimately shaped a collective consciousness around the 
existence of historical ruptures, such as the Middle Ages, 
which itself was a concept formed not in the Middle Ages 
but during the Early Renaissance.

## S. 53

53
2. Brunelleschi and the 15th Century
Filippo Brunelleschi 
Born in Florence in 1377, Brunelleschi was trained as a 
sculptor and belonged to the prestigious Florentine guild of 
silk merchants. The construction for the dome of the Flor­
ence Cathedral of Santa Maria del Fiore is considered 
Brunelleschi’s highest achievement. Design for the dome 
began in 1418. Brunelleschi, at the time working primarily as 
a goldsmith, produced a successful design for the large 
dome of the church through an open competition. The drum, 
stretching for more than 40 meters, was the biggest ever 
known, bigger even than the Pantheon. Brunelleschi came up 
with a number of ingenious technical solutions to address 
the constructive problems of the dome, including stone 
anchors, a double shell, and hanging scaffolds instead of 
supporting structures. Thanks to the successful completion 
of the dome of Florence, the design became associated 
entirely with Brunelleschi’s persona, the reason he became 
famous. It was a unique and innovative work of art created 
from the ‘exhaustion of his God-given talents’.
Brunelleschi appears time and again in the writings of 
Leon Battista Alberti, an important writer and architect of 
the 15th century, as the epitome of a creative spirit. For 
Alberti, Brunelleschi expressed an important sentiment of 
the time: a generalized optimism and the conviction that an 
unprecedented cultural awakening was at hand, principally 
through the world of art. Over the following centuries, the 
image of Brunelleschi was cemented and gained even more 
recognition as the father of Renaissance architecture. In 
reality, Brunelleschi was not a rigorous scholar of Roman 
antiquity, as demonstrated by his use of the orders (mostly

## S. 54

54
Corinthian). This strongly suggests that he was not as 
interested in the re-foundation of classical architecture as he 
had long been depicted, and it is unclear whether he ever 
visited Rome.
Other Projects by Brunelleschi 
Ospedale degli Innocenti, Florence
This project is a hospital for abandoned children and 
was commissioned by the powerful guild of silk merchants, 
to which Brunelleschi belonged. The unique and most impor­
tant feature of this building is the loggia, which runs along 
three sides of the building. This type of space already exist­
ed (see, for example, the Loggia dei Lanzi in Piazza della 
Signoria), but Brunelleschi transformed it by turning pillars 
into columns, adding bases and capitals, closing the arches 
with a cornice, and shaping the proportions and the profiles 
of the entablatures. The vaults of the portico consist of 
clean geometrical shapes obtained by adopting the section 
of a sphere (creating hemispherical vaults). The use of pure 
geometric forms in fact is visible throughout, in the repeti­
tion of circles and squares in both plan and elevation.
Florence of the 15th century was characterized by what is 
known as Proto-Renaissance, where ancient models from 
antiquity were combined with typical features from 11th- and 
12th-century Tuscany. Some elements in the Ospedale degli 
Innocenti, such as the proportions of the columns, the com­
position of the arches, and the windows on the upper level, 
can be traced to the 11th-century Florentine basilica of San 
Miniato al Monte. Another influence from the Florentine

## S. 55

55
2. Brunelleschi and the 15th Century
Proto-Renaissance was the interior of the Romanesque 
church of Santi Apostoli (11th century), especially for its 
Corinthian columns. Perhaps the most important reference 
for Brunelleschi was the Romanesque Florentine Baptistery 
(11th-12th century), at the time (erroneously) considered an 
ancient building erected under Emperor Augustus as the 
Temple of Mars. Referring to it therefore meant establishing 
a direct relationship with antiquity.
The Ospedale degli Innocenti, however, actually features 
elements that were completely unthinkable in ancient archi­
tecture, such as the architrave, which ornamentally breaks 
and contradicts the ideal function of the entablature as a 
load-bearing element. This and other references suggest 
that, in reality, Brunelleschi never did establish a direct 
connection to Roman antiquity with the Ospedale degli Inno­
centi, but rather he was inspired by a number of examples 
embedded in the local Proto-Renaissance culture.
The Old Sacristy of San Lorenzo, Florence
Giovanni de’ Medici, the first important representative of 
the Medici family to become a patron of the Renaissance, 
commissioned the Old Sacristy as an addition to the old 
basilica of San Lorenzo. Later on, the whole basilica under­
went important changes and more additions, one of which 
was Michelangelo’s New Sacristy (see chapter 8).
The room of the Old Sacristy was regulated by simple 
geometric shapes, such as a square plan. Both the main 
dome and the side dome sit on arched pendentives and are 
therefore inscribed in a square. The most striking feature of

## S. 56

56
the Sacristy is the use of pilasters and their relationship to 
the walls. Corners are marked and structured by architectur­
al elements that form a sort of colored scaffolding distin­
guishable from the light plaster of the wall. These additional 
architectural elements are built in the typical Florence pietra 
serena, a grey-green sandstone, establishing a correspond­
ence between the walls and the arches. The pilasters are 
articulated in three different ways—bent inwards, turned 
around a corner, or as extruded profiles.
Again, in the context of the quest for ‘architettura all’an­
tica’ (architecture in the manner of the ancients), 
Brunelleschi’s use of pilasters for the Sacristy is ambiguous. 
According to Alberti, who was much more rigorous in his 
classical research, pilasters are structurally independent from 
walls. By this rationale, Brunelleschi’s Sacristy can be read 
as a building resting on pilasters and arches that have been 
‘absorbed’ into the walls, rather than a shell of masonry 
with applied decorative pilasters. 
Church of San Lorenzo, Florence
Similar to the Ospedale degli Innocenti, the church of 
San Lorenzo is characterized by the inclusion of elements 
from antiquity that are combined with medieval features. In 
this case, Brunelleschi was probably inspired by the cathe­
dral of Florence, especially in the verticality of the central 
space, which recalls Florentine Gothic architecture. This 
design strategy can be described as a synthesis between 
classical models, such as round arches, and more recent 
forms, which Brunelleschi observed in other Proto-Renais­
sance Florentine architecture. Individual elements, such as

## S. 57

57
2. Brunelleschi and the 15th Century
classical columns and pilasters, bases, and entablatures, are 
assembled within an overall composition that is still evoca­
tive of medieval architecture. In other words, the original 
system is retained, but the forms have changed: instead of 
the pointed arch, Brunelleschi adopted the round arch, and 
instead of the ribbed vault, he employed the hanging or 
pendant dome. This substitution of forms and elements in 
Renaissance design is generally carried out as follows:
• 
A medieval structure is replaced with antique pilasters 
or columns;
• 
The ‘suggested’ entablature is replaced with a com­
plete one (architrave, frieze, cornice);
• 
The pointed arch is replaced with the round arch;
• 
The cross-ribbed vault is replaced with the compound 
dome, or ‘Pendentif Kuppel’.
ARCHITECTURAL THEORY IN THE RENAISSANCE
During the 16th century, many architectural treatises were 
devoted to the theme of the orders. Special abridged edi­
tions, the so-called ‘column-books’ (‘säulenbücher’), were 
dedicated entirely to columns. While at the beginning of the 
15th century the management of the orders was much more 
creative, by the end of the Renaissance this had become 
more rigorous and canonized. Treatises would often begin 
with a synopsis of the column, how the orders are composed, 
and their ideal hierarchy (a late example is Scamozzi’s 
treatise, discussed in chapter 4). This sort of architectural 
theory was based on two kinds of sources: ruins and texts.

## S. 58

58
Ruins
Ruins were a complicated matter, as nothing had been 
excavated and there was not enough knowledge to classify 
the buildings chronologically. Furthermore, Roman ruins 
mostly featured Corinthian and Composite orders. Very few 
examples of Doric and Ionic architecture survived into the 
Renaissance, and no Doric temple at all. Chief references in 
Rome for these orders included:
• 
The portico of the Basilica Aemilia, once a public 
building at the Forum, composed by columned halls 
but with completely non-canonical capitals;
• 
A fragment of the Agrippa Thermae;
• 
The lower floor of the Theatre of Marcellus, featuring 
Doric semi-columns;
• 
The Colosseum, featuring the Tuscan order on the 
ground floor;
• 
The small Ionic temple at the Forum Boarium.
The primary sources of the Renaissance were therefore 
essentially all in a ruinous state, half handed down, and 
therefore in need of interpretation, which was often difficult 
to reconcile with written sources. By means of drawing, these 
ancient ruins were thus collected and creatively interpreted.
Texts
Texts formed the second important source for referencing 
antiquity. These all referred, with no exceptions, to Vitruvius 
as the main authority for ‘architettura all’antica’. However,

## S. 59

59
2. Brunelleschi and the 15th Century
as already discussed, Vitruvius did not treat the orders 
systematically, leaving the topic open to colorful interpreta­
tions. 
Two passages in the Ten Books are particularly relevant 
in regard to the orders: Book I, chapter 2 and Book IV, 
chapter 1. In the former, Vitruvius deals with the temple as 
an example of decor, because one employs decor to establish 
which order of columns is better suited to which temple, and 
therefore to which god. He presents the Doric temple, the 
Corinthian temple, and the Ionic temple, but none are ex­
plained sufficiently. With such little information, one could 
not re-draw or reconstruct these temples exactly. In Book 
IV, Vitruvius provides a historical excursus on the origins of 
the orders. In reality, however, he only introduces the Doric, 
derived from the strong male body, and the Ionic, derived 
from the slender female body. The invention of the Corinthi­
an is briefly mentioned as an adaptation of the Ionic to an 
even slimmer proportion, which Vitruvius associates with the 
figure of a virgin.
The Tuscan order is mentioned elsewhere, completely 
unrelated to the Greek column types, while the Composite 
order is not mentioned at all. Furthermore, Vitruvius does 
not explain how columns, architraves, and the other ele­
ments fit together. As a result, the Ten Books did not really 
provide a standard definition of the orders, which were pro­
cessed only during the Renaissance, thus becoming a prod­
uct of the 16th century.

## S. 60

60
Leon Battista Alberti
Leon Battista Alberti (1404–72) is considered the em­
bodiment of the ideal ‘uomo universale’, the universal man 
of the Renaissance. Born in Genoa, Alberti belonged to a 
Florentine family that had been exiled. He studied ecclesias­
tical law in Bologna and Padua and became a writer. In 
1435 he was finally allowed to come back to Florence. Alber­
ti wrote on a number of topics, such as theology and satire, 
and also on art and architecture. In his famous work on 
painting, entitled De Pictura (c. 1435), he writes:
Often I was both astonished and pained, because so 
many outstanding and divine arts and sciences, which 
— as their works and traditions show — were numer­
ous in those so powerful times of the ancients, to­
day have disappeared and are almost completely lost: 
painters, sculptors, architects, musicians, surveyors, 
rhetoricians, soothsayers and similar very noble and 
admirable educated people can hardly be found to­
day, and there is little to praise them. So I found, all 
the more when I heard it confirmed by many, that na­
ture, the master of things, had long since become old 
and tired, and therefore was no longer able to form 
giants or great talents, of which she produced count­
less wonderful ones in her youthful and glorious times.
Alberti recognized and praised the important creative 
processes developing in Florence, which he considered as 
significant as those of the ancients. In fact, he even argued 
for the superiority of modern achievements, gained against 
far more difficult odds than in ancient times.

## S. 61

61
2. Brunelleschi and the 15th Century
Among the artists praised in Alberti’s treatise we find:
• 
Donatello and the images of the prophets he painted 
in the campanile of the Florence Cathedral. Alberti 
recognized the innovation in the anatomical rep­
resentation of the figures, the plasticity of the bodies, 
the garments, and the individual physiognomy of each 
figure; 
• 
Lorenzo Ghiberti and especially his three bronze 
doors, originally gilded, that he designed for the 
Baptistery of the Florence Cathedral; 
• 
Luca della Robbia, for the choir pulpit in the cathe­
dral of Florence, which included a sculpted group of 
extremely plastic figures;
• 
Masaccio, in the painting of the Cappella Brancacci, a 
group of dynamic figures located in a fully three-di­
mensional space.
Of Brunelleschi, Alberti wrote: 
Who could ... not praise the architect Pippo [Filippo 
Brunelleschi] at the sight of such a large construc­
tion [the dome of the Florence cathedral], which ris­
es to the heavens and is large enough to give shade 
to all Tuscan peoples, and which was erected without 
a framework of beams or a vast quantity of wood?
Alberti was the only humanist of his time who had 
enough knowledge to read and understand Vitruvius and Ro­
man antiquity. As a result, he was the first to present the 
orders in a systematic way. Moreover, Alberti looked at 
Roman ruins and noted what was missing from the Ten

## S. 62

62
Books, such as the Composite order, and therefore added it 
to the canon of classical orders. Similarly, Alberti excluded 
the Tuscan order because he could not identify it in Roman 
examples. Alberti also established compositional rules for 
the orders: the Doric at seven diameters in height, the Ionic 
at eight, and the Corinthian and the Composite at nine. He 
standardized the proportionality of the orders.
However, Alberti never applied these as strict principles 
to his own architecture. In the first book of his De Re Aedifi­
catoria (On Building, c. 1450) he explicitly demystified the 
canon of orders as constituting not a principle to follow but 
rather something that provides inspiration. Alberti also iden­
tified a number of classical capitals that are not related to 
any of the canonic types and which were not documented in 
classical texts like Vitruvius or Pliny, called ‘capitula mixta 
lineamentis’ (mixed capitals). As a result, Alberti also 
provided his research strategy into the composition of the 
orders with a historical grounding.
Francesco di Giorgio Martini
Francesco di Giorgio Martini (1439–1501) was trained as 
a painter and sculptor and worked primarily on military 
buildings. In 1478 he was commissioned by Federico da 
Montefeltro, lord of Urbino, with the completion of the 
Palazzo Ducale. At this time, Francesco di Giorgio also 
began writing an architectural treatise, of which two versions 
exist, one from around 1480 and a later edit from around 
1490.

## S. 63

63
2. Brunelleschi and the 15th Century
In the meantime, Francesco di Giorgio also worked on a 
translation of Vitruvius’s Ten Books. No translation of 
Vitruvius existed at that time, so Francesco di Giorgio had 
to study Latin to understand and translate the Ten Books. 
In his version of Vitruvius’s treatise, he added a commentary 
and a set of his own drawings as a sample of his inventions, 
because his intent was to stimulate architectural ideas 
rather than establish a canon of decisive rules. Francesco di 
Giorgio’s approach also demonstrates that at the end of the 
15th century there was still no need to establish canonic 
rules, as would be the case during the 16th century. Instead, 
one could play with Vitruvian norms through their own 
creative power, adopting the text and Roman ruins as inspi­
ration.
Indeed, Francesco di Giorgio produced a very peculiar 
categorization of the orders. Similar to Alberti, he defined 
the Doric as six diameters high, the Ionic as eight, and the 
Corinthian as nine. However, the distinction between the 
orders was not dependent on decoration but entirely on 
proportion. Capitals were themselves divided into units and 
followed the same rules as the columns (for example, the 
Doric capital occupies one third of the column diameter). 
According to Francesco di Giorgio, therefore, the capitals 
themselves could be the object of creative reinterpretation, 
as long as they began with the appropriate proportions. The 
Corinthian capital, for instance, was identified by its height, 
corresponding to a full column diameter, rather than by its 
acanthus decoration.
It is important to note that this sort of 15th-century 
interpretation of the orders, which deviates from a systemat­

## S. 64

64
ic approach, is by no means simply the result of a misunder­
standing. On the contrary, it is a genuinely intelligent way of 
dealing with the Vitruvian text. With this system, Francesco 
di Giorgio achieved a number of things:
1. His column is based on a truthful translation of 
Vitruvius (although it may not consider the text as a 
whole);
2. It allows the actual variety of Roman architecture to 
be taken into account; 
3. This system allows both invention and clear classifi­
cation. The architect has the freedom to create anew.
Donato Bramante and the High Renaissance
The end of the Medici dominance in 1494 was accompa­
nied by a shift in the artistic epicenter of Italian peninsula 
from Florence to Rome. The chief patron of the arts then 
became the papacy, already a strong political actor. 
The most famous example of this relocation is Donato 
Bramante (1444–1515). Originally from Urbino, Bramante 
worked in the area as a painter until he had to move to 
Milan in 1476, to the court of Ludovico Sforza. In 1499 he 
was expelled from Milan by the French troops. He moved to 
Rome, where he immediately received prominent commissions 
from members of the curia, mainly cardinals. In 1504 Pope 
Julius II appointed him as chief architect for the new St. 
Peter’s church.

## S. 65

65
2. Brunelleschi and the 15th Century
With this move from Milan to Rome, Bramante also 
changed his design approach. The rich decorative apparatus 
adopted in his last Milanese project, Santa Maria delle 
Grazie, was dramatically opposed to the more refined and 
intellectual approach of his first Roman work, the cloister of 
Santa Maria della Pace. The alternation of pilasters and 
columns in the upper and lower floors manifests the entan­
glement of two different forces. It is not only about purifying 
elements and surfaces, but the design also involves the 
reasoned superposition of the orders, an operation clearly 
inspired by the façades of Colosseum and of the Theater of 
Marcellus.
Bramante’s attitude towards antiquity evolved into a 
constant and direct confrontation with the ruins of Roman 
architecture. For example, in St. Peter’s church, Bramante 
reproduced, though on a bigger scale, the same Corinthian 
capitals as in the portico of the Pantheon. The practice of 
copying and of reproducing models from antiquity was per­
fected only through attentive replication, as exemplified by 
Bramante’s work.
As the work of Bramante demonstrates, the High Renais­
sance was dominated by a canonization of the classical 
vocabulary and its rigorous study and use, mostly through 
projects commissioned by the papacy. This stage ended in 
1527 with the sack of Rome. The Pope had to flee the city 
and artists followed, taking with them the knowledge of clas­
sical models acquired in Rome. As a result of this migration, 
treatises became very important media for spreading classi­
cal knowledge, in an attempt to systematize canons and 
obtain universally valid rules. Bramante’s influence in turn

## S. 66

66
shaped what is known today as the ‘circle of Bramante’, 
which would play a decisive role in both built architecture 
and 16th-century theory.

## S. 67

67
2. Brunelleschi and the 15th Century
CONCLUSION
Different moments of the Renaissance can be articulated 
through the architect’s changing relationship with antiquity 
and the strategies adopted to design ‘architettura all’antica’. 
From Brunelleschi’s mediated classicism of the mid-15th 
century, Alberti and Francesco di Giorgio attempted at 
canonizing the vocabulary of the classical orders through 
more rigorous interpretation of Vitruvius. Donato Bramante 
embodies the next phase of this process, characterized by a 
careful application of proportions to obtain a universal form 
of classicism.

## S. 71

71
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
INTRODUCTION 
The Ten Books on Architecture and its many subsequent 
interpretations have deeply shaped the history of architec­
ture. The Roman Empire and the power vacuum left after its 
decline in the 5th century profoundly shaped medieval Euro­
pean society. Classical knowledge survived in part by being 
transferred to Byzantium and the Arab world, though pri­
marily by being passed down through monasteries and 
religious institutions. Prior to the advent of the printing 
press, classical texts were copied out and commented on in 
Latin, generally by monks. On the one hand, copying led to 
the survival of these manuscripts over the centuries; on the 
other hand, manuscripts could easily become corrupted 
through the copying process and would therefore frequently 
change over time. The culture of the Renaissance sought a 
return to the original sources of classical knowledge. Moreo­
ver, through a renewed interest in the purer, cleaner versions 
of ancient texts, Christianity’s position as the successor of 
Roman culture was legitimized.
Classical principles and texts from the Roman period 
were ‘re-discovered’ and brought into wider circulation dur­
ing the Renaissance. To understand the history of architec­
ture and art of the 15th Century, it is important to recognize 
the way in which architects and theorists dealt with the past 
and how their understanding of working ‘all’antica’ (in the 
manner of the ancients) became a tool with which to explain 
and legitimatize their actions in the present.

## S. 72

72
The definition of the book and how it shaped the architec­
tural culture starting from the 15th century onwards.
The different meanings of translations, copies, or prints of 
Vitruvius’s treatise, and how they claim authority.
The place of Cesariano’s publication in a historical context.
The role of Raphael’s letter to Leo X in the quest for an 
architectural system and models.
The themes of Raphael’s letter (authorities of the past, 
architectural principles, history of architecture, society and 
architecture).
The international political context and how it was reflected 
in the city of Rome.
How to describe the architecture of the Tempietto and the 
design difficulties that the circular plan implied.
The references for the Tempietto, whether ruins, models or 
symbols.
How the references are embodied and played in the Tempi­
etto to create a foundation for Christianity.
The political program represented by the Tempietto.
How classical and symbolic languages were incorporated 
into a Christian vocabulary and into a religious program.
