3. The High Renaissance in Rome
Raphael and Baldassare Castiglione, ‘Letter to Pope Leo X,’ Mantova, Archivio Privato Castiglioni.

## S. 74

74
VITRUVIUS AFTER VITRUVIUS
The text by Vitruvius had been completely lost after the 
fall of the Roman Empire. What we read now is not his 
original treatise, but a copy, a reconstructed Latin text. Our 
Vitruvius, therefore, is a construction, much in the same way 
that architecture is a construction to Vitruvius. We are 
examining a sort of hypothesis of what Vitruvius may have 
said. Around 132 mediaeval manuscripts of Vitruvius’s text 
are available today, the oldest of which dates from the 9th 
century. Therefore, there is a gap of almost 10 centuries 
between the original and the earliest copy at our disposal. 
Over this period, many additions, mistakes, omissions, and 
misunderstandings may have occurred. Moreover, the knowl­
edge of Greek, from which Vitruvius drew much of his termi­
nology, was largely lost until the mid-15th century, so many 
mistakes have occurred in the transmission of Greek vocabu­
lary. Additionally, his illustrations were lost and never cop­
ied. As a consequence, comparing the surviving body of 
manuscripts shows important differences, but contains no 
illustrations that go back to the original. Each manuscript 
copy that has survived offers a different version of the same 
text, almost like a cloud surrounding the lost, original 
Vitruvius.
A fundamental moment in the process of the Renaissance 
‘rediscovery’ of Vitruvius occurred around 1414, when Poggio 
Bracciolini discovered a complete manuscript of the treatise 
in a monastery in St. Gall. This is a turning point, not 
because Vitruvius was forgotten—as we have seen, quite a 
few manuscripts of his work were circulating at the time—but 
because Bracciolini approached the book in line with the

## S. 75

75
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
emerging Renaissance ideals. Bracciolini was what we today 
would call a humanist, interested in all aspects of Antiquity. 
For a humanist, the culture of Antiquity contained models 
that could be reactivated in present philosophy, legal theory, 
literature, ethics, etc. As was done with other ancient Roman 
texts, Bracciolini began trying to retrieve the original form of 
Vitruvius’s text in order to rediscover its original meaning. 
Bracciolini’s ‘discovery’ was the first of a long process of 
reestablishment of the Ten Books, which picked up steam in 
the late 15th century and continues to this day, though no 
longer at the initiative of architects and their patrons, but 
by philologists and classicists.
An important catalyst was the emergence of printing with 
movable type (mid-15th c.); the first printed edition of Vitru­
vius dates from 1485–86. A key moment in the publication 
history of Vitruvius was the edition in 1511 by Fra Giocondo, 
whose reconstruction combined a large number of manu­
scripts. His work was so thorough that the modern English 
translation we use as our main source for this course uses it 
as a base. Fra Giocondo also was the first to add illustra­
tions in print, with the aim to clarify the text, as well as a 
glossary, which is a list of key terms and their definitions. 
From 1511, then, a very good Latin edition of Vitruvius was 
available in print. The next important step in the editorial 
history of Vitruvius was the publication of the first Italian 
translation by Cesare Cesariano, in 1521.

## S. 76

76
The Chosen of the World Configured by Cesariano
The translation of Vitruvius by Cesare Cesariano is 
situated at the beginning of an important period for the 
formation of architecture theory. In the book, Cesariano 
illustrates the importance of this historical moment with the 
image of an allegory, labelled ‘mundi electiva caesaris cae­
sariani configurata’ (The Chosen of the World Configured by 
Cesare Cesariano). It is a dense and complex allegorical 
illustration of the world, constructed by Cesariano himself. 
The image is divided into two parts. On the left, the poor, 
dominated by Envy and Ignorance, are divided by the wheel 
of fortune from the wealthy and powerful, depicted on the 
right. A figure symbolizing Audacity invites Cesariano, repre­
sented in the center, to join the side of the fortunate. Cesari­
ano depicts himself as both an architect, with compass and 
ruler, and a learned man with his laurel crown, an attribute 
of the Greek god, Apollo. The suggestion is that through ed­
ucation and learning he was able to leave poverty and igno­
rance behind him.
The allegorical image can be properly understood only in 
within its original context. Placed at the opening of Book VI 
of the Ten Books, in which Vitruvius presents his ‘reasons 
for the use and symmetries of private buildings’ (‘Di Lucio 
Vitruvio Pollione incomenza il libro sexto in lo quale si ratio 
cina de la utilitate et symmetrie de li privati aedificii’), the 
image mirrors the story originally narrated by Vitruvius 
himself. The main event that Vitruvius writes about, and 
which Cesariano comments in his translation, is the ship­
wreck of the Socratic philosopher Aristippus who, cast 
ashore on the coast of Rhodes, escapes his bad fortune

## S. 77

77
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
thanks to his education. By retrieving lost knowledge and 
making it available again ‘for the great advantage and 
necessity of the world’, Cesariano was able to accrue author­
ity of his own. His translation and commentary help to 
formulate a new social role—that of the learned architect. 
Cesariano’s Book, Between the Middle Ages and Modern 
Times
Cesariano used the most modern means available to him, 
namely the illustrated, printed book, to express his erudition 
and to publicly process Vitruvian knowledge. The completion 
of Cesariano’s work came at a time of great change for the 
medium of the book. Although Cesariano’s pages are print­
ed, the page composition still retains a medieval layout. 
Vitruvius’s text is placed in the middle of the page, sur­
rounded by Cesariano’s commentary that is intended to 
explain and clarify ambiguities in the original text. Cesari­
ano also maintains the system, adopted in medieval manu­
scripts, by which illustrations were used as a tool to clarify 
the text. As the printed book became a normalized product 
in Europe, however, its form also changed quite rapidly (see 
later with Palladio).
From the Renaissance onwards, the notion that lost 
knowledge was there to be rediscovered ignited a highly 
productive engagement with classical texts. Vitruvius’s Ten 
Books took on the role of the supreme authority by which to 
make the glories of the ‘architettura all’antica’ available 
again. As with Cesariano’s book, during the first decades of 
the 16th century deliberate attempts were made to recon­

## S. 78

78
struct the Vitruvian theory of architecture and to apply it in 
practice. The basic component of this theory was the system 
of the orders, laid out in a very important document, the 
letter of Raphael to Pope Leo X. In practice, we can observe 
this theory applied in the Tempietto of St. Pietro in Monto­
rio by Donato Bramante.
LETTER BY RAPHAEL TO LEO X
The letter, written around 1519, is by Raphael, who was 
known primarily as a painter but also as an architect, with 
the help of Baldassare Castiglione and Angelo Colocci. The 
letter is written as an answer to a request from Pope Leo X 
to record the architecture of ancient Rome. This architec­
ture, according to Raphael, has a value of its own, but it 
can also serve as an example for the architecture of the 
present; Leo X is portrayed as a new Augustus, under whose 
reign architecture and the arts will flourish. 
The project Raphael laid out in the letter consisted of 
building an extensive corpus of measured drawings of the 
main monuments of ancient Rome. Raphael casts this pro­
ject as a reconstruction, on paper. The largest part of the 
letter is devoted to the procedure of measuring and drawing 
the ruins of Rome. Raphael describes his measuring instru­
ments in detail and clarifies how he would divide the territo­
ry of Rome into sectors using a compass. This would then 
allow him to identify in orderly fashion the buildings he 
would measure and draw.

## S. 79

79
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
In the first part of the letter (cf. slides), Raphael pays 
much attention to the question of which buildings he should 
draw and how he would identify them. This was a complex 
matter. Identifying ancient monuments was difficult, as 
16th-century buildings in Rome were often built on top of 
ancient structures, with stones and ruins scattered about, 
and unfinished structures everywhere. Within this messy 
urban landscape, Raphael lays out his mission of recogniz­
ing ancient buildings. The only way to accomplish this task, 
he says, is by examining the style of these buildings, a style 
that Raphael says is perfect, of great craftsmanship, excel­
lent proportions, and magnificent size. As such, he clearly 
distinguishes these buildings from the architecture dating to 
after the fall of the Roman Empire, when all knowledge of 
the principles of architecture was lost. Ancient buildings are 
also different from the good architecture that has reemerged 
since, which is based on the same principles as the architec­
ture of the Romans but has not yet reached the same mag­
nificence. 
By measuring ancient buildings, Raphael states, he will 
be able to retrieve the universal principles by which they were 
built, which can then be applied to contemporary architec­
ture. The drawing therefore becomes an analytical tool, a 
means of both registering existent buildings and reconstruct­
ing what was lost on the basis of the remains that are 
available. Crucially, whereas Raphael mentions Vitruvius sev­
eral times, his main source to re-establish the principles of 
architecture are the ruins of Rome.
This periodization also allows Raphael to distinguish 
‘ancient’ architecture from the architecture that was built ac­

## S. 80

80
cording to different principles: the Gothic. Even if Gothic 
architecture has a rationality of its own, he says, it is far 
inferior to Roman architecture in grace and strength. For 
Raphael and his contemporaries, Gothic architecture is 
‘modern’, i.e. not connected to the tradition of the ancients. 
We can represent Raphael’s argument as follows:
Q = quality
T = time
QM = maximum quality: Augustus and Roman Empire
QR = quality in the Renaissance: Leo X and Papacy
T
ANTIQUITY
MIDDLE-AGES
GOTHIC 
ARCHITECTURE
RENAISSANCE
Q
QM
QR

## S. 81

81
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
According to Raphael, the quality of the architecture of 
the Roman Empire is the best that we can aspire to (qm in 
the graph). With the fall of the empire, architecture fell as 
well. This is because, according to Raphael, the Romans 
went from being rulers to being slaves, and thus lost the 
knowledge to produce their architecture. This point associ­
ates the quality of architecture with the success and stability 
of a political system, an augment very close to Vitruvius. 
The Goths then introduced a new and lesser style, the Goth­
ic, to fill this vacuum, a process that lasted for centuries. 
Out of this dark chapter, architecture was then able to 
regain some of its ancient splendor (qr in the graph). Ac­
cording to Raphael, this was because of Leo X who, for 
Raphael, plays a role entirely comparable to Emperor Au­
gustus. 
Raphael’s letter is of particular interest because it relates 
architecture with history. In his attempt to describe a pro­
cess to retrieve the principles of architecture, Raphael also 
develops an idea of how architecture changes over time. The 
engine of this change is political, as it has to do with the 
flourishing and demise of the Roman Empire. Additionally, 
for Raphael there is a universal standard of good architec­
ture, which is ancient, and the only way that we can regain 
this standard is through the political circumstances at play, 
which also require the availability of architects capable to 
achieve this level and an understanding of the principles of 
architecture.
Another important point presented in the letter is its 
discussion of the orders. Raphael writes:

## S. 82

82
Of these ornaments, there is no need to say more than 
that they all derive from the five Orders used by the 
ancients—namely Doric, Ionic, Corinthian, Tuscan and 
Attic. Of all these, the Doric is the oldest and it was 
invented by Dorus, king of the Achaeans, when he was 
building a temple to Juno in Argos. Later, when build­
ing a temple to Apollo in Ionia, he set the measure­
ments of the Doric columns to match the proportions of 
a man, such that they produced symmetry [symmetria], 
firmness and beautiful dimensions, without any other 
ornaments. But in the temple of Diana they changed 
the form, ordering [ordinando] the columns with the 
measurements and proportions of a woman, with many 
ornaments in the capitals, bases and over the entire 
shaft or scape of the column, and they composed it 
in imitation of the build of a woman. This they called 
Ionic. But those that are called Corinthian are more 
slender and more delicate, made in imitation of the 
gracefulness and slenderness of a virgin. The inventor 
of these was Callimachus of Corinth, hence they are 
called Corinthian. On the origin and form of this, Vit­
ruvius writes at great length, and we refer all those who 
want to know more to him. As and when necessary, we 
shall describe the arrangements [ordini] for all of them, 
taking as a presupposition the things in Vitruvius.’
This is one of the earliest statements that explicitly lays 
down a system of the five orders, which in turn express the 
intrinsic principles of architecture. In other words, in Rapha­
el’s letter the orders become the central feature of the theory 
of architecture, much more than in Vitruvius. Raphael, of 
course, refers to Vitruvius, who was the first to explain the 
origins of Doric, Ionic and Corinthian. To these, Raphael 
adds the Attic and the Tuscan orders. In establishing a 
system of architecture, Raphael attaches it to authorities.

## S. 83

83
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
Again, in addition to Vitruvius, Raphael mentions the ruins 
of Rome as fundamental sources of truth. Raphael also 
mentions an architect who, according him, embodies the 
best qualities of contemporary architecture: Donato Bra­
mante.
THE TEMPIETTO BY BRAMANTE 
Raphael makes explicit reference to Bramante in this 
passage of his letter: 
[E]ven though these days architecture may be very 
clever and very closely based on the style of the an­
cients, as can be seen in the many beautiful works 
by Bramante, nevertheless the ornamentation is 
not done using raw materials of similar expense to 
those used by the ancients, who, it seems, realized 
what they envisaged with endless amounts of mon­
ey and whose will alone surmounted every difficulty.
According to Raphael, Bramante is a great architect but 
still cannot match the greatness of ancient architects. We 
have learned that Bramante attempted to develop a system­
atic theory of architecture, creating a hierarchy, for example, 
in Santa Maria della Pace that would regulate the applica­
tion of ornament (see chapter 2). In 1505 Bramante was 
also commissioned to build the extension to the papal 
residence, the Vatican Palace. Among other interventions, 
Bramante added a spiral ramp showcasing a catalogue of 
Tuscan, Doric, Ionic, and Corinthian columns. While in the 
cloister of Santa Maria della Pace Bramante adopted a flat 
transposition of the orders through the theater motif, in the

## S. 84

84
Vatican Palace he produced a true model by the superposi­
tion of freestanding columns.
The building that most of all embodies this quest for 
true, absolute principles of ancient and lost Vitruvian archi­
tecture is the Tempietto of St. Pietro in Montorio, built in 
the first decades of the 1500s. Not only is the Tempietto a 
masterpiece in itself, but it served as a model and a proto­
type for countless other buildings, from St. Paul’s Cathedral 
in London to the ETH Hauptgebäude in Zurich. The Tempi­
etto, modelled as a peripteral temple, has a symmetrical 
circular plan, making use of simple geometrical figures. The 
freestanding building is placed on a three-stepped base that 
distributes the ambulatory surrounding the cella. The central 
body is a cylinder surrounded by sixteen Doric columns with 
a dome on top. According to Vitruvius’s first book on tem­
ples, sixteen is the perfect number, being the combination of 
ten, expressing the ideal of the human body, and six, which 
is perfectly fractionable. The columns are then topped with 
forty-eight metopes displaying twelve liturgical objects. The 
Doric order is used very elaborately for the entablature and 
architrave and is crowned with a balustrade. On the upper 
level, the drum is articulated and marked by large windows 
and concluded with the dome and a prominent acroterion. 
The proportions are very simply deducible. Emphasis is put 
on the drum, dome, and other horizontal visual indicators, 
which aids a precise reading of the volumes. 
The interior is itself cylindrical, with an intricate geomet­
rical pattern on the floor, known as ‘cosmatesque’. The 
vertical composition of the elements recalls the tripartition 
of the exterior, namely the pilaster-entablature, the drum

## S. 85

85
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
and the dome, with the additional introduction of a crypt 
underneath, where the martyrdom of St. Peter was supposed 
to have taken place. This is organized around an altar with 
wall decorations, intentionally recalling the space of a grotto 
or a catacomb. The crypt was originally accessible down a 
straight staircase from the inside. 
The Tempietto underwent many changes, including a very 
important restoration in 1628 under the orders of Pope 
Urban VIII, which included the installation of a new stair­
case. The current altar position also does not follow Bra­
mante’s original plans. The Tempietto is generally highly 
regarded for its sophistication as well as for the multitude of 
interpretative layers coexisting in a small building, including 
the use of the Doric order, the significance of its proportion­
al and decorative systems, and the political and religious 
meanings behind the highly refined uses of the classical 
language.
The Doric Order
Bramante’s Tempietto presented a sophisticated architec­
tonic problem: how can the Doric order be articulated in a 
concentric disposition and with such a specific system of 
ratios without losing proportional coherence? This problem 
is one of proportions. In plan, the intercolumniation changes 
dramatically along the radius while the elevation is un­
touched. For the exterior, this is translated into a reduced 
intercolumniation for the ambulatory. For the interior, how­
ever, this system requires a completely different approach. 
Here the sixteen columns of the external peripteral system

## S. 86

86
are turned into four pairs of pilasters. The result is a dimi­
nution in the ambulatory but a radically different system in 
the interior, which still echoes the exterior, with four pairs of 
pilasters alternating with wider areas of five modules. How­
ever, this doesn’t solve the problem of the frames, which 
need to be fitted into very narrow confines. This is solved by 
either squeezing them or superimposing the frames over the 
pilasters. 
The proportional system in the narrower circumference of 
the interior is maintained by lifting it above a pedestal, 
where it matches up with drum and dome. This is a system 
of layered elements and combined volumes. Bramante sys­
tematically put the pilasters on the same axes as the col­
umns and marginally narrowed them down to maintain 
space; the niches become squeezed into pilasters. Even in 
the exterior, Bramante emphasizes the differences between 
doors, niches, and windows by pressing the niches and the 
windows between pilasters but allowing the doors to overlap 
the pilasters, something that is tectonically illogical. It’s 
almost as if the door ‘grows out’ from the inside and moves 
over the pilasters. Inside, this system is no longer necessary, 
because the wider space allows for more monumental niches 
corresponding to the doors.
In plan, this system provides the building with a very 
dynamic, ‘undulating’ wall. In section, Bramante also invent­
ed something extraordinary to maintain coherence in the 
Doric order. The very same system of column-entablature is 
adopted in the interior, but it is ‘raised’ on top of the pedes­
tal, giving a slenderness to the Doric pilasters. This also 
allows the interior cornice to be significantly higher than the

## S. 87

87
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
exterior cornice, producing the effect of a ‘floating’ dome.
Through a close reading of the Tempietto, the astonish­
ing sophistication of Bramante’s architecture becomes clear. 
The meaning of this precision is, again, embedded in a 
search for the ‘right’ expression and values. It is about 
developing and mastering a sophisticated architectural 
language down to the finest details. Through a concentric 
plan, Bramante was proposing a difficult design problem for 
himself, as a way to demonstrate his ability to master it and 
solve it—finding smart solution while still remaining faithful 
to the system.
References
The design approach of the time was dominated by a 
logic of imitation, as the best way to capture and reactivate 
the lost architectural principles of Antiquity. Bramante’s 
primary reference was Vitruvius, who in Book IV of De archi­
tectura made the peripteral round temple a central theme. 
However, aside from discussing the Doric order and the 
particular position of the triglyph, Vitruvius did not explain 
how to use the Doric order in a circular temple. 
As Raphael had argued in his letter to Leo X, ruins 
could be a substantial source of authority. In Bramante’s 
time, the two most famous existing examples of a round 
peripteral temple in the vicinity of Rome were the Temple of 
the Sibyl in Tivoli and the Temple of Hercules. Some draw­
ings were also circulating at the end of 15th century and the 
beginning of the 16th century, by Francesco di Giorgio and

## S. 88

88
his circle among others, that offered detailed reconstructions 
of peripteral temples. The Teatro Marittimo and fragments 
of Hadrian’s Villa may also have been references for the pro­
ject. Still, only very few examples of Doric buildings could be 
seen in Rome, since most surviving buildings were in the 
Corinthian or Composite orders. As a result, complete exam­
ples of the Doric order, and especially of circular Doric 
temples, were not available. 
In this context, without the aid of complete examples, 
Bramante reinvented the Doric order in a cleaner and per­
fected way by applying it to the circle, the purest of forms. 
From fragmentary indications, Bramante attempted to go 
beyond the traces of a lost model, and refound the Doric 
order as a coherent and legitimate architectural language.
Religious Tensions
During the early 16th century, Pope Julius II was trying to 
rebuild Rome as a universal capital of Christianity. Christi­
anity had developed progressively from sometime after the 
birth of Christ until 313, when it was officially recognized 
under the Emperor Constantine I. By the 16th century, it had 
become the only official religion of Europe. The political 
ideal of a new Christian empire imagined by Julius II en­
tailed a process of linguistic unification, which included 
architecture as much as the classical languages. In many 
respects, the Tempietto became the very expression of these 
linguistic rules. It embodies the attempt at a unification 
between religious and classical identities. As such, it also 
represents a conflict in its simultaneous expression of social

## S. 89

89
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
and political ideologies.
The location of the Tempietto is itself not free from 
meaning and contrasts. Not too far from the Vatican, the 
building is located in the courtyard of a Franciscan monas­
tery dedicated to St. Peter. The Tempietto was commis­
sioned as a votive church by the Spanish royal family, with 
the patronage of the Spanish king, and today still houses 
the Spanish Academy. Pope Sixtus IV gave the monastery to 
the visionary Franciscan reformer, Amadeo de Meneses da 
Silva (1420–82), who was asked for a spiritual intercession 
by the royal Spanish family in order to ensure an heir to the 
throne. This particular location was traditionally associated 
with the crucifixion of Peter, usually considered one of the 
most important saints of Christianity and the gatekeeper of 
heaven. St. Peter was especially important in connection to 
the city of Rome as the center of Christianity.
The reasons behind this votive gift from Ferdinand of 
Aragon, not yet king, and Isabella of Castile for an heir was 
connected to the important role that Spain began to play in 
the political landscape of Europe. Spain, unified for the first 
time through the union of Aragon and Castile, was undergo­
ing great expansion and was at the threshold of its colonial 
conquests. Along with France, it was the strongest military 
power of 15th-century Europe. This geopolitical condition was 
visible in Rome in built form: the Vatican as the expression 
of the papal power, the Trinità dei Monti as an expression 
of the French power, and the Tempietto, built by the Span­
ish. The three buildings formed a triangle on the map of the 
city, staking out their territorial and political claims on 
Rome.

## S. 90

90
It is through these political and material tensions that 
the different meanings of the Tempietto can be read. On the 
one hand, San Pietro in Montorio, the monastery in whose 
courtyard the Tempietto stands, can be considered the 
primary project by Pope Julius II by which to ‘center’ Chris­
tianity at a moment of great global spread. On the other 
hand, it is important to take into consideration the relation­
ship of Bramante with Spanish patronage in Rome, whose 
buildings, specifically at the site of St Peter’s crucifixion, 
compete with the popes’ claim to primacy within Christiani­
ty.
Form as Political Re-Foundation 
The re-foundational aim of the project is inscribed in the 
relationship of Bramante with Julius II. The Tempietto 
embodies the attempt to rebuild Rome by purifying the 
language of the ancients and by re-activating it in order to 
shape the capital for a new Christian empire. The choice of 
the Doric order and of the circular plan therefore evidences a 
set of symbolical meanings that the Tempietto is meant to 
convey.
The most elementary idea associated with the round 
shape is the perfection of the circle. This notion was also 
expressed in Raphael’s letter, where he suggests that 
everything in nature tends to the perfection of the circle. 
Similarly, in the De Re Aedificatoria, Alberti writes that the 
ancients shaped temples primarily as circles. The search for 
perfection is then related to the geometry of the cosmos. For

## S. 91

91
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
example, when Palladio discussed the Pantheon (long after 
Raphael), the temple for all gods built in the 2nd century 
AD, he points to its round plan and section. This in turn 
refers to the entirety of the universe—it is a representation of 
the world.
The circle is also particularly important for the way it is 
related to the Doric order. Importantly, Vitruvius introduces 
the orders when presenting the principles of architecture and 
how they connect to the world. According to Vitruvius, the 
Doric order has no ornament because it is an expression of 
force and power. In the Tempietto this association is best 
expressed by the figure of Saint Peter. The ‘solidity’ of Peter 
is associated with the way he was represented in the Chris­
tian tradition. Peter was considered the first pope, the figure 
who institutionalized the church. As the first vicar of Christ, 
Peter embodies the ‘foundational stone’—the stone on which 
the Church is built (the Italian name Pietro, Peter, comes 
from ‘pietra’, stone). This association with Peter’s strength 
then becomes the means by which the Tempietto is anchored 
to the specific location where he was supposedly martyred. 
The circularity of St. Pietro in Montorio can be understood 
as the materialization of a building erected ‘around’ a place 
of crucifixion. 
The question of where Peter died was actually a highly 
debated issue in the 15th century. The Basilica of San Pietro 
in the Vatican was claimed to be the spot of Peter’s martyr­
dom, as expressed by the bronze door designed by Filarete, 
and compared to San Pietro in Montorio. The very similar 
iconography of St. Peter’s crucifixion in both the Vatican 
and Montorio are further evidence of conflict. Curiously, the

## S. 92

92
composition of St. Peter’s crucifixion recalls the Vitruvian 
man, inscribed in the square and the circle. 
In fact, the entire iconography of St. Peter is related to 
the origin myth of the western Church. The figure of St. 
Peter is used to convey the principles of Vitruvian architec­
ture, and this perfect form is adopted to indicate and em­
body the place of the saint’s martyrdom as the founding 
moment of the church. In turn, the spaces and built forms 
adopted to represent St. Peter’s foundational martyrdom in 
Montorio embody the perfect shape of the world and cos­
mos. This message is conveyed obsessively, down to the 
smallest details, for example in the ‘cosmatesque’ floor.
Tectonic Meanings 
In the Tempietto, further analogies and references be­
tween the Vitruvian, classical tradition and the representa­
tion of Christian values can be observed in the symbolic 
deployment of architectural elements and motifs.
First, a common thread between classical and Christian 
significance was established in the adoption of metaphors 
relating to human proportions. The association of a building 
with the body, a well-known trope of Vitruvian scholarship, 
is also part of the Catholic tradition. For example, in the 
book Rationale Divinorum Officiorum, a treatise about the 
rules of mass and the meanings of liturgy written around 
1270, the church is represented in the shape of a perfect 
human body. The element of the column is also traditionally 
adopted in connection to the bodies of the Apostles, by

## S. 93

93
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
allegorically associating the structural role that a column 
plays in a building with the supportive role the Apostles play 
in the Catholic Church.
In the Tempietto, such proximity between classical and 
Christian themes is visible not only in the well-balanced 
proportions between each part of the building, echoing a 
relationship with the human body, but also through numeric 
associations. For instance, the sixteen columns of the Tem­
pietto, already a perfect Vitruvian number, probably also 
referred to a Christian tradition defined by Sant’Agostino, 
who, in his writings, adopted the number six as a symbol of 
perfection that is connected to God’s creation of the earth.
Additionally, the typology of the Tempietto can also be 
seen as an expression both of a classical and a Christian 
model. The prototype for the Tempietto was probably the 
Roman mausoleum, which establishes a direct contact with 
Antiquity, but its early Christian precedent was also the 
martyrium. The building hence embodies the reinvented com­
bination of both Christian and classical archetypes.
Bramante’s newly established language of classical archi­
tecture is therefore adopted to express a Christian message 
through a variety of strategies, such as numeric and symbol­
ic analogies, religious similarities between pagan gods and 
Christian saints, or historical associations. For example, he 
makes a reference to a building commissioned by a Roman 
emperor who played an important role in establishing Chris­
tianity. As Christian tradition itself was very much shaped 
by classical culture, these associations are often openly 
visible, such as in the symbolism of geometrical figures and

## S. 94

94
numbers and in the relationship between the human body, 
the cosmos, and divine creation. 
By exploiting the common ground between classical and 
Christian heritage, architecture then becomes a powerful 
statement about the present, and it expresses its signifi­
cance through multiple layers of meaning. The importance of 
the Tempietto should then be understood as a result of 
Bramante’s sophistication and erudition in merging a net­
work of religious motifs and classical themes and forms, in 
order to produce a universal linguistic model. This is how 
the building immediately became an architectural reference, 
as was intended.

## S. 95

95
3. The High Renaissance in Rome
CONCLUSION
With the rediscovery of antique architecture and the 
development of the High Renaissance in Rome, the classical 
vocabulary undergoes successive processes of interpretation 
and is imbued with new significance and meanings. These 
relate to the political and religious values of 16th century 
Italy, turning the ‘architettura all’antica’ into a system of 
wider cultural values, which involves elements of reinvention 
beyond mere copying.

## S. 99

99
INTRODUCTION 
Already from the early 16th century, the linguistic regis­
ters of the Italian Renaissance and the and the paradigms of 
‘architettura all’antica’ started moving beyond the borders of 
the Italian peninsula and especially towards northern Eu­
rope. This dissemination was possible primarily through spe­
cific types of architectural books, the so-called ‘Säulenbüch­
er’ (‘column-books’). These were intended to be easily 
accessible compendiums on composition by a systematized 
‘synopsis’ of the architectural orders. The ‘säulenbücher’ 
generated a vast body of knowledge on antique architecture, 
which originated and Italy but underwent a process of reor­
ganization and, to some extent, simplification and even 
alteration of the original principles.
4. Spreading the Renaissance: the ‘Säulenbücher’

## S. 100

100
The definition of ‘Säulenbücher’. 
The difference between Serlio’s and Vignola’s methods for 
column design.
How architectural knowledge circulated to northern Euro­
pe.
 
How paper architecture was adopted and used in design.
WHAT TO KNOW

## S. 101

101
4. Spreading the Renaissance: the ‘Säulenbücher’
Hans Vredeman de Vries, Architectura, Antwerpen 1577.

## S. 102

102
VINCENZO SCAMOZZI: L’IDEA DELL’ARCHITETTURA 
UNIVERSALE
One of the latest examples of these synopses is L'idea 
della Architettura Universale (Venice 1615). Here the author 
Vincenzo Scamozzi presented a rational taxonomy of the five 
orders—what he called ‘thoscano’, ‘dorico’, ‘ionico’, ‘corinto’, 
‘romano’. These were organized by proportion, dimension 
and ornament, proposing therefore a systematic hierarchy. In 
order to justify his argument, Scamozzi refers in his treatise 
to a natural law, according to which architecture is consti­
tuted in the five orders. 
‘Nature itself, from the beginning of the world until 
our time, has always possessed the same order [...] and 
so it will always be. As far as the people are con­
cerned, during the first period after the floor Italy 
was satisfied with just one order - Tuscan. Then the 
Greeks kept to the Doric order for a while, until they 
found the Ionian and the Corinthian; [...] then final­
ly the Romans (who added the composite order) ar­
rived at these five orders, which they never exceeded.’
While Scamozzi recognized five orders in total, Cesari­
ano’s first Italian translation of Vitruvius presented six (two 
version of Doric, Ionic, Corinthian, Attic and Tuscan). Ac­
cording to Cesariano, every capital can work with each 
column shaft. Instead, Scamozzi introduced a more rigid 
system, where the relationship between capitals and shafts 
are is determined by universal, natural principles. This 
already suggests an interpretative argument, where author 
established their methodology to organize and justify the 
orders.

## S. 103

103
SEBASTIANO SERLIO: I SETTE LIBRI
Sebastiano Serlio was a Bolognese painter who studied in 
Rome and is considered the first to write an extensive archi­
tectural treatise which spread all over Europe by means of 
its printed copies. Serlio was a scholar of Baldassare Peruz­
zi, who was in turn Bramante’s adept. Between 1527 and 
1541 Serlio was in Venice, but he then moved to France, at 
the court of Francis I, where he died. 
Serlio took an important step in the systematization of 
the canon of the orders. Throughout his lifetime, he at­
tempted to carry out the project of a comprehensive archi­
tectural treatise in seven books (Sette Libri). This project 
did not come to realization, and his books were published 
independently, some posthumously and did not fully reflect 
the author’s original vision. Serlio’s books were produced as 
follows:
Book 1: geometry 
Book 2: perspective 
Book 3: antiquity
Book 4: orders of columns
Book 5: churches
Book 6: residential building (published posthumously)
Book 7: constructions on the country and city of the 
castle (published posthumously)
Book 8: fortifications (published posthumously)
Book 9: an Extraordinario Libro, with templates for 
portals
The publication history of Serlio’s books was also com­
plicated. As follows, the chronology of publication:
4. Spreading the Renaissance: the ‘Säulenbücher’

## S. 104

104
Book 4: Venice 1537
Book 3: Venice 1540
Book 1: Paris 1545
Book 2: Paris 1545
Book 5: Paris 1547
Book 9 (Extraordinario Libro): Lyon 1551
Book 7: Frankfurt 1575
Book 6 and 8: only preserved in manuscript form 
The first to be published in 1537 was the fourth book, 
entitled Regole Generali di Architettura, Sopra le Cinque 
Maniere de gli Edifici (‘the general rules of architecture 
about the five ways of making buildings or the five ways of 
designing buildings’). Three years later, the third book 
followed, dedicated to the antiquities of Rome. Here Serlio 
adopted a similar methodological strategy as Raphael in his 
Letter to Leo X: proposing to first carefully analyze the 
ruins of Rome and subsequently to deduce and apply general 
rules of antiquity. The first and second books, appearing in 
1545, dealt with geometry and perspective, the chief sciences 
that architects need to master to be able to develop a pro­
ject. The fifth book was devoted to churches and temples. 
To these, in 1551, Serlio added an additional book on door­
ways. Later he produced a sixth book on residences, a 
seventh on public buildings, and an eighth on fortifications. 
A later collection, entitled Tutte l'opere d'architettura di S. S. 
Bolognese, was also published in 1584. 
In this fourth book, for the first time the five orders were 
defined and brought into a clear hierarchical system of 
proportions. Each of its chapters is dedicated to one of the 
five orders: Tuscan, Doric, Ionic Corinthian, Composite. 
Each order is then developed in detail in its different parts,

## S. 105

105
with examples of connections and applications. These pro­
portions were established as a mathematical system of 
relationships between their parts and based on the diameter 
of the column, normalizing and systematizing their design 
beyond scale and notation (Tuscan: 1:6; Doric: 1:7; Ionic: 
1:8; Corinthian: 1:9; Composite: 1:10). In Serlio’s books, the 
entire system of architectural composition was divided into 
categories and orders. These were then associated to their 
appropriate functions, according to the Vitruvian idea of 
decorum. The applicability and the use of the orders there­
fore became a central topic for Serlio. He wrote extensively 
about what an architect needs to know in order to design 
and how to correctly use his system of proportions. 
In this articulation of the orders, there were however two 
inherent problems. Firstly, with the exception of the shaft of 
the column, the proportional system that Serlio suggested 
was very complicated. Secondly, the calculations that Serlio 
proposed did not generate entire numbers, which at the time 
was problematic. In this sense, another treatise performed 
better.
GIACOMO BAROZZI DA VIGNOLA: REGOLA DELLI 
CINQUE ORDINI D'ARCHITETTURA
Originally from Bologna, Vignola first trained as a paint­
er and later as an architect in Rome. Around 1543 Vignola 
was in Rome again, when he became the leading architect 
after Michelangelo's death in 1564. The Regola delli Cinque 
Ordini d'Architettura was published in Rome in 1562. Con­
trarily to Serlio, Vignola’s treatise has only one page of writ­
4. Spreading the Renaissance: the ‘Säulenbücher’

## S. 106

106
ten introduction and is otherwise simply based on 32 illus­
trated plates. The images then, rather than the text, are the 
main object and explanatory instrument of the book.
In the short introduction to the book, Vignola writes 
about the unavailability of direct sources of antique architec­
ture, and how ruins cannot be clear references to design. As 
a reaction to this, he seeks a way to understand and stand­
ardize good architecture. For every order, Vignola dedicated 
the same five or six plates: first the column with the entab­
lature; that the colonnade; then the theater motif (one with 
base and one without); then details of the base, capitals 
etc… and one variation of the system. Instead of the diame­
ter proposed by Serlio, Vignola divided the entire order into 
a ‘modules’. In doing so, adjustments can be made to the 
column in a simple and more straightforward manner, with­
out recurring to calculations.
Vignola does not detail the characters and genders of the 
columns, which were still important for Serlio (following 
Vitruvius). Instead, Vignola reduces the material of the 
orders to a simple and ‘dry’ system of proportions, therefore 
operating a process of isolation of the orders. Columns are 
separated from the context of the building and thus rede­
fined as a freely available vocabulary that can be combined 
as desired. The Regola turned the orders into a vocabulary 
for architecture, a basic ‘grammar’ of columns. Vignola 
produced a practical system to build, where he does not 
speak about buildings, only about orders. He put forward 
the notion of synopsis as a hierarchized system which works 
vertically (throughout the composition) and not just hori­
zontally.

## S. 107

107
Because of its simple method and digestible format, the 
book had an enormous success. It was reprinted in over 500 
editions in several languages and was used as an architec­
tural ‘textbook’ until the mid-19th century. Because of its 
international success, subsequent editions of the Regola also 
underwent processes of alteration and local adaptation. For 
instance, additional examples and plates were added to the 
original collection of columns. Palazzo Farnese in Caprarola 
by Vignola himself was added to the 1559 edition, while 
Michelangelo’s Porta Pia was added to the 1629 edition. In 
later edition we also find examples of Baroque buildings, as 
well as other prototypes close the culture where the book 
was reprinted.
DISSEMINATING THE ITALIAN RENAISSANCE 
With very few exceptions, there are almost no examples 
of antique architecture in northern Europe. This meant that 
there was no direct knowledge of antique architecture, and 
therefore the only means through which this knowledge could 
be passed on was with books and treatises. The reception of 
antique architecture in northern Europe was therefore possi­
ble by direct contact (i.e. architects travelling to/from Italy), 
but primarily thanks to the distribution of ‘säulenbücher’, 
like Vignola and Serlio. This meant that Italian Renaissance 
architecture was primarily understood as a manipulation of 
the orders. This evidently reflected only part of the story. As 
we have seen so far, in Renaissance Italy the orders were not 
conceived as simple decoration but were part of a complex 
composition. This process of dissemination of ‘all’antica’ 
architecture occurred in different ways:
4. Spreading the Renaissance: the ‘Säulenbücher’

## S. 108

108
• 
As a consequence of the Sack of Rome of 1527, when 
architects and artists fled the capital and took shelter 
in northern Italy and across the Alps;
• 
As a result of foreign architects visiting Italy (e.g. 
Hans Vredeman de Vries, see below);
• 
Through a process of copy, translation and reprinting 
of Italian architectural treatises, first Serlio and Vi­
gnola, then Scamozzi and Palladio;
• 
As a result of original architectural treatises and 
column-books produced in northern Europe, initially 
mainly based on Serlio;
• 
Through the role of international contracting authori­
ties and clients. For example, Serlio moved to France 
in 1541 (this also set an important problem about the 
accessibility of sources).
By considering some examples in norther Europe from 
the beginning of 16th century, it becomes quite evident that 
the spread and adoption of antique architecture in relation 
to the sources was quite different than in Italy, for example 
in the use of columns for Kandelabersäule or Balustersäule. 
This process of reuse and adaptation could have two sorts 
of consequences:
1. The architect takes on the examples from the ‘säulen­
bücher’ exactly how these are and applies them on an 
existing project. Such is the Rothenburg o.d.T., 
Rathaus (1572). In this case, the process of isolation 
operated by the treatise turns the columns into a 
piece of fashionable and superficial ornamentation, 
with no substantial relationship or consequence to the 
rest of the building;

## S. 109

109
2. Details and examples taken from the treatise are 
developed further, as in the case of the outer Castle 
gate in Tübingen (1606). In this case, the model of 
the Doric portal from Vignola is adopted for a royal 
palace and adapted to the local conditions. The 
portal of the castle became an articulated and 
over-decorated composition, showcasing a different 
language from the original antique examples. In this 
sense, the adoption of the orders from ‘säulenbücher’ 
was also an opportunity to invent new compositions.
Serlio’s Influence: Limiting and Liberating Effects
Serlio’s treatise disseminated the complex relationship 
between the five orders and rustication, and the vast combi­
nation of possibilities that this limited syntax carried within 
itself, with everlasting consequences. As already discussed, 
the treatise was driven by images and had a great success, 
bringing about different responses. On one hand, Serlio’s 
contribution was often understood as a supply of visual 
templates for the creative invention of architecture. On the 
other hand, these visualizations were also interpreted as nor­
malizing and therefore were considered limitations to archi­
tectural inventiveness. The Parisian churches of Saint-Eus­
tache and Saint-Etienne-du-Mont express these two 
attitudes. In these Gothic structures, the search for the 
‘architettura all’antica’ was partly driven by Serlio’s treatise, 
showcasing an early appearance of the architectural lan­
guage of the Renaissance.
4. Spreading the Renaissance: the ‘Säulenbücher’

## S. 110

110
Saint-Eustache
The church of Saint-Eustache in Paris is an important 
example of how treatises, and ‘written’ architecture in gener­
al, affect building design. The church was built between the 
1530s and the 1630s, intended to be a copy of Notre Dame 
as a way to reconfigure the neighborhood around it. On the 
outside, the composition of the whole south transept follows 
Notre Dame. However, once you enter the church, the space 
reveals itself as a hybrid. The structure is clearly Gothic, 
with tall and thin columns clustered together and the 
cross-vaulting. However, the columns are decorated with 
Renaissance ornaments only evident on close observation. 
Clearly, when the language of classical Antiquity spread 
across Europe, it arrived not on virgin territory but rather 
into a rich pre-existing building culture. In France, this 
culture was Gothic, which intertwined with a new classical 
language. 
If we look even closer, we also note that the classical 
ornament of the capitals is of two kinds. One is an exuber­
ant form of ornamentation, decorated with vegetation and 
other natural motifs and angels that crawl over the capitals. 
References to the classical vocabulary are evident, but each 
capital looks different. Together, they are known as ‘figu­
rate-foliate capitals’, a style dating from the 1520s to 1530s. 
The other kind of capital decoration is more conventional 
and follows the Corinthian and Composite canons, looking 
like copies of Roman capitals. The reason behind this 
change is the publication of Serlio’s book. We can even 
recognize precise matches with the orders in both capitals 
and bases. Friezes are similarly copied from Serlio. If we 
look even closer at these capitals in the southern side, we

## S. 111

111
see three-dimensional translations of two-dimensional en­
gravings, almost cut-and-pasted in stone and simply repeat­
ed on all sides. 
From a very complex, three-dimensional ornamentation 
and language in Renaissance Italy, we move to what is 
basically the translation into stone of paper figures. Archi­
tecture goes through a process of ‘mediatization’. It arrives 
in specific territories through copying from what it is de­
scribed in books (see chapter 3). We therefore see an in­
creasing normativity and a process of copying models, but 
also a struggle, with accompanying new strategies, to trans­
late architecture from the medium of paper to the building, 
with varying degrees of success. This struggle may have 
something to do with the inherently two-dimensional nature 
of classical architecture. The classical system comprises 
columns and architraves and is inherently a frontal system. 
This frontality is further emphasized once is mediated onto 
paper. The problem then becomes one of translation, from 
the two-dimensional book to the three-dimensional building: 
how to relate plan, elevation, and section, how to connect 
the different facades of the building, etc.
Saint-Etienne-du-Mont
The opposite is true for Saint-Etienne-du-Mont, whose 
construction began some years later and continued until the 
early of 17th century. By the superposition of horizontal 
layers, the entire façade showcases a literal search for com­
promise between Gothic section and Renaissance language. 
The highest point of conflict and union is in the center, 
where a Gothic window is framed by a structure recalling the 
motif of a triumphal arch standing on a second triumphal 
4. Spreading the Renaissance: the ‘Säulenbücher’

## S. 112

112
arch. Saint-Etienne-du-Mont also presents elements copied 
from Serlio, but these are used and rearranged creatively to 
solve particular design problems.
From around 1540 or 1550 onwards, thanks to the work 
of Serlio, but also to the growing diffusion of books on 
architecture already discussed in the previous chapter, a 
corpus of images was created. This corpus could be read by 
an ever-increasing public and could therefore widen the 
possibilities of architectural experimentation.
‘SÄULENBÜCHER’ OF THE NORTH 
Albrecht Dürer: Unterweysung der Messung
Dürer represents one of the more sophisticated and 
important examples of original northern treatises on archi­
tecture. In the third book of the Unterweysung der Messung 
(Nürnberg 1525), Dürer took up the Vitruvian idea that the 
column should be constructed according to determined 
guidelines. But he did not recognize different column types 
or even orders. Instead, Dürer maintained a uniform colum­
nar mode, which was not firmly bound to a formal canon 
and whose details were variable. Details were explicitly left 
to the imagination of the artist: ‘After that you may like the 
capital ornate with flowers, animals or other things to your 
liking’.

## S. 113

113
Hans Vredeman de Vries: Architectura 
During the second half of the 16th century, the centers of 
column-book production moved to Cologne and Strasbourg. In 
general, treatises produced at this time made rhetorical refer­
ences to Vitruvius, but in fact were mostly based on Serlio’s 
treatise (which was widely available in Flemish already in the 
late 1530s and in German from 1542), or Hans Blum, another 
important bookmaker. As already mentioned, the orders were 
understood as ornament and could be applied in a variety of 
contexts, including small-scale architecture (gable tops, por­
tals, doors, altars, epitaphs etc.). The authors of these books 
were seldom architects or master builders, but mostly painters 
or carpenters who etched their own plates and addressed their 
publications to their fellow craftsmen (stonemasons, carpen­
ters, painters and artisans). Despite the apparent arbitrariness 
of design and use of these books, the categories of the five 
orders and their characters were always retained, and thus the 
Vitruvian theme of the ornament was also maintained as a 
fundamental notion (decor, appropriateness). 
Hans Vredeman de Vries (1527–1609) represents the begin­
ning of this more independent phase of Nordic ‘col­
umns-books’. His book Architectura (first edition around 
1565) is composed as a collection of picture panels, on which 
individual parts of each order are presented with different 
ornamentations (‘manners’). The intention was to enrich the 
classical orders with a new, native vocabulary of forms, such 
as scrollwork, fittings, grotesques, diamond rustication, etc. 
This is not a misunderstanding of the Italian system of the 
orders, but a deliberate transformation of the classical canon 
into a local vocabulary.
4. Spreading the Renaissance: the ‘Säulenbücher’

## S. 114

114
Vredeman de Vries was the first to explicitly propose a 
model which was not a repetition of classical antiquity. In 
this sense, he truly moved beyond the Vitruvian tradition of 
the Italian Renaissance and adapted this language to his 
own local vocabulary of ornaments. This is visible already 
from the first page of Architectura, where space is entirely 
occupied by drawings and decorative elements in a sort of 
‘horror vacui’. 
Other books by Vredeman de Vries include Caryatidum 
(Antwerpen ca 1560); Das erst Buch gemacht auff de zwey 
Colomnen Dorica und Ionica (Antwerpen 1565); Das ander 
Buech gemacht auff die zway Colonnen Corinthia und Com­
posita (Antwerpen 1565); Architectura 3e stuck de oorden 
Tuschana (Antwerpen 1578). Among the successors of de 
Vires were Gabriel Krammer (1564-1606) and Coil Dietterlin 
(around 1550 - 1599), who followed the same strategy of lin­
guistic adaptation and similarly embraced a process of 
invention and original design in his architectural views.

## S. 115

115
4. Spreading the Renaissance: the ‘Säulenbücher’
CONCLUSION
The paradigms of imitation and invention already visible 
in Renaissance Rome can also be observed with the spread 
of the classical tradition outside of Italy, where the architec­
tural book turns in to a powerful tool for generating original 
variations of the orders in the local contexts of northern 
Europe (especially France, Germany and the Flanders).

Porta Maggiore; 
• 
Portion of wall near the Forum Romanum at 
the church Saints Cosmas and Damian;
Mantua, Italy:
• 
Palazzo del Te.

WHAT TO VISIT

## S. 139

139
5. Rustication
INTRODUCTION 
The political and religious program behind the quest of 
‘architettura all’antica’ imposed particular ‘functions’ of 
representation upon architecture. Rustication emerged as an 
element that competed with the column system and carried 
specific expressive and symbolical connotations that were 
transferred into moral qualities. Rustication introduced a dif­
ferent architectural principle in the discussion about Roman 
architecture and the five orders, namely the issue of the wall. 
The term ‘rustication’ originates from the Italian ‘rustico’, 
indicating a rough stonework. The definition of rustication 
implied the characteristic appearance of a wall, intentionally 
built out of uneven stones to convey solidity. It became codi­
fied as part of the repertory of architecture in the treatises of 
Sebastiano Serlio (see chapter 3). His treatises display how 
the codification of the classical language gives way to exper­
iments with form.

## S. 140

140
How political and religious programs transferred particular 
meanings and representative functions onto architecture.
Cortesi’s definition of the Palace of Cardinals and his defi­
nition of architecture.
The definition of rustication as an element competing with 
the system of the five orders, and their historical definition.
How the repertoire of rustication pushed architectural syn­
tax in new directions and how it entered cultural imagery 
as a symbol, an allegory, etc., within the arts.
The example of Palazzo del Te.
Sebastiano Serlio’s treatises.
The ambiguity of rustication.
Different influences of the codification of rustication and 
examples.

## S. 141

141
5. Rustication
Engraving of the Septizonium (1582) from Antoine Lafréry, Specvlvm Romanae Magnificentiae.

## S. 142

142
STREETS OF CONFLICT AND ARCHITECTURAL FUNC­
TION
As shown in the Tempietto, the political intentions and 
power conflicts of 16th-century Rome were transferred to and 
embodied by architecture, which in turn shaped the space of 
the city. The same was also true for the competition between 
important families, which often was not just symbolic or 
abstract, but lead to the damage of buildings and, in some 
cases, to the total destruction of palaces. The notions of 
power and defense are related to architecture just as much 
as religion and ancient architecture are intertwined. Not only 
was architecture meant to revive antiquity, achieving classi­
cal proportion and syntax, but it was also required to state 
authority and defend power. 
From the mid-14th century to the mid-16th century, it 
became common practice to give to the urban palace the ap­
pearance of a fortification, through the use of rough stone­
work. This rough stone, which contributed to a powerful and 
solid appearance, would be labelled ‘rustication’. The term 
covered a wide variety of forms and wall structures that 
generally produced an idea of solidity and massiveness. At 
the same time, it can also be considered a form of ornamen­
tation, or even an architectural element in its own right.
THE CARDINAL PALACE AND REPRESENTATION OF 
POWER: DE CARDINALATU LIBRI TRES
The treatise De Cardinalatu Libri Tres, by Paolo Cortesi, 
published in 1510, provides some insights into the formation

## S. 143

143
of this phenomenon. Cortesi was one of the most prominent 
humanists at the court of Pope Julius II. Active in the de­
bate on literary imitation, Cortesi was a supporter of the 
most purist and extremist positions, advocating for Cicero’s 
Latin as the only true language. Cortesi directly exhibited 
his mastery of Latin in his treatise De Cardinalatu Libri 
Tres. This was the last book, published after Cortesi’s 
death, of a long and productive literary career. It was in­
tended as a manual for cardinals, the office just below the 
pope in the Catholic hierarchy. Cardinals are elected by the 
pope and are therefore eligible themselves to be elected as 
popes. At this time, while cardinals did not have precise 
tasks, the figure of the cardinal, also defined as the ‘prince 
of the church’, began to emerge. The office of the cardinal 
began to function as a ministry of the pope’s state and 
assumed both religious and political roles.
The treatise aimed at detailing how to become a cardinal 
and how to ethically, religiously, and politically behave as 
one. Cortesi paid special attention to the cardinal’s palace, 
dedicating an entire chapter to it. Cortesi depicted the asso­
ciation between power and architecture in different ways. On 
the one hand, the cardinal, whose role represented papal 
authority, required a palace because he needed to guarantee 
the proper functioning of his household and the good behav­
ior of his family. In the figure of the cardinal, Cortesi said, 
good manners of the family are associated with good organ­
ization of the palace. On the other hand, the palace was 
considered the means to convey an image of power, through 
the incitement of admiration. 
5. Rustication

## S. 144

144
In De cardinalatu Libri Tres, Cortesi shows himself to be 
a learned man, a connoisseur of Vitruvius and Alberti, 
someone who reflects the architectural knowledge of his 
period. By following the logic of the Vitruvius’s treatise, 
Cortesi writes precise instructions for how to choose the 
location for a cardinal’s palace, how to organize its plan, 
how to decorate it, and how to arrange its interior spaces. 
What differentiated Cortesi from other contemporary think­
ers, such as Raphael, was his position on ornamentation. 
Cortesi considered exterior ornaments as function of the 
time, such as a foil, changing according to the fashion of 
the age. At the same time, the exterior ornaments had to fit 
with the social and political purposes of the building of 
which they were part.
Rustication as Ornament
The external facade of a palace enacted and directed 
contrasting impressions within the city, which made the role 
played by ornament very important. If, on the one hand, the 
palace should express splendor as the embodiment of a 
social role, on the other hand it was also prudently recom­
mended that it also express powerful magnificence. This 
message of power had to be as widely understandable as 
possible, so as to be intelligible by everyone. Rustication was 
meant to easily impress. Cortesi argues that it is this bal­
ance that the social meaning and function of ornamentation 
lies:
Decoration of the exterior of the Cardinal’s palace. 
Such [external] decorations of palaces which make them

## S. 145

145
5. Rustication
appear attractively designed and sumptuously execut­
ed are also to be recommended for reasons of pru­
dence. Thus, the ignorant mob will be deterred from 
threatening the cardinals with harm and from plun­
dering their goods by the mightiness of the building 
and through admiration for its opulence. Since it is 
clear that the uneducated multitude is usually led by 
its sense[s] rather than by rational reflection, we can 
see why the sight of the sumptuous cardinals’ palac­
es easily restrains the admiring multitude from doing 
harm; for, since the multitude is guided by the feeble 
[judgment] of the sense[s], it believes the cardinals’ pow­
er to be so great as to prevent the mob from expelling 
the cardinals or from plundering their goods. (Paolo 
Cortesi, De Cardinalatu Libri Tres, Book 2, De Domo)
To argue his position, Cortesi provided examples of 
failures and misfortunes, such as Pope Eugenius IV, who 
had to flee Rome because he was housed in a palace that 
was too modest and did not impress enough, demonstrating 
the dangers of not using architecture according to status. 
Therefore, for Cortesi the cardinal’s palace had to be attrac­
tive and sumptuous in order to trigger admiration and to be 
entirely associated with power. The argument that architec­
ture plays a social and political function is also developed 
expressively by Cortesi in the section of De Cardinalatu Libri 
Tres devoted to the organization of the plan: 
The plan [descriptio] of the house ought, moreover, 
to be considered in two ways: one according to the 
distribution of rooms [membra] and the other accord­
ing to the variety of ornamentation. Now the way in 
which a house is disposed [partition] usually chang­
es according to the condition of the times [temporum

## S. 146

146
conditio]. There is no doubt that the differences be­
tween the Greek and Roman modes [modum] of build­
ing reflects changes in the fashion of the times [ra­
tio temporum]. Indeed, even the Greeks differed among 
themselves at one and the same time in the manner 
[ratio] of building while the Romans employed some, the 
Ionic, others the Corinthian, others the Doric and yet 
others the Tuscan type [genus] of construction. Later, 
due to the times, there was not only a turning away 
from the ancient system of design [ratio symmetriae], 
but a new manner [ratio] of building was introduced 
as well. (Paolo Cortesi, De Cardinalatu Libri Tres, 
Book 2, De Domo, The Plan of the Cardinal’s Palace)
Cortesi classified the way in which the house is disposed 
as a ‘Temporum Conditio’, strongly emphasizing how living 
habits change over time. Similarly, styles were also under­
stood as expressions of ways of living at different times and 
with different needs. Ornamentation should be designed 
according to the taste of the ancients. As part of the orna­
mental vocabulary of palaces, rustication also became part 
of ‘architettura all’antica’, encoded with associations to 
power, just like other elements of classical ornamentation. 
At the same time, Cortesi dismissed the Gothic as the result 
of a bizarre taste for novelty. The way to correctly choose a 
style according to social conventions was the criterion of ‘fit­
ness’. By adopting the right architectural idiom, Cortesi 
argued, a building would be built efficiently, not expensively, 
it will be sophisticated, and it will convey the right impres­
sion of power.

## S. 147

147
5. Rustication
THE ELEMENT OF RUSTICATION AND ITS CODIFICA­
TION
As suggested by Cortesi, attached to the notion of rusti­
cation was a social meaning. The interest in rustication was 
triggered by the attentive study of a number of monuments, 
such as the Forum of Augustus and the Palace of Nerva, 
Porta Maggiore, Ss. Cosma and Damian, that circulated in 
different printed reproductions, such as those by Fra Giocon­
do and Antonio da San Gallo. The particular associations 
produced by these examples, such as the ‘ancient’, the 
‘primitive’ and the ‘original’, were carried over into moral 
connotations of strength, incorruptness, virtue, military 
power, and protection.
Importantly, the use of rustication introduced a different 
architectural principle—the wall—into the discussion about 
Roman architecture and the system of the five orders. A 
rusticated architecture is an architecture of walls, not just 
an architecture of columns and architraves. A reflection on 
the uses of rustication allowed architects to explore the 
tensions between the two fundamental tectonic principles: 
columns/trabeation and wall. This in turn unfolded a new 
range of formal experimentations.
The awareness of these two codes, column and wall, as a 
means to achieve decorum and expression became particu­
larly visible in early 16th-century palace architecture. Bra­
mante expressed this tension by superimposing these two 
systems in Palazzo Caprini, the so-called palace of Raphael, 
which no longer exists. A rusticated base with arches was 
interrupted by a sort of rusticated architrave forming the

## S. 148

148
socle for the upper body and articulated by means of double 
Doric pilasters, closed behind by a massive wall articulated 
by set-back windows.
AMBIGUITY AND EXPLORATION IN RUSTICATION
Rusticated architecture was explored in a dialogue with 
Antiquity and should not be considered a new invention. The 
genealogy of rustication itself is found within Roman archi­
tecture. But from then on, two different characters of rustica­
tion emerged, generating a certain ambiguity. 
On the one hand, rustication evoked the essential charac­
ters of the wall, such as strength and solidity, and evoked 
the processes of its construction. The most famous reference 
in Rome was the wall of the Septizonium. On the other hand, 
because of the roughness associated with rustication, the 
term also evoked the notion of ruin. These two opposite 
interpretations injected into the notion of rustication a 
conceptual ambiguity, at once construction and destruction. 
Investigating the formal possibilities of rustication invited a 
reconsideration of the tectonic dimension of the architectural 
orders and their elements, as well as of architectural typolo­
gies. Rustication began to be associated with building types 
such as porticos or city gates because of its suggestive 
strength. In turn, columns began to be conceived as assem­
blages of different independent rusticated stones, rather than 
one monolithic piece. An entire scenario of new references 
was presented in the fine arts that expressed an interest in 
the symbolism of rustication. Other themes also came into 
play, such as the relationship with nature—the theme of the

## S. 149

149
5. Rustication
grotto or cave, for example—and the materiality of architec­
ture.
Palazzo del Te
One of the most challenging uses of rustication in an 
early 16th-century building was presented by Giulio Romano 
in Mantua. In the early 1520s, Federico II Gonzaga, Duke of 
Mantua, asked Romano, a pupil of Raphael’s, to work for 
him in Mantua. Among other building works he was respon­
sible for, the Palazzo del Te (1524–1535) expresses rustica­
tion in its most convincing form. The palace was aligned 
alongside the city, but its main façade was oriented towards 
it. Romano created an axial composition with square court­
yards, surrounded by enclosed spaces and overlooking a gar­
den, with ponds and a nymphaeum. 
The façade is articulated through the almost redundant 
superimposition of an order of pilasters on top of a very 
heavily rusticated wall. This introduces the two chief archi­
tectural themes of the building. The system of Doric pilas­
ters and heavy Doric architraves recalls in its decoration the 
Basilica Emilia. What begins in the main façade continues 
and increases exponentially as the building unfolds, reaching 
a climax in the Androne (courtyard). This dynamism pre­
sents a competition, not merely a tension, between an archi­
tecture of smooth orders and an architecture of rough 
stones. For instance, the column shafts are presented as 
untreated stones. The effect of rustication is clearly present­
ed in opposition to the Doric order, which in turn is brought 
back to its hierarchical and tectonic significance. With this

## S. 150

150
building, Romano intentionally presented the interplay be­
tween column and wall, demonstrating his awareness of 
architectural codes and syntax. He learned this when he was 
in Rome during his youth, while working with Raphael and 
experiencing Bramante’s architecture. 
Romano worked in the social context of the Gonzaga 
court, at the time one of the most important courts in Italy. 
The Palazzo del Te, located just outside of the city, is of a 
type somewhere between a palazzo and a villa. The compli­
cated pictorial and decorative programs also define the 
construction not just as a place for leisure and relaxation 
but also as a sophisticated and reflective environment. In 
the Palazzo del Te architecture relates to the courtly context 
rather differently than with Cortesi. Architecture here be­
comes the expression of sophistication and refinement rather 
than of strength and power.
SERLIO AND THE CODIFICATION OF RUSTICATION 
Like Giulio Romano, architects from the circle of Bra­
mante took up rustication as a motif to experiment with. As 
a result, during the 1530s and 1540s a codification of rusti­
cation as an essential element of ‘architettura all’antica’ 
emerged. This codification was first laid out in Serlio’s Seven 
Books. As seen earlier in chapter 3, Serlio’s treatise played 
an important role in the dissemination of ‘architettura 
all’antica’. In this process, the book played a fundamental 
role as the medium for the propagation of knowledge and 
did so primarily visually.

## S. 151

151
In the third book, dedicated to antiquity, Serlio expresses 
an association between ancient and ‘modern’ architecture. 
For instance, the Tempietto was presented together with a 
series of examples of ancient centralized buildings. Serlio 
here expands the definition of ancient architecture to include 
as much of ancient as modern buildings. As a result, he sees 
no stylistic differences between the best buildings of the 
present and those from the past. 
On the frontispiece of Serlio’s third book is a ruin, hous­
ing the allegorical figure of architecture. Inscribed on top of 
is the phrase, ‘Roma quanta fuit ipsa ruina docet’ (‘what 
Rome was, this very ruin teaches us’). While the category of 
the ruin was still deeply associated with the meaning and 
authority of retrievable knowledge, the ruin was also depict­
ed as an entirely rusticated structure, suggesting that in a 
ruinous landscape some part of the core of Roman architec­
ture remained. As a result, the rusticated building was 
presented as literally the last existent element of Roman 
architecture, and thus it retained an ‘original’ character. By 
the time of Serlio, rustication had become the basic struc­
ture of architecture. This is also confirmed by his first book 
where, after presenting general rules and the five orders, 
Serlio produced a compendium of rusticated structures.
The Definition of Rustication
In Serlio’s treatise, the five orders are presented as iso­
lated elements in a hierarchy, but without context, simply 
placed next to each other. The orders become the main 
‘actors’ and Vitruvius’s authority is no longer necessary to 
5. Rustication

## S. 152

152
introduce them. Immediately following the orders, Serlio 
introduces the theme of gates. The pretext for discussing 
city gates was the necessity of protecting buildings located 
within cities. The topic of gates then allowed Serlio to inves­
tigate rustication and to present a wide range of formal 
combinations for it. An example of a wall, namely the por­
tion of wall near the Forum Romanum at the church Saints 
Cosmas and Damian, was then represented to introduce the 
ancient Roman origins of rustication. It is also in these 
pages that for the first time Serlio employs the word rustica­
tion. The etymology of the word rustic, as used by Serlio, 
implies simplicity and has a strong agricultural connotation, 
evoking non-urban life as the ideal context for this kind of 
stonework. 
Rustication as the origin or the basis idiom for architec­
ture was reinforced by Serlio in the introduction to the gen­
eral principles of architecture. When providing examples of 
possible combinations of orders, he particularly emphasized 
the Doric and the Tuscan, for their solidity and simplicity 
and their compatibility with rustication. From these exam­
ples, he then extrapolated a kind of Italian ‘native’ architec­
ture.
At this time, rustication embodied a primitive aspect, the 
origin of architecture, and therefore a moral virtue. It also 
brought, syntactically and conceptually, a different under­
standing of architecture itself. The wall was a different 
system than the column and could be combined with the 
orders in many different ways, as Serlio demonstrated in 
great detail.

## S. 153

153
CONCLUSION 
The notion of rustication entered into the architectural 
vocabulary of the Renaissance through a complex process of 
experimentation and codification. It thus introduced the 
element of the wall into the lexicon of 16th-century architects 
as a system with powerful and ambiguous significance. 
Through the work of Cortesi and Serlio, rustication was 
made part of the classical vocabulary and turned into an ap­
propriate source for ‘architettura all’antica’.
5. Rustication
