## S. 157

157
6. Andrea Palladio
INTRODUCTION 
Andrea Palladio (1510–80) was a Venetian architect who, 
trained as a stonemason, quickly became one of the most 
influential figures of late Renaissance. He completed dozens 
of projects, mostly churches and villas, all around Venice 
and the Venetian countryside. In 1570 Palladio published a 
treatise entitled The Four Books on Architecture (I Quattro 
Libri di Architettura). The book became one of the most 
translated and quoted references of the Italian Renaissance, 
turning ‘Palladian architecture’ into a proper architectural 
lexicon, adopted by architects until well into the 20th centu­
ry. Palladio’s career should be understood through the 
changing architectural environment of northern Italy, which 
in turn reflected the social economies of the Veneto region.

## S. 158

158
Where Palladio is situated in the socio-political context of 
16th century Venice.
The structure of the Four Books, and how it promotes 
Palladio’s understanding of the architecture of the private 
house.
The basic design principles of Palladio’s villas, and the me­
aning of notions such as decor and convenienza.
Palladio’s attitude toward the use of the orders and rusti­
cation.
TO KNOW

## S. 159

159
6. Andrea Palladio
‘Villa Rotonda’ (1566-70?) as illustrated in the Quattro Libri.

## S. 160

160
PALLADIO AND THE FOUR BOOKS
Andrea Palladio’s Four Books on Architecture quickly 
became one of the most successful and influential treatises 
of the Renaissance. The book deals with the problem of how 
to use classical elements in actual buildings, and for a wide 
range of design problems, often going beyond the orders. In 
the preface to the book, Palladio writes:
Guided by a natural inclination, I dedicated myself to 
the study of architecture in my youth, and since I al­
ways held the opinion that the ancient Romans, as in 
many other things, had also greatly surpassed all those 
who came after them in building well, I elected as my 
master and guide Vitruvius, who is the only ancient 
writer on this art. I set myself the task of investigating 
the remains of ancient buildings that have survived de­
spite the ravages of time and cruelty of the barbarians, 
and finding much in them much worthier of study than I 
first thought, I began to measure all their parts minute­
ly and with the greatest care. … Accordingly, seeing 
how different the usual manner of building is from the 
things that I had observed in those structures and had 
read about in Vitruvius and Leon Battista Alberti and 
the other excellent writers who came after Vitruvius, 
and also from those which I myself built recently, which 
have been greatly appreciated and praised by those who 
employed me, I consider it worthy of man … to make 
public the design of those buildings that I have collect­
ed over such a long period and at such personal risk.
Palladio announces at the beginning of the book that, 
much like Raphael in his letter to Leo X, he went to Rome 
and recorded and measured ruins. Thanks to these measure­

## S. 161

161
6. Andrea Palladio
ments, he was able to understand the principles of classical 
architecture. Survey drawings like Palladio’s fit in a universe 
of drawings produced in Rome around 1520, at the time of 
Raphael’s letter, suggesting a collective and intense engage­
ment with ruins as sources of authority. 
The House as Model
Palladio measured mostly temples, as these were the 
most available buildings still standing. Later in the preface, 
however, Palladio says that he wants to begin the treatise 
with private houses. The reason for this is explained by 
means of an origin myth. Palladio argues that it is from the 
house that the public building originates. It is very likely, 
Palladio continues, that people first built private houses, 
then started building public spaces. These were then mod­
elled on private houses. According to Palladio, the house is 
therefore the first element of architecture. This contradicts 
Vitruvius, who argued that it was through communities that 
architecture was generated. Vitruvius mentions huts, but 
architecture for him is exemplified by the temple. The ideal 
of the Vitruvian man is itself associated with the design of 
temples. Palladio inverts this paradigm. As a consequence, 
he is faced with a problem of references to the past. What 
models of Roman private houses are available in the 16th 
century? With regards to books, we know that Vitruvius 
speaks in quite general terms about houses in Book VI. 
Physical ruins of Roman houses, on the other hand, did not 
survive (as discussed in chapter 2, the architecture of Antiq­
uity as it was known in the 16th century was an architecture 
of public buildings).

## S. 162

162
As reaction to this obscurity, Palladio’s second book is 
dedicated to demonstrating that one can actually design 
houses according to the principles of the ancients. He starts 
by discussing ‘decoro e convenienza’. ‘Decoro’ is derived 
from decor, fittingness, the fifth Vitruvian principle of archi­
tecture. A house, according to decor, should fit the social 
status of its inhabitant. It is with this same idea that Pal­
ladio begins the book. Architecture is a matter of social 
status. Concurrently, ‘convenienza’ is suitability. A house 
should accommodate a family, as the cornerstone of society. 
An architect should pay attention not just to the facade of 
the building and the more the public parts, but also to the 
services and practical aspects of living, as well as the more 
private and intimate ones. Just like with the human body, 
whose more apparent parts, such as the face, are also the 
more pleasant to the eye, so with a house, whose visible 
components, like the facade, are more pleasant than the 
private, hidden ones. However, all components are equally 
important and should be designed with care.
The houses described in Palladio’s second book, like 
Palazzo Valmarana, are based on Greek models, which 
Palladio reconstructs at the end of the book, suggesting 
that he is working according to the principles laid down by 
Vitruvius. These houses are sequences of geometric rooms, 
organized around an inner square and along a symmetrical 
axis. Of course, the Vitruvian model that Palladio presents 
is a form of constructed evidence, speculative and largely 
hypothetic. Importantly, Palladio locates his own project on 
the same level as the Greek one, as a form of personal 
promotion. Therefore, simply by a juxtaposition of images, 
Palladio makes three important claims:

## S. 163

163
6. Andrea Palladio
1. It is possible to reconstruct houses according to the 
principles of the ancients (despite the fact that we 
have no idea what they actually looked like).
2. Palladio is the authority to follow in this matter.
3. He is able to design according to these principles.
The Palladian Villa
The second part of the first book is dedicated to villas, 
which are houses in the countryside. Here Palladio does not 
even mention ancient villas but discusses only his own pro­
jects. To understand why the typology of the house was so 
important for Palladio, we need to look at the social and 
geographical context of mid-16th century Venice. Since the 
late 15th century, Venice had undergone significant political 
change. With the fall of Constantinople in the mid-15th 
century, Venice was transformed from a naval power into a 
land-based economy. The Venetian hinterland became the 
main source of income and the Venetian upper class rein­
vented itself as gentry. Members of the gentry class pos­
sessed not only a city palace but also an equally important 
villa outside the city, used to both escape urban life and to 
farm and produce crops. Villas were places of leisure as well 
as private industries, generating wealth for the upper class. 
Palladio addresses this very specific class of society, who 
needed both a palace in the city and a villa in the country­
side.
Palladio presents himself as the provider of both these 
estates, specifically targeting the Venetian upper class that 
was becoming more and more dependent on the wealth of

## S. 164

164
the land. By making a (supposedly) archaeological reference 
to the Vitruvian past, Palladio is therefore providing an 
architecture that offers the Venetian gentry the mold of the 
Roman senator. He refers implicitly to Roman sources on 
agrarian life that provide description of villas. A very wellknown example is a ancient roman treatise of agriculture, 
De Re Rustica, by Columella, which says:
The size of the villa and the number of its parts should 
be proportioned to the whole enclosure, and it should 
be divided into three groups: the villa urbana or man­
or house, the villa rustica or farmhouse, and the vil­
la fructuaria or storehouse. The manor house should 
be divided in turn into winter apartments and summer 
apartments, in such a way that the winter bedrooms 
may face the sunrise at the winter solstice, and the 
winter dining-room face the sunset at the equinox. […] 
The summer bedrooms, on the other hand, should look 
toward the midday sun at the time of the equinox, 
but the dining-rooms of that season should look to­
ward the rising sun of winter. The baths should face 
the setting sun of summer, that they may be lighted 
from midday up to evening. The promenades should 
be exposed to the midday sun at the equinox, so as 
to receive both the maximum of sun in winter and the 
minimum in summer. But in the part devoted to farm 
uses there will be placed a spacious and high kitchen, 
that the rafters may be free from the danger of fire, 
and that it may offer a convenient stopping-place for 
the slave household at every season of the year. It will 
be best that cubicles for unfettered slaves be built to 
admit the midday sun at the equinox; for those who 
are in chains there should be an underground pris­
on, as wholesome as possible, receiving light through 
a number of narrow windows built so high from the 
ground that they cannot be reached with the hand.

## S. 165

165
6. Andrea Palladio
According to Columella, the villa is made of three parts: 
the villa urbana for dwelling, the villa rustica for production, 
and the villa fructuaria for storage. In the ancient concep­
tion of the villa, we already have a composite structure, serv­
ing both leisure and industry. Other ancient sources similarly 
refer to the villa as possessing a complex infrastructure. For 
instance, in his description of one of his own villas, Pliny the 
Younger writes that:
The face of the country is extremely beautiful: imag­
ine to yourself an Amphitheatre of immense circum­
ference, such as could be formed only by the hand of 
nature. … You would be delighted were you to take a 
prospect of this place from a neighboring mountain as 
you could scarce believe you were looking upon a real 
country. [...] My house, though built at the foot of a 
hill, has a view as if it stood upon the brow of it. The 
ascent is so gradual and easy, that you find yourself on 
the top almost before you perceive yourself ascending.
One of the aspects emphasized here is how the villa sits 
in the landscape, giving the occupants a sense of ownership 
over the land, for instance by providing a view or by being 
easily accessible. The conditions of how one designs a villa 
therefore have to do with orientation, wind, landscape, and 
so on. 
Palladio’s Villa Emo, for instance, is a symmetrical 
building, whose main body (the villa urbana) is a geometri­
cal body based on a grid. To the sides are the two wings 
occupied by the farmhouses (villa rustica and fructuaria). 
Palladio then uses the productive parts of the villa to grow 
the overall body of the building, and to increase the rep­

## S. 166

166
resentational aspect of the villa. The villa looks much bigger 
than it is. Thanks to the use of arcades and other classical 
elements, the farmhouses become part of the overall compo­
sition. At the same time, Palladio makes clear that there is 
a hierarchy, by creating a monumental entrance at the 
center, with a pediment and a monumental staircase. The 
two sides are more humble compositions of brick. This 
strategy can be observed in many of Palladio’s designs. In 
Architectural Principles in the Age of Humanism (1949), the 
historian Rudolf Wittkower argued that, in fact, all of Pal­
ladio’s villas were based on the same gridded scheme, with 
plans made of squares and rectangles dividing the house in 
zones of modular elements. 
Indeed, Palladio was always very explicit about this 
strategy. In the second book of his Quattro Libri, he dis­
cusses how he added ‘frontispieces’ on the facades of these 
villas. This was a way to signal the building, making it 
stand out and easy to recognize, adding status and a place 
for the owner to place his family’s emblems. Palladio justi­
fies the adoption of these fronts by arguing that the ancients 
also did this in their temples and public buildings, which, as 
he explained earlier, derive from private houses. Palladio 
then legitimizes his designs by reverse-engineering the archi­
tecture of the Roman house through the temple. In other 
words, by referring to the Roman house as the model for the 
temple, Palladio was legitimized to adopt templar elements 
for his houses, which were themselves based on the Roman 
house. For instance, the Portico Ottavia in Rome, built in 
the Corinthian order, is quite literally applied in the facade 
of the Villa Cornaro. The same portico can also be found in

## S. 167

167
the barn of Villa Angarano, only with the humbler Tuscan 
order. 
It is important to remember that Palladio’s alleged 
connection between houses and temples is largely made up. 
It is a way to historically justify a design path tailored for 
the Venetian bourgeoisie who were anxious to legitimize, 
maintain, and protect their social status. This social status 
was a matter of rights and public order, which makes this 
historical legitimization very important. In his second book, 
then, Palladio presents a design strategy that targets a 
specific social class, eager to model itself on Roman citizen­
ry, but that also allows architects to use the vocabulary of 
Roman architecture in different contexts. In fact, if we look 
at Palladio’s architecture, we see that he takes this very 
basic position even further. Palladio was accomplishing two 
operations at the same time:
1. Providing a model for domestic architecture, which 
became incredibly successful.
2. Providing an enormous amount of freedom in combin­
ing architectural elements into original compositions.
Palazzo Thiene
Palladio’s inventiveness is demonstrated in several exam­
ples. The Palazzo Thiene in Vicenza (1540s), for instance, 
is the result of a very free composition of elements. The base 
of the facade is a very rough rusticated wall. Above the wall 
is an order of pilasters, with another, cleaner order of rusti­
cation behind. At the top of the pilasters are composite 
6. Andrea Palladio

## S. 168

168
capitals, frieze, cornice and roof. The window frames are 
developed in a way that, while on paper they are almost 
invisible, in reality they are clearly three-dimensional masses. 
Even stranger is an additional profile, running along the 
upper parts of the windows but pushed behind the pilasters, 
as if the these were only thin strips, glued onto a preexisting 
structure. Palladio is here playing with our expectations: the 
strip neither interrupts the pilasters nor runs over them. 
As discussed in the previous chapter, during the 16th 
century architects started experimenting with the relation­
ship between walls and pilasters as two fundamental tectonic 
structures. The combination of the two then became a way 
to articulate both the horizontal composition (doors, win­
dows...) and the vertical layering (floors, orders) of ele­
ments. We can observe the strangeness of Palladio’s strategy 
by comparing Palazzo Thiene with an earlier example of a 
similar typology, the 15th-century Palazzo Medici by 
Michelozzo, where the tectonic logic between the different 
levels of rustication is very legible and clear. 
Palazzo Valmarana 
Another important example of Palladio’s incentives is the 
corner of the Palazzo Valmarana, in Vicenza (1565). The 
facade is decorated with a set of very big columns of the 
Corinthian order, called ‘colossal’ because they run up 
through two floors. The bottom floor, however, also has its 
own pilasters, with the rustication framing the base and 
providing an ornamental impression. The entablature on top 
fits with the colossal order, while the attic above is also

## S. 169

169
divided along the lines of colossal columns. Similar to Pala­
zzo Thiene, the entablature of the first floor sits behind the 
colossal columns. The logic of the facade, however, is called 
into question in the corner. The main tectonic system is 
abandoned; the windows and openings on the sides are 
smaller and more numerous, cutting through the cornices 
and decorative implant of the facade. The column in the cor­
ner is also shorter and is completed with caryatids. 
The emergence of an interest in the tectonic languages of 
architecture and their effects on the expectations of the view­
er can be traced to early-16th century Rome, and especially 
coming from the circle of Bramante. For example, the Palaz­
zo Branconio dell'Aquila by Raphael (ca. 1520) demonstrat­
ed how material elements could be reorganized without 
necessarily following tectonic logics, so as to drive the atten­
tion of the viewer. Here the position of the niches on top of 
columns contradicts structural continuity; similarly, the pres­
ence of a cornice instead of an architrave on the first floor 
does not follow structural logics; the decorative medals of 
the façade displayed famous architects and artists with 
whom Raphael wanted to be associated. All these elements 
evidence an explicit intention to experiment with the tectonic 
and expressive possibilities of architecture, without necessar­
ily abiding to the strict linguistic rules of classical canons.
6. Andrea Palladio

## S. 170

170
CONCLUSION 
Andrea Palladio constitutes one of the most significant 
interpreters of the classical tradition in architecture. His 
Four Books became an important reference for generations of 
architects, including the English Inigo Jones (157–1652), 
Lord Burlington (1694–1753) (see chapter 16), and even 
Thomas Jefferson (1742–1826). By tailoring Antique archi­
tecture for the very specific clientele of mid-16th century 
Veneto, Palladio provided a very liberating classical vocabu­
lary, at once intellectually coherent and capable of be con­
stantly recombined. This direction became so popular that it 
came to be identified as a Palladian Style. Palladio’s experi­
mental approach towards architecture was part of a new 
trajectory of classical composition, where the rigid canons of 
the High Renaissance were becoming the object of a com­
plex and highly sophisticated process of reinterpretation. 
This process came to be known as Mannerism.

## S. 174

174
SOURCES
• 
Robert Venturi, Complexity and Contradiction 
in Architecture. New York: Museum of Modern 
Art 1966. https://xenotheka.delbeke.
arch.ethz.ch/venturi_complexity_and_contra­
diction_in_architecture/
Philadelphia, USA:

• 
Guild House;
Rome, Italy:
• 
Casa Girasole.

WHAT TO VISIT

## S. 175

175
7. Mannerism
INTRODUCTION 
Palladio’s compositional liberties towards normative 
models can be defined as Mannerist. This term describes a 
set of complex interpretative attitudes towards the tradition­
al processes of architectural composition, such as the princi­
ple of imitation, which emerged during the 16th century. 
Mannerism, especially in the figures of Palladio and Michel­
angelo, continued to fascinate architects throughout history. 
One example of the enduring influence of Mannerism archi­
tect is architect Robert Venturi, who adopted Palladio’s 
approach to architecture in his writings and projects during 
the 1970s and 80s.

## S. 176

176
The principle of imitation.
 
What Mannerism means and its key features in architec­
ture.
Historiographical origins of Mannerism and its possible 
genealogy.
Meaning of the concepts of complexity and contradiction 
for Robert Venturi.
TO KNOW

## S. 177

177
7. Mannerism
Robert Venturi, Vanna Venturin House, 1962-64.

## S. 178

178
THE PRINCIPLE OF IMITATION
In order to fully understand the meaning and significance 
of Mannerism, we need to first investigate the ways in which 
design itself was characterized and articulated during the 
16th century. The principle of imitation is a crucial aspect of 
this process, as it clarifies how early modern architects relat­
ed to the notions of invention and creativity. In the letter to 
Leo X, Raphael writes:
Of these ornaments, there is no need to say more than that 
they all derive from the five Orders used by the ancients—
namely Doric, Ionic, Corinthian,Tuscan and Attic. …
There are two further works in addition to the three men­
tioned [by Vitruvius]: namely Attic and Tuscan, which 
were not, however, much used by the ancients. The Attic 
has columns with four faces, the Tuscan is very similar 
to the Doric, as will be seen in the prosecution of what 
we intend to do and show. And there will also be many 
buildings composed of different styles [maniere], such 
as Ionic with Corinthian, Doric with Corinthian, Tuscan 
with Doric, depending upon what seemed best to the 
artificer when matching these buildings appropriately 
to their intention, especially in the case of temples.
Raphael suggests that, alongside the canonic uses of the 
orders, there are also instances where these can be used and 
‘composed’ in different ways, following other ‘maniere’. 
These models can then generate new objects and architec­
tural elements. This idea follows the concept of ‘imitation’ 
as it was understood in Italian humanism, a notion that was 
in many ways opposite to how we understand creativity 
today. Today creativity is almost entirely associated with 
originality—one is creative when doing something new. Until

## S. 179

179
7. Mannerism
the 18th century however, creativity existed only in a mean­
ingful relationship with a previous model. This model should 
still be recognized, but so should one’s contribution or 
interpretation. This idea was first developed in early Renais­
sance literature, where the ancients were rediscovered and 
became models for modern poetry and prose. In order to 
define this relationship, we can refer to a letter by Francesco 
Petrarca, an important Italian writer in the early Renais­
sance: 
An imitator must see to it that what he writes is simi­
lar, but not the very same; and the similarity, moreover, 
should be not like that of a painting or statue to the 
person represented, but rather like that of a son to a 
father, where there is often great difference in the fea­
tures and members, and yet after all there is a shad­
owy something,— akin to what our painters call one’s 
air,— hovering about the face, and especially the eyes, 
out of which there grows a likeness that immediately, 
upon our beholding the child, calls the father up before 
us. If it were a matter of measurement every detail 
would be found to be different, and yet there certain­
ly is some subtle presence there that has this effect. 
In much the same way we writers, too, must see to it 
that along with the similarity there is a large measure 
of dissimilarity; and furthermore such likeness as there 
is must be elusive, something that it is impossible to 
seize except by a sort of still-hunt, a quality to be felt 
rather than defined. In brief, we may appropriate an­
other’s thought, and may even copy the very colours 
of his style, but we must abstain from borrowing his 
actual words. The resemblance in the one case is hid­
den away below the surface; in the other it stares the 
reader in the face. The one kind of imitation makes 
poets; the other … apes. It may all be summed up

## S. 180

180
by saying with Seneca, and with Flaccus before him, 
that we must write just as the bees make honey, not 
keeping the flowers but turning them into a sweet­
ness of our own, blending many very different flavours 
into one, which shall be unlike them all, and better.’
As explained by Petrarca, imitation is not mere reproduc­
tion, but rather a much more subtle and in many ways 
problematic form of resemblance. There is no reason why 
one would just do something different for the sake of it. 
Instead, creativity exists in choosing, in finding the right 
references. 
The problem of imitation, then, lies in finding the right 
models. This, in turn, produces contradictory positions in 
terms of what and how these models can be chosen. Accord­
ing to some, one should explore and freely take models from 
everywhere, until the right path is revealed. As the bee takes 
nectar from the flower and mixes it in the beehive, so artists 
take from many models, chosen as they best fit with their 
will, and make them into one single whole. Others argue 
that some models are better than others, and those should 
be given precedence. This latter position was typical of the 
16th century, where the anxiety about following the models of 
Antiquity was stronger. For example, as seen in chapter 3, 
Raphael proposed a process for selecting Roman antiquities. 
Mannerism needs to be understood within an ongoing 
debate around the principle of imitation, where architects 
started to ‘push’ the boundaries of this system. The adopted 
models are still visible, but stretched to the limits, and 
therefore at once highly regarded and dangerous.

## S. 181

181
THE DEFINITION OF MANNERISM
The term Mannerism was first introduced at the begin­
ning of the 20th century, in reference to a moment of transi­
tion between the Renaissance and the Baroque. It expresses 
the way in which architectural design was conceived by some 
architects, such as Michelangelo and Palladio, already from 
the 1520s, in which the rules of architecture were rendered in 
original and sophisticated ways, generating a strong sense 
of ambiguity. 
All the architecture discussed so far has followed the 
logic of imitation: selecting multiple models, making the 
chosen model recognizable, as well as showing the ability to 
play with them. In Mannerism, the model was also adopted 
as a way to surpass the mere copy. Historical debates on 
the causes of these shift are still ongoing. On the one hand, 
Mannerism can be seen as a reaction to the classical canon 
developed up to themed-16th century by extremely skilled 
artists, who challenged the canonical repertoire of the high 
Renaissance. On the other hand, the restlessness and ironic 
initiatives of Mannerism are also interpreted as an expres­
sion of the turmoil in the political and religious conditions 
typical of the 16th century, such as the Reformation and the 
Counter-Reformation. 
Some key concepts of Mannerism are:
• 
Ambiguity. The reinterpretation of architectural ele­
ments and of their relationship generates new and 
puzzling relationships between the parts, which do not 
necessarily abide to the logics of architectural compo­
7. Mannerism

## S. 182

182
sition (see ‘the double functioning elements’ below). 
This is the case of some of Palladio’s project, such as 
the façade for Palazzo Valmarana (see chapter 7).
• 
Playing with the rules of architecture. Mannerist 
architecture is predicated on what can described as a 
sort of emancipation and detachment from the norms 
of classical language. This allows for a playful ap­
proach towards the manipulation of this language.
• 
Self-awareness and sophistication. What can be seen 
as the playful attitude of Mannerism towards the rules 
of the classical canon is actually generated from the 
conscious study and deep understanding of these 
rules. The resulting composition is produced through 
a very sophisticated and accurate process of reinter­
pretation and elaboration. 
• 
Striking and confusing visual effect. The resulting 
effect is often strange and confusing, with elements 
such as niches, walls and pillars behaving in the 
overall composition as something different. 
ROBERT VENTURI AND MANNERISM
The historical recognition of Mannerism during the 20th 
century had a profound impact on architecture practices, 
especially from the 1670s onwards. The American architect 
Robert Venturi (1925–2018) was one of the main representa­
tives of ‘postmodern’ architecture, was particularly interested 
in the architecture of Andrea Palladio, and especially in 
Palladio’s interpretative freedom adopted in the composition 
of architectural elements. Venturi’s first project, the house 
for his mother Vanna Venturi, completed in 1964, evidences

## S. 183

183
a sort of ‘Palladian’ approach to architecture with strong 
Mannerist bent. In this house, Venturi made explicit histori­
cal references and played with these references and their 
meaning. The first reference is the Vitruvian man, embodied 
by the figure of Vanna herself, which seems to be the meas­
uring system for the whole building. 
Moreover, Venturi made the very ‘Palladian’ gesture of 
putting a monumental pediment on top the house. This is a 
thin screen, added to the façade rather than part of its 
structure. The house is thus turned into a monument by mo­
bilizing the same sort of language of ancient public architec­
ture that Palladio had adopted centuries earlier. At the 
same time, the pediment is cut into two, an explicit reference 
to Casa Girasole in Rome by Luigi Moretti (1950), a build­
ing beloved by Venturi. This was a palazzina, a typical 
high-class apartment building for the Roman bourgeoisie, 
somehow echoing Palladio’s clientele. This house similarly 
has a screen, placed on the front of the building, with a 
pediment on top, again another reference to Palladio. In 
quoting Moretti then, Venturi is also quoting Palladio. 
In the Guild House, a house for the elderly completed in 
1963, Venturi would again pick up this strategy. The build­
ing is symmetrical, with a pediment represented in the fa­
cade as a way to give to the humble building a more monu­
mental appearance. The pediment is in fact a window, a 
negative space, which illuminates the television room for the 
residents—one could say the most important space of the 
building. The antennas on the top are gilded, as a way to 
celebrate the simple program of the building. In both Van­
7. Mannerism

## S. 184

184
na’s House and the Guild House, Venturi adopted a Palladi­
an strategy on two distinct levels:
1. By adding symbolic and decorative elements to the 
buildings, such as pediments and gilded antennas, 
Venturi provide status to an otherwise rather simple 
program.
2. In adopting references from the Vitruvian tradition 
and from other buildings, Venturi explicitly took up a 
license to ‘play’ with the elements of architecture. 
Both these strategies explicitly echoed the Palladian 
tradition. In an interview, Venturi spoke of Palladio’s influ­
ence: 
Well, speaking of influence and Palladio, one of the most 
thrilling and relevant experiences I ever had was reading 
Rudolph Wittkower’s book on Palladio. There are two 
parts to that work. One is focused on the issue of propor­
tion, which does not interest me at all—it never has, not 
the golden mean nor Corb’s modulor. But Wittkower’s 
interpretation of Palladio as a Mannerist, and not the 
orthodox Classicist that Lord Burlington and his fol­
lowers made him out to be, that was a great revelation.
Palladio as a Mannerist is a notion that Venturi also 
uses in his first book, Complexity and Contradiction in Archi­
tecture. There, Venturi argues that architects have the right 
to look back at history and learn from it. At the time, in 
1966, this was a bold statement. The book appeared at a 
controversial moment, when architects were starting to react 
to the paradigms of ‘functionalism’ and ‘a-historicity’ predi­
cated by the modern movement, in order to return to history

## S. 185

185
as source for inspiration and references within the design 
process. Venturi’s wrote that ‘as an architect I try to be 
guided not by habit but by a conscious sense of the past’. 
This quote can be considered a key tendency of postmodern­
ism. It was by means of critical tools—such as literary criti­
cism—that the architect could deal with the past and bring it 
back to the present.
Venturi singles out particular periods in history that 
interest him, moments in which architecture was more com­
plex and contradictory. The book adopts Palladio’s architec­
ture, such as the Palazzo Valmarana, as a way to illustrate 
this. Michelangelo (1475–1564), almost contemporary to 
Palladio, is another hero of Venturi’s discussion. The niche 
of the Biblioteca Laurenziana (see chapter 8), Venturi says, 
shows an unprecedented freedom in reassembling architec­
tural elements in ways that have nothing to do with their 
original classical composition. This in turn generates an 
ambiguous composition, where nothing is what it appears. 
According to Venturi, the two paradigms of complexity 
and contradiction represent everything that was missing 
from the conventions of modernism, which through simple 
geometry and standardization had become an un-reflexive 
habit. Complexity and contradiction were intended as ways 
to express the architecture of the past, such as mannerism, 
baroque and rococo. 
7. Mannerism

## S. 186

186
CONCLUSION
Robert Venturi promoted the notions of complexity and 
contradiction in Palladio and Michelangelo’s architecture, by 
recognizing that they engaged with the meaning of architec­
tural elements within the syntax of the classical orders. 
Venturi argued that especially Michelangelo recognized and 
knew how these elements should be adopted, but willingly 
used them in entirely different ways. Therefore, the beholder 
can never fully understand and read how the syntax is func­
tioning and is therefore continuously surprised and confused. 
These sophisticated attitudes toward architectural composi­
tion were identified as typically Mannerist. They are espe­
cially visible in one of Michelangelo’s most important pro­
jects, the Biblioteca Laurenziana.

190
SOURCES

Giorgio Vasari, Le Vite de più Eccellenti Pittori, 
Architetti e Scultori Italiani, Firenze 1568 
(2nd ed.), vol. IX ‘Michelangelo’. https://
xenotheka.delbeke.arch.ethz.ch/vasari_lives_
of_the_most_eminent_painters_sculptors_
and_architects_vol_9/
Florence, Italy:
• 
Biblioteca Laurenziana;
• 
The church and monastery of San Lorenzo.

## S. 191

8. Michelangelo and San Lorenzo
INTRODUCTION 
One of the most important examples presented by 
Wittkower and Venturi to illustrate the meaning of ‘double 
functioning’ elements were, Venturi writes, ‘the pilasters of 
Michelangelo’s niches in the entrance of the Laurentian 
Library’ which ‘also look like brackets.’ In both the Bibliote­
ca Laurenziana and the New Sacristy in San Lorenzo, the 
architectural language can never be understood in a univocal 
way, but instead it evokes in the viewer a sense of destabili­
zation.

## S. 192

192
The main architectural features of the Biblioteca Laurenzi­
ana.
How Michelangelo displayed a sense of ambiguity in the 
vestibule of the Laurenziana.

## S. 193

8. Michelangelo and San Lorenzo
Michelangelo, Ricetto in the Biblioteca Laurenziana.

## S. 194

194
THE BIBLIOTECA LAURENZIANA
The Biblioteca Laurenziana, built from 1523 onwards, 
represents the early example of a new typology, the public 
library, which would later spread throughout Europe in the 
courts of important families. The Florentine Medici family 
commissioned Michelangelo Buonarroti (1475–1564), one of 
the most important figures of the late Renaissance and the 
entire history of architecture and art, to build a library to 
hold the family’s book collection and to make it accessible 
to the public. Schooled in Florence by the painter Ghirlan­
daio, Michelangelo became close to Lorenzo de’ Medici. 
During the 1490s and early 1500s he left, first for Venice and 
Bologna, then for Rome. The commission of the library 
brought him back to Florence, after which he would move 
again to Rome for good. 
The Laurenziana is part of the complex of San Lorenzo, 
a medieval building system comprising a church (refurbished 
by Brunelleschi) and a monastery, on top of which Michelan­
gelo built the library, making it accessible by a staircase in 
the courtyard. The layout of the library comprised a long 
reading hall and an entrance hall, or vestibule (ricetto) 
connected by a staircase, following typical features already 
‘invented’ in earlier examples, such as the San Marco library 
in Florence by Michelozzo. The focus of the project is the 
interior, where the entire spectacle of the library is displayed. 
Outside, the building is in fact quite uninteresting. The 
interior is also where all the technical and design problems 
emerged. The structure of the library had to weigh as little 
as possible, so as to not put too much strain on the build­
ing underneath; it had to be solid enough so as to protect

## S. 195

195
8. Michelangelo and San Lorenzo
the books, especially from fire; and it had to provide spatial 
autonomy from the church and monastery.
Michelangelo’s interest in playing with the conventional 
hierarchy between columns, walls, and openings (i.e. where 
columns or pilasters stand in front of or frame walls, which 
in their turn contain openings) is visible in both the reading 
room and the vestibule. In the reading room, a sequence of 
pilasters encloses set-back walls. The walls are developed as 
surfaces framing the windows, almost as to emphasize the 
thin immateriality of the walls between the pilasters. At the 
same time, the window frame is pushed out to exactly the 
same level as the pilasters, giving the impression of a pro­
gressively hollowed surface, and creating a sequence of 
frames that emphasize the interior rhythm. In a way, this is 
also repeated on the ceiling and is even supported by the 
rhythm of the reading benches, which are aligned with the 
architectural structure. The windows are lowered to just 
above the benches, so as to catch as much daylight as 
possible. In fact, the level of the reading room is determined 
by the extent to which the windows could be lowered on the 
exterior. To ensure this result, Michelangelo altered the 
element of the wall, which, to be kept thin, underwent a 
process of articulated profiling.
The Vestibule
This process of profiling is particularly evident in the 
vestibule, or ricetto, where the lower basis is built on walls 
and surmounted by columns set between the walls, rather 
than in front of them. The masses of the walls between the

## S. 196

196
columns are then articulated by niches. In a sequence, the 
console, typical of Michelangelo, sits beneath the columns, 
while the one across the architrave leads to the upper pilas­
ters. Michelangelo introduces a vertical order, running 
through the entire organism except in the corners, where the 
wall surfaces meet, and the vertical articulation enters into a 
conflict.
Due to the pre-existing loggia located around the exter­
nal perimeter, the ricetto features two orders of lowered 
windows, of which the lower sequence is blind. This interplay 
between the masses of the wall is then emphasized by the 
vertical alignment of elements, particularly in the vestibule 
but also in the reading room. The overall effect is that of an 
inward-looking façade, evidencing that there is no main 
representational façade and that the vestibule takes center 
stage for the entire work. By reversing such architectural 
code, Michelangelo presents an exterior façade built in an 
interior space, as a sequence of four façades. This then also 
impacts the space between the consoles in the corners, which 
almost disappears. 
The tectonics of the space of the vestibule is therefore 
the object of an intricate play with expectations: inside/
outside, façade/interior, representation/service, structure/
decoration, vertical/horizontal layout. In 1934 Rudolf 
Wittkower, a German art historian specialized in Italian 
Renaissance and Baroque, published an important essay on 
the Laurenziana and on the distinction between Baroque and 
Mannerism. In it, he wrote:

## S. 197

197
8. Michelangelo and San Lorenzo
The task proper to the orders is to bear burdens and to 
articulate wall surfaces. The orders thus generally stand 
in relief before the wall; but here the wall stands before 
the orders. A correct feeling for the reversal which has 
here taken place has led to the description of the walls 
as expanding into the room. The oppressive sensation 
of an overwhelming mass plays the main part in this in­
terpretation. Essential, however, for a real understand­
ing of this architecture is the fact that the usual form 
of wall and covering orders is simply reversed. The ob­
server is plunged, unconsciously, into a remarkable sit­
uation of doubt and obscurity. He perceives, on the one 
hand, that walls and orders have exchanged function, 
reacts immediately, however, to the instinctive consid­
eration that this is impossible. The whole arrangement 
of the wall articulation thus contains a conflict to the 
solution of which the architecture provides no clue.
Wittkower noticed how difficult it is for the viewer to 
come to a coherent and conclusive reading of the Biblioteca 
Laurenziana, because of its ever-present contradictions that 
destabilize and challenge the viewer’s interpretation. 
Later in the article, Wittkover also introduced the idea of 
the ‘double function’ of architecture. According to this prin­
ciple, each architectural element can be ‘read’ in more than 
one way, so that it is impossible to arrive to a single, coher­
ent interpretation of the architectural whole. Every reading is 
destabilized by a possible alternative. and each of these 
interpretations lead to a different understanding of architec­
ture itself. Unsurprisingly, Venturi was particularly attracted 
by these ‘double functioning elements’ as generators of 
complexity and contradiction (see chapter 7). This process

## S. 198

198
argued for a more internal way of looking at architecture, 
which was seen as a formal problem explored by a syntax of 
forms.
In the Laurenziana there is no sequence of elements, 
such as base, shaft, capital, and architrave, but rather a 
sequence of highly detailed profiles. This is evidenced by 
Michelangelo’s drawings of cornices, which suggest an inter­
est in the expressive potential of these profiles, in which 
elements are isolated and elaborated. This creative use of 
profiling then feeds into the decorative system of both vesti­
bule and reading room where, for example, the cornices on 
top of the pilasters are ‘edited’ and are missing the frieze.
In turn, the staircase is also not a very practical solu­
tion, but rather a composition of three staircases bound 
together. This three-flight staircase plays an important role 
in the scenography of the space. Thanks to the interplay 
between the central ramp and the distinguished sides framed 
by a low step-like balustrade, Michelangelo emphasizes a 
bodily movement through the vestibule, bringing the viewer 
to fully experience the architectural work. Here again, Mi­
chelangelo plays with our expectations. The main staircase 
is almost cascading downward, inviting downward movement 
rather than upward. Therefore, climbing happens on the 
sides. Wittkower explains: 
Analysis of the stair leads to no other result. In the 
central flight the sense of the whole stair is reversed. 
The steps fall in a cascade from the library door; the 
two side flights climb upwards. In the meeting of the 
three flights the two tendencies clash. Following a

## S. 199

199
8. Michelangelo and San Lorenzo
side flight upwards, at the place where it unites with 
the centre one has the sense of being thrown down 
again. Following the central flight down- wards, the 
movement is contradicted by the upward movement 
of the sides. There is no solution: one is forever torn 
between the upward and the downward tendencies.
MICHELANGELO, VASARI, AND THE NEW SACRESTY
As discussed previously, the term Mannerism was coined 
in the early 20th century. However, the idea that Michelange­
lo was operating in an exceptional way, taking liberties and 
deliberately playing with the canons of classicism, was al­
ready understood during the 16th century. A very important 
voice in this process of recognition was Giorgio Vasari, a 
Florentine painter and writer. He was the author of one of 
the most important projects of European art history, a 
compilation of biographies of artists, entitled The Lives of 
the Most Excellent Painters, Sculptors and Architects (Le 
Vite de’ più Eccellenti Pittori, Scultori e Architettori). The 
first edition was published in 1552, the second in 1568, four 
years after Michelangelo’s death. The purpose of Vasari’s 
Lives was to present a history of art, which makes him one 
of the first art historians in Europe. This history was built 
through the lives of its practitioners, which is a very modern 
operation still adopted today. Vasari had two purposes:
1. Prove that Florence was the place where art first 
flourished in the late 15th century;
2. Demonstrate that art can evolve and be perfected, an 
idea already present in Raphael’s letter to Leo X.

## S. 200

200
Vasari takes these premises even further, arguing that 
the arts are able to surpass the models of Antiquity, all 
thanks to Michelangelo. In fact, after Michelangelo’s death, 
Vasari edited an expanded the second edition of the book, 
further emphasizing Michelangelo’s importance. Vasari 
argued that Michelangelo was able to invent and create a 
new and original language. About the Biblioteca Laurenzia­
na, Vasari writes that:
[Michelangelo] displayed this technique even more ef­
fectively and sought to make it known in the Library 
of San Lorenzo in the same location: that is, in the 
beautiful divisions of the windows and the ceiling, and 
the marvelous entrance hall. Never before had there 
been seen such resolute grace in the whole as well as 
in the parts, as in the corbels, tabernacles, and cor­
nices, nor was there ever a more commodious stair­
case: in it, he executed so many unusual breaks in 
the steps and departed so far from the usual custom 
in other details that everyone was astonished by it.
The difference between Raphael and Vasari, then, is that 
whereas the former associated with his own time an asymp­
totic tension toward the perfection of the ancients (see chap­
ter 3), Vasari argued that this level had been surpassed. A 
new perfection was achieved thanks to the originality and 
creativity of Michelangelo, who adopted ‘licenses’ (licenze), 
a freedom to go beyond what had already been done.
In the Sacrestia Nuova, the New Sacristy (1520–1526), 
the burial chapel for the Medici family in San Lorenzo, 
Michelangelo worked with the same inventiveness he dis­
played in the Laurenziana. Here as well, he disorients the

## S. 201

201
viewer with the free use of parts, such as cornice-like ele­
ments adopted to support the columns, instead of setting 
them on top of it. In doing so, the profile is again enhanced 
to a higher degree of expressiveness. In the New Sacristy, 
Michelangelo drew directly from the constructive vocabulary 
of Filippo Brunelleschi, who refurbished the church and 
authored the Old Sacristy, by proposing a white body articu­
lated by means of grey sandstone, in which white marble 
niches are added (see chapter 2). Michelangelo took the 
anatomy of the Old Sacristy and challenged the system 
established by Brunelleschi, which was already finely ambig­
uous. 
On the one hand, Michelangelo questioned and resolved 
Brunelleschi’s ambiguity in the way he positioned the pilas­
ter in a state of tension with the wall and in relation to the 
corners. On the other hand, Michelangelo clearly marked the 
central space of the Sacristy by creating a separate space for 
the niche and the altar, which appears to emerge from be­
hind the wall. Here, the beholder finds difficulty in reading 
the planes of the niche and the depth of the walls, and in 
relating them to the space of the chapel. The space is en­
tirely composed by the pressure of architectural elements, 
emphasizing the verticality and the height of the room. The 
doorframe ends on a tablet, that does not have a capital, 
but only a sort of volute in support of the pilasters. These 
are in fact not real pilasters, since they are embedded in the 
wall and seem to support a broken pediment. These are 
actually referring to Brunelleschi’s architecture, for example 
in the frieze running downward in the Ospedale degli Inno­
centi. When Vasari writes about the New Sacristy, he again 
argues for Michelangelo’s originality. He finds that:
8. Michelangelo and San Lorenzo

## S. 202

202
since Michelangelo wanted to execute the project in im­
itation of the Old Sacristy done by Filippo Brunelleschi 
but with a different order of decorations, created inside 
a composite decoration, more varied and original than 
ancient or modern masters had for some time been able 
to achieve, for in the originality of its beautiful cornices, 
capitals, bases, doors, tabernacles, and tombs, Michel­
angelo departed in a significant way from the measures, 
orders, and rules men usually employ, following Vitruvi­
us and the ancients, because he did not wish to repeat 
them. His license has greatly encouraged those who have 
seen his way of working in order to set about imitating it.
Michelangelo did not want to follow Vitruvius or the 
ancients, and therefore did something entirely new. Vasari 
uses the concept of ‘imitation’, arguing that Michelangelo 
wanted to follow Brunelleschi. At the same time, his New 
Sacristy looks very different from the Old, hence establishing 
a new conception of the principle of imitation (sec chapter 
7). Interestingly, Vasari also notes how those who tried to 
follow him only achieved mediocre results. Vasari writes that 
in imitating Michelangelo:
Their fantasies were subsequently seen to exhibit more of 
the grotesque than reason or rules in their decorations. 
Thus, artisans owe an immense and everlasting debt 
to Michelangelo, since he broke the bonds and chains 
that made them all continue to follow a common path.

## S. 203

203
CONCLUSION
In San Lorenzo, Michelangelo an ability to manipulate 
the classical canon in new, sophisticated and unexpected 
ways. This creative approach towards the principle of imita­
tion was identified by Vasari as an unprecedented and great­
ly successful contribution to the history of architecture and 
art and remained an unsurpassed reference for 20th-century 
architects like Venturi.

## S. 207

9. The Santa Casa of Loreto and its Copies
INTRODUCTION 
The conflict between Catholicism and Protestantism in 
the political circumstances of the 16th and 17th centuries were 
also reflected in architecture. The case of the Santa Casa in 
Loreto is less about design, some of which began in the 15th 
century and was altered in the 16th and 17th centuries, thus 
crossing over from Gothic to Renaissance, than about how 
architecture conveys cultural meaning and participates in 
political and religious events. The shrine, which was intend­
ed for devotional use, was directly related to the religious 
and political tensions of the time. This relationship was 
reflected in the ways in which the Santa Casa was represent­
ed and adopted in the religious and political contexts of 16th 
century Europe.

## S. 208

208
History of Loreto, the meaning of the Santa Casa for Chris­
tianity and for the political power of the Church.
Evolution of the compound of Loreto.
The shrine of Loreto, the space and elements that charac­
terize it.
The meaning of the body of the Virgin Mary as prototype 
for the church.
Replicas of the Santa Casa and meanings in relation to 
pilgrimage and Reformation/Counterreformation dispute.
How the Hergiswald in Switzerland is a replica of the Santa 
Casa.
TO KNOW

## S. 209

209
9. The Santa Casa of Loreto and its Copies
Plan of the Santa Casa with the marble covering.

## S. 210

210
ARCHITECTURE AS POLITICAL PROJECT: THE SHRINE 
OF LORETO
In 1507 Pope Julius II summoned Bramante, who was 
already working for him on St. Peter’s in Rome, to Loreto, a 
small city on the Adriatic coast of Italy founded on a hill as 
a fortified medieval town. There, Bramante and Julius II 
developed a large project for the Apostolic Palace, a build­
ing to host the pope that echoed ancient examples, such as 
the Forum of Julius Caesar (Julius II actually modeled his 
own name and image on the emperor).
The defense system proposed for the Apostolic Palace of 
Loreto must be placed in the context of the expansion of the 
Ottoman Empire, which from the mid-14th century until the 
late 17th century covered a range equal in size to the Roman 
Empire. In the 16th century, the Ottoman Empire spread 
across the Balkans to eventually face the city of Loreto 
across the Adriatic Sea, turning the Mediterranean into a 
stage of permanent conflict.
The plan, which was never completed in its entirety, was 
to build not just a papal palace, but a fortified compound, 
framed by four monumental towers on the long side, sup­
ported by smaller towers, and accessible by a single entrance 
through a bridge. The palace was to include a set of arcades 
encompassing a square and ending in a newly designed 
façade for the existing church. The ensemble was also to 
include a forum with loggias, to accommodate the pope and 
his visitors. The unifying focal point was the church, already 
present on the site, dedicated to the Virgin Mary or, more 
precisely, to her house. This was the shelter where Mary was

## S. 211

211
supposed to have lived in Nazareth and where she received 
the annunciation conceived the Christ Child. It was an ex­
tremely important object of significant religious veneration.
According to Catholic tradition, Mary’s cottage was said 
to have been miraculously transported by angels from Naza­
reth to the Adriatic coast of Italy after the Saracens con­
quered the Holy Land in the 13th century. The cottage first 
landed in Trsat, Croatia, and from there moved to two other 
locations before finally arriving in Loreto. All this movement 
was propelled by the Virgin’s dissatisfaction with the piety 
displayed in the former locations. This legend was first 
recorded at the end of 15th century, but very quickly became 
part of the Catholic tradition over the course of the 16th 
century. 
When Bramante laid out his project, a key impetus was 
to protect the most important structure in Loreto from the 
threat of the Ottoman expansion: Mary’s holy house, a 
small cottage, often referred to as a relic, itself housed 
within the 15th-century church of the Apostolic Palace. It was 
called the Holy House, ‘Santa Casa’, hence the name of the 
church containing the relic within. 
A shrine, designed to encase the cottage within the 
church, was begun in 1513, probably after a design by Bra­
mante from 1509. After Bramante’s death, it was continued 
by Andrea Sansovino and completed around 1538. The 
shrine, essentially a white box built in Carrara marble, could 
be described a building in itself, given its structure and 
refined decoration. The richly decorated base supports Co­
rinthian columns crowned by an elaborate Corinthian archi­
9. The Santa Casa of Loreto and its Copies

## S. 212

212
trave with a frieze mosaic of a meander pattern (modeled 
after the Ara Pacis in Rome) and finished by a balustrade 
on top. The columns span two orders of niches filled with 
sculpted high relief sculptures. A strip of dark marble 
around the bottom of the base gives the impression that the 
whole shrine is lifted above the floor and therefore does not 
interfere with whatever is inside.
Within this shrine is the Santa Casa, the Holy House of 
Mary, a small brick structure, much humbler, simpler and 
older than the marble shrine Bramante designed to house it. 
The thin interior wall of the house presents in great detail 
the supposedly original humble abode of the Virgin Mary, 
including a small altar and paintings on the brick walls. The 
openings, three doors and one window, are the only points of 
correspondence between the interior wall of the house and 
the external one of the shrine.
When this grand marble shell around the Santa Casa was 
erected, Loreto was already the foremost Marian pilgrimage 
site in Europe, probably deliberately placed away from cities, 
on a road easily reachable by pilgrims. The phenomenon of 
pilgrimage, which is not just Christian Catholic, consisted of 
traveling some distance for a variety of reasons, primarily 
devotional (such as health or atonement) but also commer­
cial. For example, the Santa Casa in Loreto became impor­
tant for female monarchs and queens, who traveled to it on 
a pilgrimage to ask for an heir through holy intercession, for 
which they brought expensive gifts.

## S. 213

213
The Architecture of the Church of Loreto
While the relic of the Santa Casa had long been managed 
by the local clergy, during the 15th century the papacy gave 
it more attention, which attests to the political and religious 
importance of the relic. Under Julius II, in 1507 the sanctu­
ary became subordinate to the Holy See, after an initial 
failed attempt by Sixtus IV.
Before the interventions of Bramante, the Santa Casa was 
initially surrounded by an octagonal portico, connected by 
walls and with four towers around the perimeter of the 
house, recalling the image of a fortress. It was probably 
through building these four towers that the foundation for 
the sacristy of the church in which the relic resided was 
established. Construction of the church probably began in 
1471 and was completed by the end of the 16th century. The 
plan originated from a combination of centralized and longi­
tudinal volumes. The apse is divided into three smaller 
apses, creating an organic connection with the overall struc­
ture and the nave in particular. This layout is particularly 
suited for liturgy, as well as for the pilgrims to circulate 
around the Santa Casa, enclosed in its shrine. The shrine is 
given prominence within the central space by the three aps­
es. The design for the dome, by Giuliano Da Sangallo, was 
probably based on a preexisting drum and caused a number 
of technical problems. Sangallo’s studies again lead to an 
architecture of imitation, specifically to the dome of Santa 
Maria del Fiore by Brunelleschi, in Florence, one of the most 
important Marian churches in Italy. 
9. The Santa Casa of Loreto and its Copies

## S. 214

214
The Body of Mary as Prototype for the Church
The Santa Casa was at once relic and reliquary, both the 
physical remains of the place where the history of salvation 
had materialized and Catholic truth had been manifested, 
and also an object containing that sacred event. Given the 
belief that a fundamental mystery had taken place in the 
Santa Casa, the building could be considered the holiest of 
Christian places, and a prototype for every church of Christi­
anity. 
The veneration of Our Lady of Loreto, which is some­
times called the cult of the Holy House, centered on the 
belief that the Holy House was where the Annunciation had 
taken place. The miracle of the Annunciation constitutes the 
epitome of purity and chastity, but more importantly, the 
Holy House, the Santa Casa, represented the historical place 
where the word became flesh (from the Gospel of John 1:14). 
This belief had a whole series of theological implications, 
not least because it embodied an anti-Protestant argument 
of material proof for the truth of Catholicism (that is, 
Mary’s role in salvation). 
With the Santa Casa enclosed in its shrine, the meaning 
of the object intertwines with what is expressed by architec­
ture, which in turn becomes a symbol for divine mystery. 
That is to say, the Santa Casa is hidden within a protective 
architecture, therefore establishing an analogy between the 
Santa Casa and the body of the Virgin Mary as a sort of 
shell-enclosed purity. The relationship between the architec­
ture of the shrine and its constituent elements is displayed 
in the reliefs decorating the shrine, accentuating a condition

## S. 215

215
of transition between outside and inside and activating the 
analogy between architecture and the body of the Virgin. In 
other words, by entering the mystery of the Santa Casa 
through the shrine, one can access the mystery the Virgin 
Mary.
The figures in the lower niches of the shrine are all 
prophets from the Old Testament, who predicted the Annun­
ciation in prophecies about the arrival of Christ. In the 
upper level are the sibyls, female prophets of ancient Rome. 
This paring was not uncommon in the 16th century, as even 
the prophets of the Roman religion were presented as an­
nouncers of what had been foretold in the Old Testament, 
namely the birth of Christ (again, ancient history is merged 
with Christian history).
The analogy between the body of the Virgin Mary and 
the Holy House is made explicit in the shrine by a relief of 
the Annunciation by Andrea Sansovino. In the relief, the Vir­
gin appears in a section of the house and a beam of divine 
grace penetrates her body. Just as the image of the Virgin is 
understood as a gate to heaven, so one then has to physi­
cally enter the shrine in order to uncover the mystery of 
faith. Also, the arrangement of the church space surrounding 
the house of the Virgin, designed ‘around’ the shrine and 
only accessible through the church, is meant to suggests 
that Loreto was in fact the original location of the house. 
This analogy can also be applied to the Apostolic Palace 
and city of Loreto, as there is only one way to enter the city, 
like in a fortress.
9. The Santa Casa of Loreto and its Copies

## S. 216

216
The Virgin Mary, Stronghold of Religion
The image of the fortress can also be extended to the 
Virgin Mary, a stronghold for religion and as a place of 
refuge. This association became particularly important in 
the 16th century, on two fronts. Firstly, people turned to the 
Virgin for protection against the Ottoman Turks in the 
Mediterranean wars (e.g. the battle of Lepanto of 1573). 
Secondly, Catholicism at this moment was also challenged 
by the Protestant Reformation. 
The Catholic Church was slow to respond to the emer­
gence of Protestantism, which began in the first quarter of 
the 16th century. The Protestants identified three major areas 
of contention in the Catholic Church: the veneration of 
saints, such as Mary; the way images were used, which 
according to the Protestants was idolatrous; and the func­
tion of relics. Between 1545 and 1563, the Council of Trent 
formulated a series of institutional reforms and promoted 
better practices for Catholics, many of which were not imple­
mented until well into the 17th century. The Council of Trent 
specifically addressed the three criticisms moved by the Prot­
estants by affirming Catholic traditions in the face of this 
contestation, outlining how these three means of devotion 
could actually help believers establish contact with the 
divine. Catholic propaganda also came into action, conflat­
ing the Protestants with the Turks as enemies of the one 
true faith. In this way, the ‘modern’ enemies of the Church of 
Rome were compared to the heretics who perpetrated early 
Christian persecutions and the martyrdom of Catholics (see 
chapter 10).

## S. 217

217
Bramante’s project for Loreto can then be read as a 
significant political move to claim territory and bring the 
entire land under specific spheres of religious influence. On 
one hand, the shrine of Loreto was meant to constitute a 
religious stronghold to expand and reestablish Catholic pow­
er in reaction to the Reformation. On the other hand, the 
fortified compound of the Apostolic Palace was meant to 
physically defend the coast from the Saracens, establishing 
an important military presence on the Adriatic sea.
Replicas of the Holy House of Loreto
The veneration of the Holy House of Loreto led to a 
mediatic phenomenon with important implications for coun­
tries far beyond Italy. During the 17th and early 18th centu­
ries, the Holy House of Loreto became mobile again—not its 
physical form carried by angels, but in the reconstruction of 
its form through copies. Prominent visitors went to Loreto 
on pilgrimage, obtained grace from the Virgin Mary, and as 
an act of thanksgiving built a new holy house upon their 
return home. Founding a replica of the Loreto relic therefore 
became an act of performance that constituted a similar 
degree of piety as in the original sanctuary. 
Not only could the structure sanctify any place it was 
built in, but by visiting it, a visitor could experience the holi­
ness of the place as if it were the original. The mobility and 
formal replicability of the Holy House of Loreto imbued it 
with the unique capacity to ‘sanctify’ geographical space on 
various levels: 
9. The Santa Casa of Loreto and its Copies

## S. 218

218
1. By visiting a copy of the Santa Casa, one could avoid 
long pilgrimages to the original place of grace.
2. The copy would constitute a powerful symbolic link 
between the local Catholic communities and Italian 
Catholicism.
3. Copying the relic could be a lucrative endeavor be­
cause of a new sale of indulgences 
Because the Holy House was the archetypical church 
building (the first church ever, allegedly consecrated by St. 
Peter himself), the visitor to the ‘new’ chapel would have a 
similar experience as a pilgrim to Loreto. This concept is 
well represented by the 17th-century Atlas Marianus, a sort 
of travel guide illustrating to the most important statues of 
the Virgin that existed at the time. The frontispiece of the 
book depicts the Santa Casa hovering over earth and pro­
jecting beams of light on specific places in Europe, suggest­
ing an act of replication. The idea of the architectural copy 
is part of a long (medieval) tradition of devotional 
draughtsmanship in the context of pilgrimages, based on the 
notion that ‘sacred measurements’ ought in themselves to be 
considered relics, and were endowed with the same powers as 
the original model. 
The Swiss Holy House
The Swiss phenomenon of copying the Loreto house is a 
further illustration of how architecture embodies meaning 
and represents the concerns of the time. During the 16th and 
17th centuries, the territories of Southern Germany remained 
mostly Catholic, but were in a state of permanent conflict

## S. 219

219
between Catholic and Protestant factions. The act of copy­
ing the Holy House of Loreto and the shrine, even with 
fragmentary reproductions of its internal arrangements, 
represented a physical way of religiously occupying a territo­
ry. It provided Catholics with a powerful physical manifesta­
tion of the most geographically relevant of relics, therefore 
claiming entire regions by holy intercession.
Making a copy was based on the possibility of reproduc­
ing a sacred object by selecting certain attributes of that 
object. For the Santa Casa, these particular attributes in­
volved not just the virtues of Mary, but also its measure­
ments. These attributes and measurements were reproduced 
in the 17th century church of Hergiswald, near Lucerne, one 
of the earliest examples north of the Alps. 
The church comprises a nave without side aisles and 
crossed by a transept. The building looks like an average 
Swiss countryside church, with plain, undecorated white­
washed walls, tall lancet windows, and a pointed roof, with a 
small turret on the crossing. The church of Hergiswald 
began as a small chapel for the use of a hermit who lived in 
the area. It was then expanded in 1620, first becoming a 
Marian chapel, then the Loreto house was erected at the end 
of the nave. A second chapel was built after that, and even­
tually the church was completely reoriented in a centralized 
space and completed in 1648/49. On the outer wall of Her­
giswald’s Loreto house hangs a large painting by Johann 
Dietterlin from 1652, depicting and explaining the ‘transla­
tio’, the transportation of the Loreto Chapel to Lucerne, and 
therefore setting the (local) importance for the veneration of 
the house.
9. The Santa Casa of Loreto and its Copies

## S. 220

220
The Marian cult was established in Switzerland thanks to 
this church, which became an important place of pilgrimage, 
and as such was soon too small. In the 1660s the church 
was then further expanded and covered with a new roof. The 
new ceiling, painted by swiss painter Kaspar Meglinger, 
consists of a very rich and colorful grid of 324 panels, cover­
ing the ceiling like a gigantic canopy. The panels are a 
collection of emblems about the Virgin, essentially ‘explain­
ing’ the Virgin’s attributes.
The Ceiling ‘Emblematics’
During the early modern period symbolism occupied an 
important place in visual representation, and one way to 
express such symbolism was through ‘emblematics’. This 
was a strategy of visual communication made of an ingen­
ious interplay of word and image, challenging the viewer to 
discover and derive hidden meanings, and requiring an active 
engagement on the part of the observer. The ceiling at 
Hergiswald is one of the largest and most diverse emblemat­
ic cycles in the world, presented in a consistent pattern of 
rectangular blue framed panels studded with gilded stars.
The whole series unfolds from the panel on Mary’s As­
sumption—her ascent to Heaven—, located on the crossing 
above the high altar. This is the only panel with a sort of 
narrative, the only with space, landscape, and human fig­
ures. Members of the sisterhood of Lucerne stand in prayer 
around the empty grave, while musician angels accompany 
Mary on her upward journey. The shape of the empty tomb 
forms the blueprint for all the other panels.

## S. 221

221
The other panels follow the emblematic guiding principle 
of conciseness (brevitas). They all have a uniform white 
background and contain almost no indication of space or 
human figures. The themes range from the microcosmic to 
the macrocosmic and include naturalia (objects from the 
natural world like animals, starts and trees), and artificialia 
(objects of human artifice, like weapons, clothes). Each 
emblem is a composition of a small image and a short piece 
of text and refers to one aspect of Mary and of her role in 
Salvation, glorifying her distinctions and virtues. There is no 
order or hierarchy of content, no beginning and no designat­
ed end.
With adoption of these emblems as decorative elements, 
the ceiling produces the overall impression of a combination 
of sobriety and simplicity, but also copiousness (copia) and 
variety (varietas), the latter being identifiable as prototypi­
cal Baroque qualities. 
9. The Santa Casa of Loreto and its Copies

## S. 222

222
CONCLUSION
The processes of codification and mediatization of the 
Holy House of Loreto is evidence of the deep interconnection 
between architecture and political and religious phenomena. 
The dissemination of the relic through reproductions also 
testifies the significance of architecture as bearer of mean­
ing, rather than just tectonic system of physical elements.

223
9. The Santa Casa of Loreto and its Copies

## S. 227

10. The Colosseum
INTRODUCTION 
The Colosseum, one of the best-preserved monuments of 
Antiquity, has always stimulated a great deal of interest 
among architects and writers, charged as it is with a succes­
sion of often competing meanings. When writing about his 
trip to Rome in 1786–88, the German writer Johann Wolf­
gang von Goethe refers to particular architectural elements 
of the Colosseum that appeared especially striking when 
seen at night—‘the great masses of light and shadow’ and 
‘the enormous and yet educated masses’. Adopted for the 
purposes of Catholic propaganda, the Colosseum continued 
to maintain a sense of massive monumentality deeply asso­
ciated with the Roman empire. As a result, the meaning of 
the building and its reception often diverged.

## S. 228

228
The meaning of the reception of Antiquity as exemplified 
by the Colosseum.

The situation of the city of Rome in relation to the Colosse­
um.
Uses of the Colosseum.
Means used to turn the Colosseum into a place representing 
Christianity.
