10. The Colosseum
Carlo Fontana: L‘Anfiteatro Flavio, 1725.

## S. 230

230
RECEPTION AND REPRESENTATION
The Flavian Amphitheatre (Anfiteatro Flavio), also 
known as the Colosseum, is an oval structure built in Rome 
between 72 and 80 A.D. to hold public spectacles, such as 
gladiator fights, executions and reenactments of famous bat­
tles. The vast seating around the arena, organized on six 
successive levels, could hold up to 80,000 spectators. The 
exterior of the building was composed by four parts: three 
stories of superimposed arcades decorated with semi-col­
umns of Doric, Ionic and Corinthian orders, and a large 
attic (Attika) on top.
Not only was the Colosseum important as a building of 
Antiquity, but also constitutes a useful case for understand­
ing the reception of Antiquity in the Renaissance. The histo­
ry of the Colosseum and its relevance for Renaissance and 
later architecture are two intricately related stories which 
together shaped different interpretations of its construction.
The feature of most immediate importance in the Colos­
seum as a Roman ruin is its entirely preserved north side, a 
degree preservation that is rare in any ancient building. The 
main reason for such preservation is the way the Colosseum 
was built, entirely of travertine, a type of limestone, rather 
than the far less durable combination of brick and cladding, 
which characterizes most of Roman buildings of the Imperial 
period. 
In the Renaissance, the Colosseum became so important 
that is was idealized, becoming part of the architectural can­
on. Such a well-preserved building from Antiquity was of

## S. 231

231
10. The Colosseum
particular interest for Renaissance artists and intellectuals, 
who depicted and studied it in treatises and other writings. 
A significant example of how the Colosseum was added to 
the architectural vocabulary of the Renaissance is the work 
of Giuliano da Sangallo. Around 1500, Sangallo transformed 
the Colosseum through a series of drawings, turning its 
elliptical shape into a regular circle. He also changed the 
proportions of the Colosseum’s attic. Sangallo then project­
ed his own ideas by interpreting the building as a new form, 
reflecting an idealized and perfected vision of Antiquity.
A more faithful representation of the building came forty 
years later at the hands of Sebastiano Serlio. In his third 
book on architecture, Serlio published the Colosseum’s 
elevation together with some of its elements and details. It 
was not only the high level of preservation of the Colosseum 
that attracted architects. During the Renaissance, the typical 
building associated with Greek or Roman antiquity was the 
temple, but architects needed to find different references for 
new typologies that emerged through the evolution of cities. 
The Colosseum became an important model for the design 
of non-religious architecture, and especially the palazzo.
PALAZZO RUCELLAI
One of the earliest and most renowned examples of the 
reuse of the Colosseum is on the facade of the Palazzo 
Rucellai in Florence, designed in the mid-15th century by 
Leon Battista Alberti. Because the Colosseum was associat­
ed with notions of beauty and morality (perfect proportions, 
the site of martyrdoms…), buildings that referred to it con­

## S. 232

232
veyed particular meanings, according to their type. Wealthy 
merchants and noble families such as the Rucellai often 
expressed their prestige through the façades of their palaces, 
presenting themselves as arbiters of civil conventions and 
taste, at the same time enriching the experience of the city 
in which such facades appeared.
In the Palazzo Rucellai, Alberti directly refers to the 
Colosseum with the succession of storeys articulated on the 
façade, each storey presenting one of the three orders, a 
same tri-partite arrangement that appears on the Colosse­
um. However, he transformed the half-columns of the Colos­
seum into pilasters, laid flat against a rusticated wall. This 
motif of an arcade flanked by columns superimposed one on 
top of the other became a very well-known compositional 
strategy, known as superimposition or theater motif. 
The façade of the Palazzo Rucellai, where the three 
orders are collected in one single building, turned the Colos­
seum into a model and the Palazzo an intellectual allusion 
to it. Such a delicately ordered façade, referring to such a 
widely known monument as the Colosseum, reflected the 
status of its inhabitants. Alberti was very familiar with the 
superimposition of the Doric, Ionic, and Corinthian orders in 
the Colosseum, having actually visited the Colosseum during 
his stay in Rome in the 1430s. The knowledge of the ar­
rangement of the orders in this way, however, was also 
accessible to people living far north of the Alps, who never 
visited Rome, manly through Serlio’s treatise (see chapter 
4). Alberti, who was primarily an intellectual rather than an 
architect, wrote and translated Latin, and himself contribut­

## S. 233

233
10. The Colosseum
ed to the diffusion of the Colosseum as a model through the 
publication of De Re Aedificatoria (see chapter 2).
EVOLUTION AND DISSEMINATION OF A MODEL
The superimposition of orders, which in the 15th century 
was a two-dimensional, almost graphic transposition of the 
Colosseum onto a facade, developed over the centuries into 
a more tectonic articulation of the façade. One example is 
the cloister of Santa Maria della Pace, one of the early 
works of Bramante, in which the theater was adopted 
through the superimposition of Ionic and Corinthian orders 
(see chapter 2).
Other examples of the application of the model of the 
Colosseum’s façade are the courtyard of Palazzo Farnese in 
Rome, begun in 1517 by Antonio da Sangallo, later altered 
by Michelangelo, and the Town Hall in Zürich built in the 
late 17th century. The latter example also demonstrates the 
international dissemination of the theater motif, acquired 
not only directly by travelling to Rome but also actively 
discovered through treatises.
The Basilica Palladiana and the Loggia del Capitaniato, 
both designed by Andrea Palladio on opposite sides of 
Piazza dei Signori in Vicenza, are two further examples of 
the reception of the Colosseum as a model for façade design. 
For the Basilica, Palladio covered an existing market hall 
with a new perimetral loggia. The façade is divided into two 
levels, Doric below and Ionic above, each in a repeated 
succession of columns and arcades with flanking columns.

## S. 234

234
The motif seen here, where an arcade rests on small col­
umns and is flanked by two trabeated spans, is called Serlia­
na or Palladiana, references to its first use by either Serlio 
or Palladio. The Corinthian order can be found in the Palaz­
zo del Capitaniato. 
THE COLOSSEUM AS A NEW SITE OF CHRISTIANITY
 
The intellectual, critical, or creative reception of the 
Colosseum did not move in unison with what actually hap­
pened to the building itself. The Colosseum was plundered 
several times in its history. When the Roman Empire col­
lapsed, the city shrank and became fragmented until the rise 
of the papacy during the 14th and 15th centuries, when it 
again became a center of power. During the height of papal 
power, Rome’s urban fabric was laid out in the so-called 
Nolli map, designed in 1748. Named after Giambattista 
Nolli, the map renders Rome very precisely, treating it as a 
black surface and leaving the streets, open spaces, and the 
interiors of churches in white—in other words, Nolli wanted 
to reveal what he understood as public spaces.
With this strategy of representation, the Nolli map clearly 
expresses the state of the Colosseum between the 17th and 
18th centuries. On Nolli’s map, the Colosseum is situated 
between Santa Maria Maggiore and San Giovanni in Latera­
no, which for more than a thousand years was the seat of 
the bishop of Rome, the pope. Despite this spatial relation­
ship to two of the most important churches of 16th-century 
Rome, the Colosseum stood as an autonomous structure 
and without establishing real connections with its surround­

## S. 235

235
10. The Colosseum
ings. At the time of the Empire, Rome had about one million 
inhabitants, more than any other European city, and was 
enclosed by walls. During the Middle Ages, the city was 
dramatically reduced to about 10,000 citizens. The city walls 
continued to be understood as the legal boundaries of Rome, 
separating what was referred to as habitato, or inhabited 
space, from dishabitato, or uninhabited space. Although 
these two areas each had a very different status, because of 
the growing interests of the Catholic Church in the city as a 
whole, both areas were considered part of Rome in Nolli’s 
map. 
From its solitary location in the shrunken city of Rome, 
the Colosseum soon found a new purpose. In fact, over the 
centuries it had not only been plundered, but had also been 
protected or ‘claimed’ many times and in different ways. By 
the end of the 14th century the Colosseum was owned by the 
Salvator Brotherhood, a group of clerics centered at the 
Lateran (Laterano), the region of the Vatican where the 
Pope resides. While the clergy took care of the monument, 
they also marketed its stones. At the same time, the Colos­
seum had also become an important part of the annual 
procession for the Assumption of the Virgin Mary on 15 
August, which began in the Middle Ages. During the proces­
sion, an icon of Mary was carried from Santa Maria Mag­
giore and was met at the Colosseum with an icon of Christ, 
brought from San Giovanni in Laterano.
The ritual connection between Christianity and the Colos­
seum established with these processions was solidified from 
1490 onwards with another religious ritual, the Passion Play. 
This reenactment of the Passion of Christ, approved by the

## S. 236

236
pope, was held in the Colosseum for about fifty years. The 
event is minutely described in inventories, with a description 
of the accurate staging for the play, such as columns, artifi­
cial skies, etc. It was in this context that a new chapel was 
erected, Santa Maria della Pietà, in reference to the final 
event in the Passion Play, in which Mary holds her dead 
son. 
In this way, the Catholic Church adopted the Colosseum 
more and more openly and started using it as station for 
pilgrims. This interest was evidently motivated by a desire to 
replace the image of the Colosseum as a stage for pagan 
theatrical performances with one for Christian rituals. The 
Passion Play was abolished in 1539 for various reasons, 
including violence in the performances. Nevertheless, the 
little church of Santa Maria della Pietà remained in the 
Colosseum until the 19th century. 
THE COLOSSEUM AND THE COUNTER-REFORMATION 
The enactment of the Passion was a Christian link to an 
ancient past; at the same time, it was an opportunity to 
admire the organization of a theatre building that could no 
longer be built in the 16th century. During the first half of the 
16th century the Catholic Church was shaken to its roots by 
a series of religious reforms, originating in northern Europe 
as a consequence of the Protestant Reformation (see chapter 
9). Under the leadership of Protestant reformers, such as 
Martin Luther in Germany and Ulrich Zwingli in Switzerland, 
many regions of Europe moved away from the control of 
Rome. In turn, the Catholic Church responded with the

## S. 237

237
10. The Colosseum
Counter-Reformation (formulated during the Council of Trent 
of 1545-63), which attempted the re-establishment of Rome 
as the only center of Catholicism.
In the early stages of the Counter-Reformation, Christian 
martyrdom was an important recurring theme. It was not 
only an object of historical interest, but it framed the inten­
tion and affirmation of the church during the 16th century in 
the face of the Reformation. Martyrs were Christians execut­
ed because of their religious beliefs, Jesus being the most 
important one. The 1591 book Trattato de gli Instrumenti di 
Martirio by Antonio Gallonio is a particularly significant 
example. It illustrates several instruments of martyrdom, 
which were true symbols of this tradition. The Colosseum, 
known to be the site of martyrdoms already in ancient 
Rome, is featured several times. The theater motif used 
earlier in the century thus became, as the century pro­
gressed, a significant image alluding to the foundation of 
Christianity. 
The Jesuits, a religious order founded in 1540, actively 
propagated Christianity all over the world, including the New 
World of the Americas. Many of these missions failed, how­
ever, and were then reframed as contemporary martyrdoms. 
The circumstances of their martyrdoms were then reproduced 
through different media, such as frescos, where the imagery 
was then related to the Passion of Christ. As part of the 
Counter-Reformation, early Christian martyrs were then often 
associated with contemporary ones. A complex apparatus of 
propaganda therefore glorified martyrdom to inspire new 
potential martyrs and to thus continue the promotion of 
Catholic Christianity.

## S. 238

238
St. Ignatius in the Colosseum and the Propagation of a 
New Truth
A key moment for the iconology of martyrs was the 
reform of the calendar. By order of the pope, Cesare Baronio, 
a highly educated cardinal, compiled the Martyrology, ac­
cording to which every day was devoted to the celebration of 
a saint, as a way to remember his or her martyrdom. This 
calendar, which we still use today, was introduced by Pope 
Gregory XIII. It regularized the year and with it, the saints 
and martyrs who were to be recognized. This is the so-called 
Gregorian calendar, which in the Western world superseded 
the Julian calendar. 
According to the Gregorian calendar, 1 March celebrates 
a group of 260 martyrs who were executed in the Colosseum 
by order of Emperor Claudius. The Colosseum then became 
popularly understood as a place of martyrdom, adding the 
ultimate layer of Christian interest to Colosseum, in addition 
to its relevance as an object of Antiquity, unique for its size, 
the layout of its seats, its well-preserved northern façade and 
the theatrical motif. 
However, the multiple nature of such interest resulted in 
new and original interpretations. One example is the martyr­
dom of St. Ignatius. On 1 February, at the time of Emperor 
Trajan, Bishop Ignatius of Rome was martyred by being 
condemned ad bestiam, that is thrown to the lions. In a 
17th-century fresco in the church of San Vitale in Rome, the 
martyrdom is represented within an already ruined Colosse­
um, therefore ‘moving’ the martyrdom in time and insisting 
on its contemporary relevance. This reactivated Christian

## S. 239

239
10. The Colosseum
martyrdom also led to a total reinterpretation of the amphi­
theater itself. For example, St. Ignatius’s relics, originally 
located in San Clemente on the road from the Colosseum to 
the Lateran (according to the Panciroli guide of around 
1600), in later literature is described to have been moved to 
the Colosseum itself. In other words, the Colosseum, an 
outstanding and much-admired ancient building, but with no 
Catholic history, was appropriated and repurposed as a 
Catholic site. 
By means of written propaganda, relics began to be 
imaginatively placed in the Colosseum. Following the relics 
of St. Ignatius came a whole series of relics of martyrs who 
had suffered their martyrdom somewhere in the area, or 
which were at that time accommodated in nearby churches. 
In the guidebook of Rome by Pietro Martire Felini, Le cose 
meravigliose dell'alma città di Roma (1565), martyrs were 
even depicted fighting back during their martyrdoms, with 
the arena becoming the stage for rewriting Christian history. 
As a result, during and after the Reformation the Colos­
seum became a symbol and a medium for the propagation of 
new, revisited beliefs and histories. This activity, under the 
authority of the Catholic Church, was evidently more con­
cerned with building a strong propaganda than with histori­
ography. 
COMPETING FUCTIONS OF THE COLOSSEUM
As a result of the Colosseum’s significance for Catholic 
and Renaissance culture, many competing interpretations of

## S. 240

240
the Colosseum emerged. For example, in his autobiography 
from around 1558, painter Benvenuto Cellini writes that after 
having entrusted himself to a false priest in order to win the 
love of Sicilian Angelica, he was taken to the Colosseum to 
summon the devil. This sort of apocryphal, unregulated layer 
of narrative embedded in the Colosseum led the Catholic 
Church to enforce facts and truths upon the building, even in 
a physical way.
Thanks to a particularly devoted Theatin monk, Father 
Carlo Tomasi, the Colosseum turned into an important site 
for the Great Pilgrimage, taking place every 25 years. For 
the Jubilee year of 1675 changes in the building were made, 
especially in the inscriptions, and the existing perimeter wall 
was closed to protect it. The Colosseum thus became a huge 
reliquary. However, this was still not enough to give the 
Colosseum its new architectural significance, especially 
considering the aura of admiration that the building in­
spired.
The Project of Carlo Fontana
The papal architect Carlo Fontana (1638–1714), who 
came from a long dynasty of famous Baroque architects 
from the area of Ticino and Lombardy, was moved by the 
idea of providing an architectural statement for the martyrs 
and in the late 17th century designed a church set in the 
arena of amphitheater. His design was, on the one hand, 
bound by the exemplary character of the Colosseum as an 
ancient model for the superimposition of the classical orders; 
on the other hand, the project was closely informed and driv­

## S. 241

241
10. The Colosseum
en by the dozens of narratives of martyrdoms that had been 
associated over the centuries with the space of the Colosse­
um (like that of St. Ignatius).
Fontana depicted his project as a dramatic Baroque 
juxtaposition of ancient and new buildings. The design 
consisted of a new church inserted into and related to the 
preexisting structure of Colosseum. Fontana therefore re­
ferred to the Catholic history of the building, while using an 
ancient model. For example, the vertical divisions of the new 
church precisely followed the layering of the Colosseum’s 
theater motif: the nave of the church corresponding to the 
first gallery, the drum of the dome to the second gallery and 
the triumphal statue on top to third gallery. Additionally, 
Fontana restored the seats of the arena as steps represent­
ing the ascension of the martyrs into heaven, conceptually 
reversing the relationship between scene and beholder.
Fontana also designed a new layout for the arena. The 
two focal points at either side of the ellipse of the Colosse­
um are completely rewritten, and become, respectively, a 
round fountain and the entrance to the martyr church, which 
is attached to one corner of the Colosseum and occupies 
exactly one quarter of the ancient arena. The circular 
church, flanked by two bell towers, also recalls San Pietro in 
Montorio, another place of martyrdom and compositionally 
similar.
Fontana published this project as part of a larger book, 
mostly dedicated to a careful reconstruction of the Colosse­
um, entitled L'anfiteatro Flavio (1725). Only in the last part 
of the book does Fontana unfold his project, which he calls

## S. 242

242
‘Del restituir l’onore all’anfiteatro Flavio’ (Of returning the 
amphitheater to its honor). Like many architects of the 
time, Fontana claimed that his project was a ‘restitution’ 
(from the Latin ‘restituere’): by reconstructing an ancient 
building and imagining a new layout and function, the build­
ing was ‘given back’ lost magnificence. Fontana’s project 
was never built.

## S. 243

243
10. The Colosseum
CONCLUSION
In the 18th century the Colosseum was already becoming 
the object of early touristic and archeological interest. Nev­
ertheless, Fontana’s project became an important reference 
in the process of recognition of the building’s importance to 
the Catholic faith. The Colosseum was eventually consecrat­
ed and in 1750 crosses were set up around it to celebrate 
the Way of the Cross. The small church, of which Goethe 
also wrote about, was kept. The competing interpretations 
of the Colosseum, the result of centuries of architectural 
fascination and religious appropriation, continued through­
out the 18th century.

## S. 247

11. The Transformations of Rome
INTRODUCTION 
The increasing grip of the papacy on the city of Rome 
gradually transformed it into the capital of a religion grow­
ing across the world. As such, Rome was often seen as the 
archetypical capital: a city at the center of a territory and a 
state, the place from which this territory was governed, but 
also where this government and its power was enacted. 
Rome was seen as the embodiment of the whole state, a 
perception that generated iconographies and practices repre­
senting the power over the territory.

here: the synergy between planning and image building.

## S. 251

251
11. The Transformations of Rome
ROME AS THE CENTER OF CHRISTIANITY
As importance of the papacy in Rome grew over the 16th 
and 17th centuries, the city underwent a process of change 
that transformed the original Roman imperial city, fallen and 
shrunken during the medieval period, into the new center of 
Christianity. Indeed, Catholicism was becoming a religion of 
increasingly global scope. The more the institution of the 
Catholic Church expanded its territory, the more it required 
a center to which it could refer and by which it could be 
represented.
As the seat of the pope, Rome had to be a center of both 
political and of spiritual power. The pope was the spiritual 
head of (Western) Christianity, and the temporal ruler of the 
Papal States on the Italian peninsula. These states that 
would continue to exist until the unification of Italy in 1861, 
after which Rome became the capital of the new country of 
Italy. This dual nature, at once political and religious, led to 
the development of a kind of archetypical city. One of the 
metaphors most often adopted to describe Rome in the 17th 
century was that of a theater: the city where the papacy 
represented itself to the world, as in a theater performance; 
but also a stage for the world, which contained representa­
tives and ambassadors from all Christendom. 
The process that Rome underwent was to become to 
some extent typical for other capitals as well. The city was 
physically transformed, becoming more efficient as a center 
of government and administration. It became more defined 
as a seat of power and of authoritative institution. But while 
the city became an ensemble of physical structures and

## S. 252

252
infrastructures, at the same time, on a more abstract level, 
it also developed an image of itself. Rome became represent­
ed in a wide array of media (paintings, prints, guidebooks, 
poetry) as the central theater of the world. This image had 
effects both internally and externally. The image of Rome 
was reproduced time and time again within Rome, almost as 
a way to overlay the perfect imagined city on a still imper­
fect reality. At the same time, the image of Rome was also 
being projected outside, as a means to represent the papacy, 
and its claims on power and authority, to the world. Thanks 
to these images, Christians at even the greatest distance 
could entertain a specific idea of what Rome was and what it 
looked like. 
GIEDION’S INTERPRETATION OF SIXTUS V
The persistence of a conception of Rome as the ideal 
capital can be illustrated through one of the most important 
architecture books of the 20th century, Sigfried Giedion’s 
Space, Time and Architecture: The Growth of a New Tradi­
tion, first published in 1941. In this history of architecture, 
Giedion explained and legitimized the emergence of modern­
ism, which he understood as a moment when space became 
free in relation to architecture. This allowed the free use, 
experience, and occupation of space. According to Giedion, 
this altered attitude toward space was driven by changing 
conceptions of time. Initially considered fragmentary and 
divisible into units, for instance through the religious calen­
dar, it eventually became conceived as a continuous and free 
flowing entity.

## S. 253

253
An important section of Space, Time and Architecture is 
devoted to the urban development of Rome where, according 
to Giedion, transformations in the attitude towards space 
and time became particularly visible. Writing about Baroque 
Rome, Gideon noted that in the late 16th century a new form 
of central administration inspired by spiritual principles 
became indispensable for the existence of the Catholic 
Church. The newly emerging model city was global, connect­
ed to the world, and a place where diversity and various 
influences interacted in multiple ways to produce vitality in a 
new and rich organism. Giedion believed that spiritual princi­
ples were the driving force that generated a new Rome, a city 
made up of diverse components while at the same time 
integrated by unifying principles. 
Giedion’s understanding of Baroque Rome is a rather 
idealized view of the city and of its history. For example, he 
interprets Catholicism as the spiritualizing energy of the 
state, without considering the violent forces that also shaped 
the urban fabric of Rome, such as the conflicts between the 
pope and the local Roman families. Furthermore, Gideon did 
not describe how the spiritual principles governing the city 
were represented or communicated to the world. He ab­
stracted the fact that ‘spiritual’ authority was first and 
foremost Catholic and was made visible and communicated 
in order to propagate religion and the authority of the pope, 
which happened in a very explicit and theatrical fashion. 
This approach has to do with Giedion’s interests during 
the post-war era, which focused on understanding how archi­
tecture can develop a new form of monumentality that no 
longer relies on past forms, such as the architectural lan­
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 254

254
guage of classicism. Gideon was considering how modernist 
architecture could achieve a new monumentality and saw 
Baroque Rome as a model for this process. Interestingly, 
Gideon eventually identified the monumental features of the 
capital with the urban interventions of Pope Sixtus V. In his 
book, Giedion includes a map entitled ‘The Planning of 
Baroque Rome by Sixtus V’, which highlights the structure 
of the capital: a system of axes connecting ancient monu­
ments and churches, and made legible by means of visual 
markers, like obelisks or columns. Within this polycentric 
but hierarchically organized urban fabric, Gideon identified 
the city laid out by Sixtus V and its design principles as 
related to the birth of urban planning. Giedion also explicitly 
rejected the 19th-century modernization of Rome as a de­
struction of its original Baroque system. In his view, the 
transformations of Rome during the 19th century showed the 
dark side of modernity, and it was the task of the modern 
architect of the 20th century to learn the lessons of Baroque 
Rome.
THE BEGINNING: RESETTLING THE PAPAL SEE
Before the 15th century, the position of the papacy in 
Rome was fragile. It was only established in St. John Later­
an, where the pope resided as the bishop of Rome. The pope 
was marginalized, spatially and politically, due to power 
struggles between different factions and families permanently 
in conflict over control of the city. At the same time, the 
position of the pope as ruler of Christianity in the West, 
symbolically legitimizing him as successor of the Roman 
Emperor, turned him into an important competitor of other

## S. 255

255
European states—the religious power of the popes often chal­
lenged the secular authority of kings.
In the 14th century a conflict between the papal state and 
the French monarchy reached a climax when King Philip IV 
refused to submit to the authority of the pope and tried to 
exert increasing influence over papal elections by murdering 
Bonifacio VIII. Afterwards, Philip IV chose Clement V as his 
candidate for papacy, and, as Rome was not safe, moved the 
papal court to Avignon, where it remained for 60 years 
(1309–77). However, for a number of reasons, by the 15th 
century it became necessary to restore the spiritual authority 
back in Rome. This return triggered the papal schism, when 
two popes ruled, neither universally recognized, from 1378 to 
1415. This schism was solved with the Council of Constance 
(1414–18), which resulted in Pope Martin V becoming the 
sole legitimate pope and allowing for the progressive reset­
tlement of the papacy in Rome. The Ottoman conquest of 
Byzantium in 1453 also created pressure to keep the papacy 
in Rome, and to rebuild a symbolic counterpart to the Otto­
man Empire. 
What Martin V encountered when he moved back to 
Rome was a city in decline. Demarcated by the Aurelian 
walls from the 3rd century, the city had shrunk, leaving large 
sections within the walls uninhabited. The Vatican was 
connected to Rome by a single bridge (Ponte Sant’Angelo) 
and remained rather isolated from the rest of the city. Never­
theless, with the return of the papacy to Rome the Vatican 
began to emerge as a renewed and reinforced center, next to 
and independent from Rome.
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 256

256
Historically, the grounds of the Vatican spread around 
the basilica of St. Peter, one of the earliest and most mag­
nificent Christian basilicas in Rome, whose foundations were 
attributed to the Emperor Constantine. Built after 325 AD 
and probably by Constans, Constantine’s son, the basilica 
was erected around the shrine where St. Peter was buried.
Despite being unprotected by the Roman walls and there­
fore vulnerable within the city, the site of St. Peters soon 
became a very important place of pilgrimage. A defensive 
system, begun in the 6th century, was enlarged by Leo IV in 
the 9th century and became known as the Leonine walls. The 
enclosed territory incorporated Castel Sant’Angelo, originally 
the Mausoleum of Adrian, but not the basilica. Thanks to 
commerce and pilgrimage, the city within the Leonine walls, 
known as Leonine city, grew, residences and hospitals were 
built, and became a site of trade. At the same time, from 
the 9th century onward, the increasing presence of the pope 
at the Vatican led to the construction of the Vatican palace.
Centuries later, in the decades after the pope had been 
returned from Avignon, the process of resettlement of the 
papacy was developed around St. Peter as an independent 
urban entity, consolidating the area of the Leonine city also 
known as the Borgo Leonino.
A TESTAMENT FOR ROME BY NICHOLAS V
The Rome of Sixtus V that so fascinated Giedion had its 
roots in mid-15th century developments. According to sources 
from that period, the papal vision of Rome was formulated

## S. 257

257
by another pope, Nicholas V (1447–55), as part of his las 
will. The project outlined by Nicholas V aimed at restructur­
ing the city of Rome around the power of papacy as it grew 
during the first half of the 15th century. He proposed a pro­
ject to develop the Borgo Leonino into a larger site for 
residences and pilgrims, but above all as a site of govern­
ment for the papal state. In this plan, the Vatican palace 
would be not just a residence, but the center of a court.
The rebuilding of St. Peter’s obviously had a significant 
place within this program. Nicholas V planned to remodel of 
old St. Peter’s church a magnificent basilica and involved, 
among others, Leon Battista Alberti to draw up plans. 
According to the final design, perfected by Bernardo Rossel­
lino (1409-1464), a section of the transept and the apse of 
the church were expanded, and a new structure was project­
ed over the existing one. This moment was of crucial impor­
tance for the changes that would be implemented in St. 
Peter’s Basilica over the course of the 16th century. In fact, 
the apse of Nicholas V determined the scale for the church 
that was eventually entirely redesigned by Michelangelo. On 
the one hand, the project is emblematic of the level of ambi­
tion held by the popes, but on the other hand it also shows 
how, during the Renaissance, a relationship with the built 
environment became generally more audacious. Nicholas V 
basically proposed tearing down the most important existing 
church of Christianity, in order to build a much larger and 
imposing construction. 
Another part of Nicholas V’s project for Rome, which 
would be taken up again by his successor Sixtus V, consist­
ed of restoring the station churches. These were churches 
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 258

258
where, on a particular day of the year according to the 
liturgical calendar, mass was held in honor of a particular 
saint. These masses ensured the presence of the pope 
throughout the city and attracted money and patronage for 
the restoration of the churches involved. In a way, these 
churches became satellites of the papal court around the 
center of the city.
All these projects were part of a larger design of Nicholas 
V, one that triggered a persistent and continuous change 
over the following centuries, to extend the influence of the 
pope over the entire city. Nicholas V intended to develop the 
Vatican, considered the place of St. Peter’s martyrdom, into 
the real seat for a universal papacy, while at the same time 
trying to actively and definitively anchor the presence of the 
Vatican within the city of Rome. Such an ambitious project 
inevitably leads to questions about its true intentions: What 
did the pope hope to achieve with this project? How did the 
pope himself understand his ambitions? And how did his 
views relate to what actually happened? 
Some evidence of these aspects were recorded by Gi­
annozzo Manetti, a Renaissance humanist who lived at the 
court of Florence, in a three-part book entitled On the Life, 
the Achievements and the Testament of Nicholas V, Su­
preme Pontiff (Liber Primus de vita ac moribus Nicolai 
quinti summi pontifices; Liber Secundus de Gestis Nicolai 
Quinti Summi Pontifices; Liber Tertius de Testament Nicolai 
V Summi Pontificis Pridie Quam Moreretur). The text con­
sists of a biography, the achievements, and the testament of 
Nicholas V. All three are narrated in the form of a speech 
and offer justifications for the actions of Nicholas. For in­

## S. 259

259
stance, when discussing the form of religious buildings, 
Manetti ‘speaks’ for Nicholas and states that 
‘If, therefore, the figure of this temple is like the human 
body, as seemed obvious above, surely it follows that 
it would have acquired the noblest appearance, since 
we are not unaware that the human form was by far 
privileged over all the other figures of things animate 
and inanimate’. (51., Book 1, The Life of Nicholas V)
For the humanists of the time, this was an implicit refer­
ence to Vitruvius who, when addressing the topic of temples, 
introduced the human body as a paradigm of proportion. 
Nicholas V was aware that his grandiose plans (expanding 
the fortifications, developing the city, restoring churches, and 
rebuilding St. Peter’s) could have been read simply as a 
means of self-celebration. To avoid this, he presented his 
plans as beneficial for the entire Roman Church by introduc­
ing two arguments:
1. That great architectural projects would convince even 
the ignorant of the authority of the papacy and bring 
them closer to God. Nicholas V presented his projects 
as a means to convey the authority of the Roman 
Church to the masses. While the learned few could 
understand this authority intellectually through histo­
ry or knowledge, the illiterate people needed to be 
convinced by other means. Magnificent constructions 
touch people emotionally and make them understand 
through experience the power and authority of the 
Vatican. Everlasting monuments embody and express 
the eternal will of God. Even in this sense we can see 
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 260

260
a reference to Vitruvius, who in his dedication to 
Augustus expresses how monuments can convey the 
emperor’s judgment on architecture as well as his 
authority, projecting it into the future (see chapter 1). 
In Nicholas’s testament, this Vitruvian idea becomes 
associated with a Christian conception of beauty, 
where wonder and splendor are associated with the 
greatness and authority of God.
2. These great projects for Rome would also benefit the 
security of the papacy. This more practical reason 
expressed the motivation for his projects as defense. 
As a place of authority, the papacy should be strong 
and safe. 
It was under these contingent needs that Nicholas V 
framed and motivated his aspirations: 
These are the reasons—not ambition, not pomp, not 
vainglory, not a longer perpetuation of our name, but the 
greater authority of the Roman church, the more com­
plete dignity of the Apostolic See among Christian peo­
ples, and the more sure avoidance of the familiar persecu­
tions— why we conceived so many great buildings in our 
mind and heart. (Book 3, The Testament of Nicholas V)
THE PLAN FOR ROME BY SIXTUS V
The project, initiated by Nicholas V, of connecting the 
greatness of Rome to the papal see, continued to develop 
over the course of the 16th and 17th centuries. Of great im­
portance in this process is the figure of Sixtus V (1521–90). 
When considering Nicholas V’s plans, Giedion’s view of

## S. 261

261
Sixtus V appears to be too simplistic is chiefly interpreted 
as a continuation of Nicholas V. Indeed, it is important to 
recognize that Sixtus V did not invent a new image of Rome, 
and in fact his work is rooted in Nicholas V’s legacy. How­
ever, he brought his predecessor’s plan to a new scale and a 
new degree of intensity. Sixtus V employed strategies that 
had been developed over the previous century, but he also 
defined and propagated the image of the city of Rome in a 
new scale.
Sixtus V belonged to the Franciscan order. Founded by 
St. Francis of Assisi in 1209, the Franciscans identified with 
ideas poverty and pastoralism and were active in the space 
of the city through preachers and charities. Felice Peretti 
was elected Sixtus V in 1585 as a figure of compromise. He 
came from humble origins but climbed very quickly through 
the ranks of the ecclesiastical hierarchy. In the only five 
short years of pontificate, he accomplished a great deal of 
work. 
At the time of the election of Sixtus V, Rome was still in 
a dire state, ruled by warring families and with little central 
power. The pope primarily worked on the existing urban 
fabric, conceiving a new system of straight roads that ex­
panded the territories of the inhabited city farther within the 
walls. He also built architecture and infrastructure, reformed 
the government of the papal state, and restored public order 
(often through the use of force). 
In Sixtus V’s plans, numerous images of the city were 
produced as a way to guide its development, in print, paint­
ing, and literature. At the same time, they were intended to 
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 262

262
communicate and propagate the image of the city to the 
outside world. As such, Sixtus V was an important figure 
not just for the building of Rome as capital of the state, but 
also for promoting its image. The Rome of Sixtus V, as an 
urban reality and an idealized image, is still felt today. 
Some Interventions by Sixtus V
Under Sixtus V, the actual buildings of the city and the 
rebuilt city he imagined collapsed into one another. Sixtus V 
completed new monuments, reorganized the city, sanitized it, 
and expanded its territory. Within this framework, the pro­
cess of rebuilding was celebrated in an equally important 
production of images, proposing an idealized imaginary of 
what was unfolding. These images were produced by a limit­
ed set of artists, not only in frescos, but also in engravings 
and books. Rome was transformed into the holy city by 
means of architecture as much by means of image propaga­
tion. In this process of building and mediating, the pope 
assumed a central role, not just through the images, but by 
the physical application of heraldry and portraits around the 
city. 
St. John Lateran
Sixtus V put a great deal of effort into the outer center 
of Rome, the area within the Aurelian walls that was no 
longer densely populated, specifically in the area of St. John 
Lateran. This area was the traditional residence of the 
bishop of Rome and therefore a papal domicile. At this time, 
it was situated in an isolated southern part of the city.

## S. 263

263
This estate was a complicated ensemble of buildings, 
developed around the original Christian basilica of San 
Giovanni. According to legend, Emperor Constantine was 
baptized by Pope Silvester in the baptistery. Sixtus V reori­
ented the side of the Lateran area toward the city by build­
ing an imposing loggia on the original transept and by 
bringing the loggia to face the Colosseum along a new axis. 
In front of the loggia, he also planned a square with an 
obelisk.
Located in the medieval area was the Sancta Sanctorum, 
a chapel filled with the most precious relics of Rome and 
Christianity, and the Scala Sancta. This was a staircase of 
twenty-eight marble steps, believed to be the steps descend­
ed by Christ after his death sentence at the palace of Ponti­
us Pilate in Jerusalem and later brought to Rome by Helena, 
mother of Emperor Constantine. Driven by an interesting 
mixture of pragmatism, functional thinking and an attrac­
tion to modern architecture, Sixtus V decided to combine 
the Sancta Sanctorum and the Scala Sancta by moving both 
of them from their original site and then connecting them in 
a new palazzo designed by Domenico Fontana. 
The Lateran Palace
For the Palazzo del Laterano (Lateran palace), the 
principles of the Roman Renaissance palace most famously 
developed at Palazzo Farnese are applied in a very simple 
way for the exterior: a vertical volume divided horizontally 
into a base and a body, crowned with a cornice; the rustica­
tion of the corners; the articulation of the body by means of 
the aedicula of the windows; and a central gate with rusti­
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 264

264
cated frame and balcony. A noticeable characteristic of the 
interventions of Sixtus V is that in this process of mediation 
the architecture in itself was overall quite uninteresting. The 
Lateran Palace was based on a simplified and generic use of 
formulas developed in the preceding years, almost recalling 
corporate buildings, a model which could be repeated time 
and again.
The interior, on the other hand, was entirely decorated, 
in a short period of time, with a very coherent iconographi­
cal program completed by a well-organized team of artists, 
draftsmen, painters, all involved in the iconographical pro­
ject of Sixtus V. The topic of the decoration was the cele­
bration of Sixtus V’s own building projects: a full view of the 
Vatican library restored by Sixtus V, images of obelisks 
erected by him, an image of a fountain referring to the 
Acqua Felice aqueduct he built, and so on. 
The obelisk in Lateran
The obelisk is another of Sixtus V’s interventions. It 
marks the Lateran palace and is engraved with inscriptions 
referring to Emperor Constantine, an important symbol and 
figure for the propagation of Christianity. Constantine was 
frequently used in Catholic propaganda as the Roman em­
peror who defeated his rival Maxentius at the Milvian bridge 
because he adopted the sign of the cross, converted to 
Christianity, and transformed Christianity into the state 
religion of the Roman Empire. The obelisk was used to 
express this process of transformation, from pagan to Chris­
tian Empire, both materially and visually. The Romans had 
brought this obelisk from Egypt as a trophy: a sign of their

## S. 265

265
triumph and the rule of their Empire. By moving the obelisk 
to Rome, the city was designated as the center of the world. 
Sixtus erected and moved the obelisk and crowned it with a 
cross, expressing both the continuity from the Roman Em­
pire to the papacy and the triumph of the latter over the 
former. Since the obelisk was believed to be a religious 
symbol, related to the cult of the sun, the cross also indicat­
ed the superiority of the Christian religion over older cults, 
while again suggesting some form of continuity (the older 
religions as imperfect precursors to Christianity). 
The inscriptions on the base of the obelisk signaled that 
it was transformed into a monument to commemorate the 
Emperor’s baptism by pope Silvester I and to celebrate 
Christianity. This same imagery of the Emperor Constantine 
with the cross was repeated inside the Lateran church, 
through an articulated program of frescos that expressed the 
same message with different media.
Rome as Sacred Landscape
Key to the development of both the city and its image 
was the network of new churches, monuments, obelisks and 
connecting roads erected by Sixtus V. This network spanned 
the entire territory of the city, lending it a coherence. Rome 
became a landscape for the interventions of Sixtus V. Guid­
ing the pilgrim or visitor from one point of the city to the 
next, this landscape was a symbolical one, because it linked 
places of religious importance and facilitated their access. 
But the landscape was also a political one. It produced a 
powerful image of Rome as a city of obelisks and monu­
ments governed by the pope, who ruled the Christian Empire 
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 266

266
from Rome. Therefore, whereas Giedion’s interpretation of 
Sixtus V’s project as a predecessor to modern city planning 
finds some historical grounding, it inevitably underplays the 
religious motivations behind the program.
The idea of Rome as a sanctified city was activated and 
performed in the Year of Jubilee, which takes place every 
twenty-five years, when Christians are invited to Rome to 
undertake a pilgrimage to the seven main basilicas. This 
ritual turns Rome into a place of incessant procession, which 
sanctifies the space of the city, but also produces a particu­
lar experience of Rome, recalling the image of holy Jerusa­
lem.
While Nicholas V intended to invigorate the liturgy of the 
station churches, Sixtus V expanded it, by creating new 
stations across the territory of Rome and by establishing a 
new calendar, which determined when the papal court would 
visit each church to celebrate mass. In so doing, Sixtus V 
extended the sphere of influence of the pope and of his court 
from the Vatican across the territory of the whole city, while 
at the same time sanctifying the entirety of Rome.
Santa Maria Maggiore and Sixtus V’s Burial Chapel
A project of extreme importance to Sixtus V was the 
church of Santa Maria Maggiore, one of the seven main 
basilicas of Rome, dedicated to the Virgin Mary. Santa 
Maria Maggiore was situated outside of the inhabited center 
of Rome. It had become connected to the city through an 
access road begun in the 16th century. Under Sixtus V, a new 
access to the church was added, at the time not entirely

## S. 267

267
finished, directly tied to the Basilica of St. John Lateran. 
Sixtus V’s interest in Santa Maria Maggiore as a new center 
had three reasons: 
1. By developing the peripheral monument, he made the 
zone more attractive for inhabitants;
2. It expressed the pope’s devotion to the Virgin Mary;
3. Because of its vicinity to the family’s property estate 
of Peretti-Montalto, it reinforced Sixtus V’s symboli­
cal claims on the area.
This latter reason was later reinforced by making Santa 
Maria Maggiore the site of Sixtus V’s burial. To this end he 
added a chapel to the transept of the 5th-century basilica, on 
the scale of a small church, designed during the 1580s by 
Domenico Fontana, and richly decorated. Thanks to its 
scale, the chapel acted as an independent landmark, estab­
lishing a relationship with the obelisk erected just outside 
the church. The value of the chapel was increased by incor­
porating one of most important relics of Christianity, the 
holy manger, where Christ was supposed to have been born. 
The relic had been kept in a small chapel in the church for a 
long time before Sixtus V. The small shrine with the manger 
was then put in a box and moved from its original position 
into the so-called confessione (confession), an underground 
space beneath the altar of the chapel. The chapel therefore 
became a piece of building infrastructure, as the structure of 
the chapel itself allowed the removal of the holy relic from 
one place to another. The manger was lowered into the 
basement area, and above it an altar with a ciborium and a 
balustrade was built, allowing visitors to see the relic but 
not touch it. The whole arrangement established a visual 
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 268

268
connection between relic and altar, at the same time sancti­
fying the altar itself and avoiding any critical enquiry or 
direct contact with the body of the relic.
The City and the Guidebook
Sixtus V’s project for Rome also provides a clear sense of 
how urban scenography played an important role in the 
mediated processes between the city and its visual reproduc­
tion. The circulation of an image of the city evolved in a 
specific genre of books emerging at this time, the illustrated 
guidebook. Rome was reproduced via very recognizable 
reproductions of obelisks and church facades. These images 
acted as visual links between the guidebook and the urban 
space. A particularly relevant example is Girolamo Franzini’s 
Le Cose Maravigliose della Alma Città di Roma (Marvelous 
Things of the Modern City of Rome), published in 1588. 
Designed to help pilgrims orient themselves around the 
city, guidebooks were progressively distributed outside of 
Rome and acted as a medium to divulge the spaces of the 
city and its characteristics. For example, guidebooks re­
vealed the role of the obelisks in the topography of the city 
and, as a result, the presence of Sixtus V. Each obelisk, 
generally descended from antiquity, was connected to a mon­
ument, and defined a site. Each part could then be related 
to the entire fabric of Rome.
Michael Cole, an American scholar, has termed the 
process enacted by erecting obelisks and putting crosses, 
statues, or Christian symbols on top of them, as a ‘perpetu­
al exorcism’, a permanent visual reenactment of the triumph 
of Christianity over different forms of pagan antiquity. The

## S. 269

269
notion of triumph is embodied especially in the Trajan and 
Antonian columns, which were the triumphal columns of the 
emperors and upon which Sixtus V placed statues of St. 
Peter and St. Paul, the two most important apostles.
Nowhere is Christianity’s perceived historical triumph 
better expressed than in Tomaso de Laureti’s The Triumph 
of Religion (the Cross), a fresco located in the Stanza di 
Costantino at the Vatican Palace and completed in 1585. 
Sixtus V himself ordered the painting, begun before his 
papacy, to be placed at the center of the ceiling, in a hall 
decorated by Raphael in the early 16th-century. The empty 
space, framed through a strong perspective view, depicts an 
‘architettura all’antica’ of rich marble. In the middle, by 
miraculous intervention a cross replaces a pagan idol that is 
smashed to the ground.
Sixtus V and Antiquity 
For Sixtus V, Antiquity now played a different role than 
before. Only sixty years after Bramante’s Tempietto (see 
chapter 4), Antiquity no longer embodied a reference model, 
but rather a dimension to be entirely dominated by the new 
principles of Christianity. No longer was Antiquity an artistic 
and intellectual heritage that could be used and adopted 
freely, but rather something to be controlled and reshaped.
 
This new attitude towards Antiquity is clearly shown in 
several projects. The marble used to build Sixtus’ burial 
chapel in Santa Maria Maggiore came from the Septizodium 
(or Septizonium), an ancient structure at the foot of the 
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 270

270
Palatine Hill erected to screen the palaces on the Palatine 
from the crowds around the Circus Maximum (see chapter 
5). In the Renaissance, the Septizodium was considered one 
of the most beautiful ancient examples, still intact, of a 
superposition of the three orders. 
 
A similar operation, now involving monuments from the 
earliest ages of Christianity, was undertaken in the complex 
of St. John Lateran, where Christian monuments such as the 
Sancta Sanctorum and the Scala Santa were freely removed, 
rebuilt, or transferred. Even these venerated shrines now vali­
dated the new attitude toward Antiquity by being organized 
in a scenography that would show the superiority of Christi­
anity. In this shift of attitude, it is important to consider 
both the personality of Sixtus V, who did not have a human­
ist education, and the militant rise of Rome as capital of the 
Papacy and as a city that needed to be defended and propa­
gandized at a time of Protestant reforms across Europe, 
where any sort of pagan symbolism could be highly problem­
atic.
THE TRANSFORMATION OF ST. PETER’S SQUARE
When Sixtus V became pope, the obelisk that is now in 
the center of the St. Peter’s square was located on the side 
of the church. The dome of St. Peter’s, designed by Michel­
angelo, was still not finished. The square had already been 
transformed in the preceding century and continued to 
change over the course of the 17th century. Sixtus V lavished 
a great deal of his energy on this site. The dome of the

## S. 271

271
church was completed, and next to it a new palace and a 
new staircase were built. Later, in 1610, under Pope Paul V, 
the entrance to the Vatican palace was built and given a 
new façade.
The renovation of St. Peter’s square was initiated by the 
erection of an obelisk in front of the basilica. Originally, the 
obelisk was situated next to the sacristy, but Sixtus decided 
to move to the front of the church. This took considerable 
effort and required the use of complex machinery under the 
direction of Domenico Fontana. The moving of the obelisk 
was celebrated with a magnificent publication, Fontana’s Del 
Modo Tenuto nel Trasportare il Obelisco Vaticano of 1590. In 
the frontispiece of the book, the obelisk in its original posi­
tion sits next to an illustration with two angels carrying the 
obelisk, clearly recalling the iconography of Loreto that 
evokes the miraculous transportation of an object by divine 
will (see chapter 9). The presence of the figure of Concordia 
recalls that the efforts of the engineers, whose different 
solutions to the problem of moving the obelisk are only 
possible under her protection. The message is that only in 
peace and prosperity the arts can flourish. Perhaps on a 
more detailed level, the construction that Fontana invents to 
hold the obelisk recalls the fasces that Concordia is holding. 
The fasces are a bundle of arrows tied together, symbolizing 
unity. In other words, moving the obelisk proves, in the view 
of Fontana, how engineering—science—and divine providence 
work toward the same goal: the greatness of the Church, the 
power of the papacy, and the splendor of its monuments.
The same parallel is suggested in Gallonio’s Trattato de 
gli Instrumenti di Martirio of 1591, published just one after 
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 272

272
Fontana’s publication (see chapter 10). In the images of the 
ways in which early Christian martyrs met their death, em­
phasis is given to the instruments of torture themselves, and 
very elaborate settings evocate ancient pagan Rome. The 
message conveyed here is similar to that of Fontana’s book: 
it is only through refined technology that the sanctification 
of a territory can be established. Of course, in Gallonio’s 
Trattato this sanctification is achieved not by means of 
Christian monuments, as in Fontana’s book, but through the 
dispersal of the blood of martyrs, insisting on the signifi­
cance of martyrdom explored in the previous chapter.
The difficult operation of moving the obelisk fit into 
Sixtus’s intentions, as outlined above. The aim was again to 
mark the view of St. Peter’s by means of the obelisk and to 
connect the most important church in Rome with the rest of 
the topography of the city. This act also played with Chris­
tian symbols and allegories that could then be mediated and 
propagated by means of images. 
Bernini’s Square
The new space in front of the church formalized the view 
onto a monument that, by 1585, had been under construc­
tion for more than seventy years. The rebuilding of St. Pe­
ter’s began in the early 16th century and the main effort of 
the rebuilding centered on what is now the crossing. A 
significant part of the church was left standing during the 
reconstruction. The old nave and façade were still in place. 
(see chapter 13) As a consequence, the piazza was smaller 
and irregularly shaped, and the basilica was hidden from the

## S. 273

273
view by a conglomerate of buildings in front and by the 
loggia. A more regular piazza would not only liberate the 
view of the church but also improve the connection to the 
Borgo, and from there to Castel Sant’Angelo and the city 
center. 
An important step in the reconstruction of St. Peter’s 
was the completion of the new facade of the basilica by 
Carlo Maderno, finished in 1607 under Pope Paul V (1605–
21), forming a backdrop for the obelisk. In the late 1630s, 
Gian Lorenzo Bernini then designed and began to build two 
bell towers on the sides of the façade. After the completion 
of the south tower, cracks in the façade of the church re­
quired halting the works, leading eventually to the demoli­
tion of the towers.
The construction of the piazza, under Pope Alexander 
VII (1655–67), was the termination of a very long process. 
Drawn by Bernini and begun in 1656, the design solved the 
problem of framing the façade while providing two entrances, 
one to the Vatican palace and one to the basilica. Bernini 
decided to design an oval, whose northern semicircular arm 
framed the entrance to the palace when approaching from 
the Borgo. At the same time, he mirrored this figure on the 
other side, to create two axes of symmetry. One centered on 
the façade of the church, incorporating the position of the 
obelisk and the other transversal created an axis to the 
street leading into the piazza from the north. 
The intention was to have two narrow streets giving 
access to the square when coming from the city, with one 
leading directly to the entrance of the palace. The full size 
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 274

274
and expansion of the piazza would only be revealed once one 
entered from this street. At the same time, it would have 
been as if the piazza embraced the community of the faith­
ful, a point made explicit in sources at the time of the de­
sign process. In fact, the choice of a monumental element 
such as the two arms enclosing the square was the result of 
a long negotiation and a complex decision-making process. 
Bernini’s son Pietro ‘justified’ the design for square by read­
ing its architecture iconographically, as arms receiving 
anyone in need to be embraced by the Catholic faith:
The oval is proper for the piazza because, since the 
church of St. Peter is like the model of all other [church­
es], it has to have a portico that receives with open 
arms, like a mother, the Catholics to confirm their 
fate, the Heretics to reunite them with the Church, 
and the Infidels to illuminate them with the true faith.
The piazza also provides a view onto the façade of the 
basilica and the Vatican palace, visible above the northern 
arm of the piazza. From there the pope blesses the faithful 
on special occasions. The spatial expansion of the piazza 
would have been reinforced by a third screen between the 
piazza and the Borgo, hiding the oval from view until the 
visitor entered it, but this screen was never built. This effect 
has been entirely destroyed by the Via della Conciliazione, 
which now connects St. Peter’s to Castel Sant’Angelo; it was 
built in the 1930s to celebrate the Lateran Treaty between 
the papacy and fascist Italy. However, plans to build this 
connection were already made in the late 17th century.
To obtain the original scenography of the square, its 
topographical profile was very important. The terrain rises

## S. 275

275
along the axis linking to Castel Sant’Angelo and then de­
scends into the piazza to rise again towards the church. The 
effect is both of a heightened depth of the square and an 
emphasized monumentality of the church façade. In other 
words, the piazza is conceived as a hollow space in which 
the beholder sinks before moving up to the façade of the 
church.
The connection between the square and the palace was 
further developed by building the Scala Regia, designed by 
Bernini and built between 1663 and 1666, and between the 
Vatican and the city by decorating the Ponte St Angelo, also 
designed by Bernini, built between 1669 and 1672.
The Scala Regia and Ponte St. Angelo
The concluding element of the complex was the physical 
connection between the square and Vatican Palace. From the 
bronze gate located on the right side of the Scala Regia, a 
long staircase, designed by Bernini, connected the church to 
the palace. The visitor entering the Palace would be wel­
comed by a large equestrian statue of the emperor Constan­
tine the Great, also produced by Bernini. Constantine is an 
important figure in the mythology of Christianity. It was 
thanks to Constantine that Christianity was established as 
the official religion of the Roman Empire. The entrance to 
the Vatican Palace is thus signaled by the image of the 
emperor, posed on his horse, about to go into battle, in a 
reenactment of his conversion to Christianity. The statue of 
this triumphant hero of the Church plays an important 
ritualistic part in setting the scene and establishing roles as 
11. The Transformations of Rome

## S. 276

276
one enters the Vatican Palace, a manipulation of experience 
that is echoed by the walls of the staircase, which get pro­
gressively narrower as one moves toward the statue. By play­
ing with the effects of perspective, Bernini produced a space 
that appears longer than it actually is, drawing the visitor 
up to the important entrance. 
The final touch in this process of establishing the signifi­
cance of St. Peter’s was adorning the ancient Roman bridge 
connecting the Borgo, containing the Vatican City, with 
Rome. This bridge, the Ponte Sant’Angelo, which was part 
of the same project of development of St. Peter’s, was deco­
rated with twelve sculpted angels, holding the objects that 
tempted Jesus Christ. Similarly, the roofs of the two colon­
nades embracing St. Peter’s Square were adorned with doz­
ens of large statues of saints, a project which kept sculptors 
busy for a decade.

## S. 277

277
CONCLUSION
Like Sixtus V before him, Alexander VII used the new 
ensemble of Saint Peter’s square as an image to propagate 
the idea of papal Rome. The image of the square was first 
published on a medal, almost as a logo for the church, with 
an axial bird’s eye view that emphasizes the embrace of the 
square. This striking image has been reproduced in count­
less media and even served as a model for aerial photos of 
the square.
The transformation of Rome into a religious and political 
capital of Christianity was the result of a complex combina­
tion of physical (urban, architectural, decorative…) and 
propagandistic (books, iconography) projects, dating as far 
back as the 14th century. Both these aspects had the pro­
found impact upon the fabric of the city and are still felt 
today, not only in the way in which Rome is shaped, but 
also in the ways it is represented and immortalized as the 
‘eternal city’ (tourism, cinema…).
11. The Transformations of Rome

279

## S. 281
