12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel
INTRODUCTION 
Bernini’s masterpiece of the Cornaro Chapel is a clear 
example of Baroque architecture. Defining the cultural, 
religious, political, and artistic context within which it was 
created, beginning with the statue of Teresa of Avila, and 
analyzing together with the chapel reveal the main features, 
stakes, and intentions behind the Roman Baroque.

## S. 282

282
General architectural features of the Baroque.
Architectural features of the Baroque in the Cornaro Chapel 
and other 17th-century Roman buildings.
Key works of Bernini and key themes of his artistic perso­
nality, e.g., imitation of Michelangelo. 
The function of a structure like the chapel: representation 
of a family; commemoration; salvation. 
The function of art: expression of religious ideas and emo­
tions and inviting imitation—to move the viewer through 
shared emotion by presenting a striking image. 
How material and other elements are used in the Cornaro 
Chapel.
How a work like the Cornaro Chapel emerges through the 
interplay between different actors.
How the chapel fits into a sequence of different scales—
city, church, chapel.
WHAT TO KNOW

## S. 283

283
12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel
Guidobaldo Abbatini, View of the Cappella Cornaro, ca. 1651.

## S. 284

284
THE EMOTION OF ROMAN BAROQUE
Charles de Brosses was a politically active French aristo­
crat and one of many intellectuals and artists whose inter­
ests were devoted to Roman antiquity. As a historian, de 
Brosses took a keen interest in the authors of antiquity but 
also in the customs, habits, and rituals of people. He had a 
particular interest in writing the history of Rome and editing 
ancient texts. During the three months of a residency in 
Rome, in 1739–40, he sketched a lively image of the city, its 
architecture and art. 
One of the artworks he saw in Rome was Gian Lorenzo 
Bernini’s statue The Ecstasy of Santa Teresa d’Avila, placed 
in the church of Santa Maria della Vittoria and executed 
between 1647 and 1652. He ridiculed it, famously writing in 
a letter, ‘If this is divine love, I know all about it.’ In his 
view, the statue did not express any kind of divine love, 
rather a very carnal one. 
This opinion was not universally shared, however. The 
art critic Luigi Scaramuccia, in Le Finezze dei Pennelli 
Italiani (1674), wrote that ‘as she is seen to be in ecstasy, 
so she makes him who gazes fall into ecstasy by virtue of 
the excellence of that great master who made her’ (p. 18). 
Scaramuccia read Bernini’s sculpture as a perfectly executed 
portrayal of divine ecstasy, capable of inciting the same 
emotions in the viewer. Scaramuccia identified the mastery of 
Bernini with the spiritual effect of the work of art. Bernini’s 
St. Teresa statue and the reactions it generated can be used 
as a frame to contextualize what is called Roman Baroque.

## S. 285

285
TERESA OF AVILA AS EMBLEM
Bernini’s statue acquires particular relevance within the 
Catholic Counter-Reformation. In this religious and political 
process, the Catholic Church reexamined and, in many 
cases, reformed existing saints and orders (see chapter 9). 
By the time Bernini created the statue, Teresa of Avila 
(1515–82) had become one of the most venerated and sym­
bolic religious figures of the age. She was a Spanish Carmel­
ite nun who, after a period of mysterious illnesses and mys­
tic visions, initiated a reform of her Carmelite order by 
returning to the original rules of monastic life based on 
absolute poverty. While guiding this reform, she described 
her mystical experiences in autobiographical writings. She 
also founded new monasteries of the Discalced Carmelite 
order (known in English as the Barefoot Carmelites). 
Her mystical writings are among the great works of 
Spanish literature of the 16th century. She describes the 
progressive union of the soul with Christ through different 
stages of contemplation. At the same time, she describes 
this spiritual union with God in terms of bodily experience, 
such as acts of physical penetration. The twofold meaning 
that her experience carried helped people to understand the 
truth and veracity of that experience. In this way, the body, 
or her body, through the legibility of metaphor, became an 
important means through which to communicate spiritual 
experience. In Bernini’s sculpture, then, this strategy is 
reenacted; in the pose of Teresa d’Avila’s penetrated body, 
the spiritual experience becomes visible. 
12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel

## S. 286

286
Teresa of Avila, whose visions were considered a sign of 
her spirituality, was beatified in 1614, which initiated the 
process of her canonization. In 1617, to promote her cult, 
the relic of her right foot was brought to Rome for venera­
tion. In 1622 she was declared a saint in an extremely lavish 
ceremony, together with two other 16th-century founders of 
new orders, Ignatius of Loyola and Filippo Neri. Teresa of 
Avila then became one of the most important saints of the 
Counter-Reformation. 
THE DISCALCED CARMELITES AND SANTA MARIA 
DELLA VITTORIA
The order that Teresa of Avila founded, the Discalced 
Carmelites, was established in Rome in 1595. In 1605 they 
moved to the site of San Paolo alla Conversione, where the 
architects Carlo Maderno and Giovanni Battista Soria built 
a new church for them (1607–26). In 1620 the church of the 
Carmelites was rededicated as Santa Maria della Vittoria 
(Our Lady of Victory), to celebrate Catholicism’s military 
victory over the Protestants at the battle of the White 
Mountain. The church became a symbol of Christianity 
against heretics, a part of militant Catholicism. The main 
altar of the church houses the miraculous image of the 
Virgin Mary that was said to have assisted the Catholics to 
victory. 
From an architectural and urban point of view, the 
church of Santa Maria della Vittoria fits into the redevelop­
ment of Rome initiated by Sixtus V (see chapter 11). It is 
located opposite the Acqua Felice, the fountain that Sixtus

## S. 287

287
12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel
had built in the east of Rome to assist in the city’s urban 
development. Its facade embodies typical early 17th-century 
architectural features. With a clear horizontal articulation, it 
is divided into two orders. A central pediment and a vertical­
ly coherent system of pilasters shape the façade, and a 
carefully calibrated relief emphasizes the center.
THE MEANING OF TERESA OF AVILA
The autobiographical narrative of Teresa’s visions and 
the visual representations they generated were undoubtedly 
the starting point of the Cornaro chapel and of Bernini’s 
sculpture. The biographical, historical and religious elements 
define different themes within the work:
• 
Teresa is an example: both for inner reform (purifica­
tion of the soul) and for reform of the Church (purifi­
cation of the Carmelite order);
• 
The heavenly visions she experiences with her body, 
and then her actions—an ‘interior’ and an ‘exterior’ 
aspect—attest to how she is an example. How does 
one represent that?
• 
The visions are shown in a context: a chapel in a 
church. How does that affect Bernini’s work?
• 
This influence elicits very strong responses to the 
sensuality of the work, the quality of the art, and its 
religious meaning.
The ways in which Bernini dealt with these themes and 
created the setting for the sculpture belong to a set of strat­
egies and motifs in what is now commonly called Baroque

## S. 288

288
architecture. These strategies and motifs became extremely 
influential during the period 1650 to 1750, across Europe 
and beyond. At the same time, the movement was never 
without harsh critics.
THE CORNARO CHAPEL 
The statue of Santa Teresa of Avila is part of the Cap­
pella Cornaro, or Cornaro Chapel, named after its patron, 
Federico Cornaro (1579–1653), located in one arm of the 
transept in the church of Santa Maria della Vittoria. Corna­
ro was a member of one of the most rich and powerful fami­
lies in Venice, a family from which had come multiple doges 
and seven cardinals, Frederico Cornaro included. Launched 
on a political and ecclesiastical career in Rome, he became a 
cardinal in 1626 and was cardinal and patriarch of Venice 
from 1631 until 1644. From 1644 until his death, he was 
considered one of the most powerful cardinals in Rome and 
thought to have the potential to be a future pope. He accu­
mulated enormous wealth, with which he built the Cornaro 
Chapel at a cost of between 13,000 and 14,000 scudi. To 
contextualize that figure, an individual average family in­
come at this time was around 15 scudi a year. 
Purpose of the Chapel 
The art historian W.L. Barcham wrote that in the 1640s, 
Cornaro ‘was sick and aged, noble and wealthy, and a dis­
tinguished prince of the Church. Human frailty as well as 
elevated social and ecclesiastical standing demanded that he

## S. 289

289
12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel
undertake the project.’ Cornaro then wanted to accomplish a 
number of things with the chapel:
• 
Show wealth and prestige;
• 
Celebrate the importance of his family and its ties to 
Rome;
• 
Build a majestic burial place for himself. In Christian 
faith, the practice of praying for the dead to save their 
souls before Judgment Day required a place where 
they could be remembered and prayed for intercession. 
For someone in his position, commissioning something 
like the chapel was both logical and expected. However, with 
the chapel, Cornaro fulfilled this expectation in a spectacular 
way.
The chapel was built in Santa Maria della Vittoria, the 
church of the Discalced Carmelites, for several reasons. The 
Discalced Carmelites at Santa Maria della Vittoria wanted 
to celebrate their patron saint and also to enhance their rep­
utation. The new church had also become fashionable; it 
was of a high artistic quality in a new part of Rome, and it 
already housed several chapels for important families. Corn­
aro obtained the right to build the chapel for free, which was 
exceptional. Normally one would have had to pay for this 
right, as this was an important source of income for reli­
gious orders. In one of his many functions, Cornaro had 
inspected the monastery to see if it was managed according 
to the rules laid out by Teresa. As apparently this was not 
the case, he then received the transept for his chapel without 
having made any payment for it. The chapel served as a sort 
of currency, as a trade-off, a demonstration of how religion

## S. 290

290
and politics were intertwined. Cornaro, facing his own mor­
tality, wanted to affirm his status by employing the artist of 
popes and kings and by doing so, expressing the prominence 
of his family in Rome.
GIAN LORENZO BERNINI
As soon as he was offered the transept in the church, 
Cornaro began to work with Gian Lorenzo Bernini (1598–
1680). As the most important artist in 17th-century Rome, 
Bernini exerted a far-reaching control over the general artis­
tic production in the city. For example, from the late 1650s 
he had been designing and supervising the construction of 
St. Peter’s Square (see chapter 11). In a recent exhibition, 
he was called the ‘Regista del Barocco’, the director of the 
Baroque. Trained as a sculptor by his father, Pietro, he later 
worked as a painter and an architect. He began working at 
the time of the pontificate of Urban VIII (who was pope 
from 1623 to 1644). While continuing to serve private cli­
ents, he quickly became the pre-eminent artist of the popes 
because of his great ability in manipulating marble. As his 
career developed close to the papal court, one of his key 
roles was to represent the papacy—both by portraying popes 
in images and sculptures and by realizing the artistic pro­
jects promoted by them. As such, he was a major player in 
the popes’ ‘artistic diplomacy’, or soft power, through the 
production of more visual and built expressions for those in 
power than any other artist of the time.
He very quickly acquired fame with the execution of the 
statues collected at the Villa Borghese, which are complex

## S. 291

291
12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel
and dynamic compositions depicting mythical subjects. 
These sculptures reveal Bernini’s high level of expressive 
virtuosity in the details of their strong yet very human emo­
tions. These three aspects, of movement, emotion, and 
detail, undergo a sort of continuous transformation in his 
work, as if matter is not stable but itself changes continu­
ously.
Among the fashionable elite that gathered at the Villa 
Borghese, Bernini was soon celebrated as the ‘new Michelan­
gelo’. The project of establishing a ‘new Michelangelo’ was 
in fact a very explicit program with great political conse­
quences, as it could expand and cement the areas and 
influence of patrons. In the dynamic relationship between 
Urban VIII and the artist, in which the pope was a close 
patron and supporter, Bernini was commissioned to execute 
increasingly elaborate projects, and architecture and paint­
ing were soon added to his portfolio. 
Among early architectural commissions was the new 
façade and restoration of the Santa Bibiana church, ca. 
1625, and a statue of the saint in the apse. At that time, 
Bernini worked with Maderno and Borromini, the preeminent 
architectural figures in Rome in the early 17th century. 
Bernini became the architect of St. Peter’s Basilica in 1629 
and was responsible for the design of the crossing of the 
basilica (1637–42). Within the basilica he also realized the 
Baldacchino (1626–33), the Tomb of Urban VIII (1627–47), 
and the Tomb for Matilda of Canossa (1633–44). 
Fountains are another important chapter of Bernini’s 
production. The theme of movement and transformation play

## S. 292

292
with the contraposition of stone and water and light and 
allowed him to realize inventive shapes. Throughout his 
career, he also designed a number of chapels for families 
related to the papal court. 
Bernini’s Cappella Raimondi (1638–48) at S. Pietro in 
Montorio is an important precursor to the Cappella Cornaro. 
The walls of the Raimondi Chapel, constructed of a mono­
chromatic white marble, are extremely rich, decorated with 
many figurative details that provide three-dimensionality to 
an otherwise uniform material. The sculpted altarpiece, 
recalling the one in Santa Bibiana, plays with indirect, hid­
den light coming from behind the pediment of the altar, 
which amplifies the effect of lightness and spiritual exulta­
tion of St. Francis (who also is shown in ecstasy) and trans­
mits that effect to the spectator. The position of the open­
ings through which the light enters also ensures that the 
lighting of the altarpiece varies over the course of the day. 
In the main space of the Raimondi Chapel, a strict hori­
zontal articulation is enlivened by a pronounced vertical 
articulation of pilasters, frames, and columns: the walls and 
the columns melt into each other and almost become a 
curtain surrounding the space of the chapel. The idea of a 
narrative backdrop (‘scenografia’) is visible in the images of 
the two men buried there, who are shown twice, as skeletons 
awaiting Judgment Day and as praying devotees. The gaze 
of the two figures plays with the spectators and with the 
altar, articulating a scene.
While working on the Raimondi Chapel between the late 
1630s and mid-1640s, Bernini also worked on what would

## S. 293

293
12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel
soon become his downfall: completing St. Peter’s façade by 
adding two bell towers. After they were finished, the towers, 
commissioned by Urban VIII, had to be removed because of 
a structural problem with the façade. Bernini was then 
deemed responsible for a poor design. What should have 
been his consecration as a total artist—painter, sculptor, 
architect— and a ‘new Michelangelo’, became his undoing, at 
least temporarily. Bernini’s fall from popularity also had to 
do with political circumstances. In 1644, when Bernini had 
begun building the towers, his patron, Urban VIII died. He 
was replaced by Innocent X, who hated Urban VIII and 
therefore Bernini as well. During a period of temporary seclu­
sion form the public sphere, Bernini sculpted Truth Unveiled 
by Time (incomplete), a statue which suggests that at some 
point in the future, the truth about his innocence for the 
towers project would be revealed and his reputation would be 
reestablished. 
Indeed, Bernini’s disgrace was only temporary, and the 
Cornaro Chapel provided an opportunity to reestablish 
himself as a genius artist. In a way, Cornaro and Bernini 
emerged from the same milieu in Rome and can both be 
considered ‘creatures’ of Urban VIII. Also, just like Cornaro 
made a career in Venice, so Bernini had long been of interest 
to Venice. In 1631 the city had attempted to persuade him 
to work on a new church, Santa Maria della Salute. When 
Cornaro turned to Bernini for his chapel around 1647, 
Bernini took Cornaro’s commission as an opportunity to 
showcase his talent and secure his access to the best pa­
trons again.

## S. 294

294
THE ECSTASY OF ST. TERESA
The Cornaro Chapel is located in a shallow transept of 
the church of Santa Maria della Vittoria. The ceiling is an 
arched vault. The two walls leading inward are vivid with 
marble and sets of figures in high sculptural relief. 
Everything about the chapel is but a stage for the main 
focus, the dramatic statue of St. Teresa, set within a niche 
that is framed by columns and a pediment. The composition 
of the statue is worth analyzing, to understand how it 
achieves its effect on the viewer that have come to define it 
as typical Baroque: 
• 
It has no base, allowing a deliberate play with the 
weight of the marble and the idea of suspension: the 
block seems to be weightless, the body of Teresa 
seems to float.
• 
It is realized in a single material, but despite its being 
monochrome, it expresses a variety of different fabrics 
and materials: clouds, cloth, skin, etc. 
• 
The violent movement and autonomy of Teresa’s 
garment, and its sheer size, is notable; it hides the 
entire body, except for hands, feet, and face. 
• 
The calmness of St. Teresa’s face and limbs is a 
striking contrast to the movement of the garment. It 
seems as if the garment lives/is awake, while Teresa 
languishes.
• 
Cloth and cloud seem to transition into each other.
The scene depicts the moment before the angel pierces 
the heart of Teresa with his golden arrow. The action is 
suspended in a snapshot of time, creating an image at the

## S. 295

295
12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel
threshold of an extreme motion. The movement seems to be 
announced or already present by the fabric of the garment; it 
is bunched and flows from the center, which becomes the 
most moving and powerful part of the composition; it is as if 
the garment stands in for, and expresses, the invisible expe­
rience of Teresa.
The expressiveness of the spiritual experience of the saint 
is achieved by a few details. The face, with mouth slightly 
open, eyes half-closed, and head falling back, conveys ecsta­
sy; the two hands are completely relaxed, in a state of aban­
donment; and a bare foot, symbol of the religious order, 
hangs out of her garment. The spectator experiences both 
the presence of Teresa and her transfiguration: she is there, 
but not quite; she vanishes into her clothes that convey the 
energizing of her spirit. The angel, on the other hand, is far 
more corporeal and serene; even the light helps define the 
material quality of this part of the work.
THE CORNARO CHAPEL 
The sculpture is framed within an oval space by a pedi­
ment resting on four columns that follows the curve of the 
oval, receding in the front, so that it seems as if the sections 
above the columns stand up under tension. The sculptural 
space opens toward the chapel and the church, at the same 
time set beyond the screen of the niche frame. This empha­
sizes the theme of penetration. It also allows for a particular 
play with light. The source of light is hidden behind the 
pediment but washes over the scene below, directed from a 
window. Natural light also falls on the beams of sculpted

## S. 296

296
light behind the statue, emphasizing and reflecting off them. 
This provides the effect of a space that opens up toward the 
sky, or toward heaven, under which the composition of St. 
Teresa and the angel is taking place. 
The Oval
The footprint of the niche containing the sculpture is an 
oval whose transverse axis faces the rectangular chapel. It is 
part of Bernini’s long experimentation with the oval plan. 
The form is essentially ambiguous, referencing the perfection 
of the circle but also able to give direction and create an 
embrace, almost as if the whole architecture of the niche has 
been forced open and deformed by a divine energy. This 
dynamic of ‘revelation’ is further emphasized by the hidden 
light coming from above.
Where the Tempietto embodies the celebration of the 
perfection of the circle, from the mid-16th century onwards 
the circle is manipulated into the oval. The Sforza Chapel in 
Santa Maria Maggiore in Rome by Michelangelo (1562–73) 
is an early example of how the circular shape began to be 
loosened and corrupted. The dome of the church of Sant’An­
drea in via Flaminia in Rome by Vignola (1552–53), which 
from the outside is perceived as circular, is an oval, above 
an elongated space that strongly directs the viewer toward 
the space dedicated to the altar. This idea of shaping a 
space is also evident in Francesco da Volterra’s restoration 
of the main altar in Santa Pudenziana (1588). Here the 
altar fits into the sequence of bays but also becomes partly 
autonomous. This theme was tested again by Volterra, this

## S. 297

297
12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel
time at the scale of a church in San Giacomo degli Incurabili 
(1592), which is both centralizing and directed toward the 
altar, creating an immersive space that is hierarchically 
organized.
Bernini had experimented already with the oval in the 
fountain of the Barcaccia (1627–29), absorbing the two axes 
of Piazza di Spagna. More powerfully in the Cappella dei Re 
Magi, in the Collegio di Propaganda Fide, he used the oval 
to set the entrance to the chapel in direct proximity to the 
altar, providing a sense of physical embrace. The theme is 
promoted on a monumental scale in St. Peter’s Square, 
where the colonnades create an ‘embrace’ at an urban scale 
(see chapter 11).
The idea of undulation as invitation, embrace or pulsa­
tion reaches an extreme level in the work of Francesco Bor­
romini (1599–1667), who experimented with the oval shape 
at the same time as Bernini. The oval motif invited archi­
tects to explore the relationship between columns and walls. 
Renaissance architecture had established two distinct and 
related systems, the orders of columns and the walls (see 
chapter 5). Each had their own tectonic logic, design strate­
gy, and ornament. With the Baroque, the two realms began 
to fuse. The most explicit gesture of this union is the archi­
trave that follows the curve of the wall, taking the columns 
with it. This is very visible in the Cornaro Chapel. The archi­
trave itself becomes fragmented, broken, jagged, an object of 
plastic manipulation.
This language implied a quest for three-dimensionality 
using the system of the orders. As a consequence, the atten­

## S. 298

298
tion shifts to ‘secondary’ elements, such as cornices and 
profiles. They become not just means of expression—as in 
Michelangelo’s Laurentian library— but to structure or define 
architecture. Pietro da Cortona’s facades of Santa Maria 
della Pace (1656–67) and SS. Luca e Martina (1635–69) 
are clear examples.
The architectural system containing St. Teresa’s statue in 
the Cornaro Chapel is structured in a very legible hierarchi­
cal system of pilasters and entablatures, with each level of 
scale locked into another. The movement of the structure 
then culminates in the pediment. This is a primary element 
in Bernini’s scenography, setting a stage for the emblematic 
activity of the sculpture of St. Teresa. The pediment focuses 
the eye of the viewer on the scene unfolding beneath it, at 
the same time allowing the eye to also move upward, follow­
ing the plastic slopes of the triangle, to the painted heavens 
in the vaulted arch of the chapel ceiling.
Marbles Between Heaven and Earth
The chapel’s structural balance is articulated through the 
complex use of marble. The variety of marble used within 
the composition, seventeen kinds in total, weaves the chapel 
together, structuring the different elements in a complex syn­
tax and enhancing the scenographic effect through a subtle 
interplay with the expectations of the beholder. This enor­
mous variety of colored marble, most of it highly mottled or 
veined, called ‘mixed marbles’, contrasts with the mono­
chrome of the statue of St. Teresa. The material articulation

## S. 299

299
12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel
also creates an interplay between surface and architectural 
elements, suggesting different interpretations.
While Bernini followed precedents, he also challenged 
them. The Cappella Paolina in Santa Maria Maggiore by 
Flaminio Pozzo (1605–1617), is based on a clear distinction 
between architectural elements and wall surfaces. Like the 
Cornaro Chapel, the surfaces of the Cappella Paolina are 
also very rich, but each piece of colored marble is framed by 
structural elements in white marble that provide a clear 
hierarchy. As defined by treatises such as Vincenzo Scamoz­
zi’s L’Idea dell’Architettura Universale of 1605 (see chapter 
4), white marble is used to make architecture luminous and 
legible.
On the other hand, in the Cornaro there are moments of 
ambiguity, where marble starts to take over the articulation 
of different elements. Both the structural elements and their 
frames are colorful, rather than white. Marble surfaces are 
also treated in various ways: sometimes as abstract surfac­
es, sometimes as figures. Marble sometimes evokes objects 
or fabrics, sometimes it is a background, sometimes it is the 
building material of the chapel. Bernini was able to mix 
these different registers while using the same material. 
The complex use of marble in the Cornaro Chapel also 
has to do with 17th-century ideas about this material. Marble 
was considered to be the petrified expression of the combi­
nation of the four elements (water, earth, fire, air), grown 
from the soil by the grace of God: a stabilized instability and 
a petrified mixture. Spotted and veined marble in particular 
was seen as an interaction of these elements. In the painting

## S. 300

300
by Orazio Gentileschi, The Fall of the Rebel Angels, of 1591, 
the vein in the stone is used to evoke the clouds of heaven 
from which angels descend, creating the representation of an 
in-between world: between matter and spirit, air and earth. 
The same is enacted in the Cornaro Chapel, where the rela­
tionship between veined marble panels is deeply interwoven: 
darker in the lower area, ascending and becoming lighter in 
the upper part. This refers to the transposition of real mate­
rial into an ideal one, culminating in the richly painted, 
illusionistic ceiling. The painted heavens seem to burst open 
over the space and wash over the architecture.
The chapel has a second window in the upper section 
that participates in the heavenly scene. The space of the 
chapel should then be seen as a world in motion. The com­
position of light and shadow reinforces the association of 
heaven on earth. The upper window brings light into the 
painted heaven above the pediment. The mixed instability of 
creation is carried over to the medium of expression: paint­
ing becomes sculpture, sculpture becomes architecture, 
architecture becomes painting. What is materialized through 
the play of matter is the idea of heaven on earth, or the 
transformation of earth into heaven. A material metamor­
phosis is therefore enacted: the painted heaven is made of 
stucco, which makes it sculptural; this sculpture in turn 
becomes architecture; the architecture which is materialized 
in marble becomes a painting. Bernini’s play with materials 
was already very well understood at the time, as demonstrat­
ed by literature; he was valued for his ability to make the 
religious mystery visible by the skilled placing and manipula­
tion stone and light.

## S. 301

301
12. Bernini and the Cornaro Chapel
Cornaro’s Presence in the Chapel
As the patron, Federico Cornaro’s person appears in the 
chapel in several instances. As in Bernini’s Chapel Raimondi, 
a carving in high relief of Cornaro is placed to one side of 
the chapel, where he sits together with other members of his 
family behind a prayer bench and admires the sculpture of 
St. Teresa. The figures of the Cornaro family appear seated 
in a sort of opera box, in an optimal position to view the 
scene enacted before them. The illusion of receding perspec­
tive behind them is achieved through a low-relief composi­
tion that recalls the architecture of a palace rather than that 
of a church. Some of the figures interact with each other, 
turning away from the figure of St. Teresa, while others are 
clearly watching the niche. In this position, Cornaro becomes 
immediately visible when entering the chapel. In fact, the 
arrangement of the figures gives the impression that they are 
sitting in the space together with the viewer, experiencing 
the same scene of St. Teresa’s moment of ecstasy. The 
representations of the Cornaro family work as mediator 
between Saint Teresa and the spectator, between heaven and 
earth.
Cornaro is buried beneath the floor of the chapel, his 
burial space indicated by black stones inlaid with depictions 
of two skeletons, who seem to be emerging from the under­
world, both with their hands raised in classic supplicatory 
gestures. This carving is a counterpart to the painted ceil­
ing, the base of a vertical axis between earth and heaven. It 
also places Cornaro’s body close to the altar of the chapel, 
where the prayers for his soul would be recited.

## S. 302

302
CONCLUSION
In the Cornaro Chapel, architecture and other arts enter 
in a dynamic relationship, behaving as if they were ‘some­
thing else’ (painting turns into sculpture, sculpture into 
architecture…). Similarly, light becomes material and mate­
rial becomes light. This continuous process of transfigura­
tion is intended, metaphorically and experientially, as a 
transformation of earthly into heavenly space. As a result, 
the Cornaro Chapel reveals the following architectural 
themes of the Baroque:
1. Elements like the niche and the oval plan contribute 
to dynamic spaces.
2. The architecture is less articulated by a system de­
pendent on the orders, or on the relationship between 
column and wall (i.e. a two-dimensional system), than 
by an interplay between wall surfaces, frames, and 
moldings. Secondary elements of the orders, like 
cornices, become primary elements of design and are 
heavily manipulated.
3. Emphasis is on the materiality of architecture. and 
the explicit use of color and monochromy creates a 
tension that can be played with.
4. The three arts of architecture, sculpture, and painting 
are used together as a whole.
5. Scenography is employed explicitly to engage with the 
theme of seeing.
6. The designer clearly intends to appeal to and draw 
the viewer into the composition.

305

Christina of 
Sweden

Rome, Italy:

Palazzo Riario (completely rebuilt in 1730 and 
renamed Palazzo Corsini);

## S. 307

307
13. The Rome of Christina of Sweden
INTRODUCTION 
As seen previously (chapter 11), 17th-century Rome under 
Sixtus V (1585–90) underwent a vast and very dynamic 
process of urban development. One of the primary accom­
plishments of Sixtus V was the establishment of a network 
of roads, connecting crucial points, monuments, and build­
ings around the city, and often marked by obelisks. These 
roads passed through parts of Rome that until then had 
been uninhabited or underpopulated (disabitato), which also 
established a system of accesses to the city.
The project of reconnecting the urban ‘tissue’ of Rome, 
which included marking its intersections and connecting the 
building works that accompanied this massive construction 
process, were promoted by an equally impressive propaganda 
project. The purpose of this project was twofold: to make 
Rome more accessible and to improve the quality of life in 
the city. Moreover, this new road system facilitated devotion­
al life in Rome, because it provided direct access to the most 
important churches of the city. In a way, access roads and 
monuments were a way to reconstruct the territory of Rome, 
giving the whole city a new coherence, both practical and 
visual.
In 1655, the visit of Christina of Sweden in Rome was an 
occasion to celebrate the city’s newly established territorial 
coherence. This event is represented in a print from 1655, in 
which a long procession moves through the city, from Porta 
del Popolo, past the Arco di Portogallo, Castel St Angelo, 
and ending at St. Peter’s. Each of these moments was indi­
cated by an obelisk.

## S. 308

308
How architecture is used to frame and commemorate both 
events and power relationships, with the examples of Porta 
del Popolo, St. Peter’s Square, and Palazzo Riario.
The designs of Porta del Popolo and St. Peter’s Church.
The different ways in which architectural and urban spa­
ce is represented in different kinds of images (procession 
prints, guidebooks, views of ceremonies), and what is 
being represented.
Through the figure of Christina, how religion, politics, and 
gender relationships are articulated in architecture and 
urban space.
TO KNOW

## S. 309

309
13. The Rome of Christina of Sweden
The Rome of Christina: Engraving depicting the entrance of Christina Sweden in Rome in 1655.

## S. 310

310
CHRISTINA OF SWEDEN AND THE THIRTY YEARS’ 
WAR
Christina of Sweden (1626–89), known as the Minerva of 
the North, inherited the throne at the age of five, when her 
father, King Gustav II, was killed in 1632. In the perception 
of Catholics, Gustav was an enemy who supported Protes­
tant Reformers and attacked Catholic centers across Europe. 
For this he was even nicknamed the Lion of the North.
The ability for a woman to be a sovereign ruler had 
become possible only in the previous year. The legitimacy of 
Christina as the successor a ferocious Protestant warrior 
was in part founded on her physical appearance and her 
resemblance to the king. People recognized her as the natu­
ral successor the Lion of the North. She received the same 
upbringing and education as a young prince: in addition to 
learning fencing and horse-riding, she was given the best 
humanistic education one could have. She spoke five lan­
guages, knew theology, and so on.
The backdrop to Christina’s upbringing was the violent 
Thirty Years’ War, the religious conflict throughout Europe 
that ended in 1648, with the Peace of Westphalia. This 
treaty divided Europe into Catholic and Protestant spheres. 
The three rulers who signed the treaty were Louis XIV of 
France, Emperor Ferdinand of the Holy Roman Empire, and 
22-year old Christina. This treaty was seen by the papacy in 
Rome as an enormous defeat, as it recognized the legitimacy 
of Protestant Reformers and of Protestantism and its vari­
ous forms as a state religion. This was the case for Sweden, 
where it was even illegal to be a Catholic.

## S. 311

311
Christina grew up in a newly divided Europe and was 
seen as one of the emblems of Protestantism and Lutheran­
ism. At the same time, the Thirty Years’ War was actually 
very beneficial for Sweden, as the Swedish troops looted and 
stole artworks, books, and valuable artifacts from Catholic 
courts, such as Prague. The arrival of this new knowledge 
supplemented Christina’s humanistic education and stimu­
lated the artistic life of Stockholm. During Christina’s reign, 
René Descartes came to visit and ended up staying in Stock­
holm until his death. One of the topics discussed by Christi­
na with Descartes was the meaning of religion and religiosi­
ty. Because of her education, Christina was able to question 
her faith and the values of Protestantism.
THE CONVERSION OF CHRISTINA
Despite already being Sweden’s sovereign, Christina was 
officially crowned in 1650, in a lavish celebration with trium­
phal arches built for the purpose, a new silver throne made 
just for her, and a three-day feast. However, she did not 
want to be queen, perhaps because she did not want to 
marry and carry a child. She said that ‘it requires more 
courage to marry than to go to war’. This may also have to 
do with her religiosity and the fact that, as she grew up as 
queen-elect, she began to doubt her Protestant faith. Chris­
tina put plans in place that would allow her to eventually 
leave the Swedish throne and abdicate her Lutheran (Protes­
tant) faith. 
In 1649 Christina made her cousin, Carl Gustav, heir to 
the throne, and she abdicated just five years later. When 
13. The Rome of Christina of Sweden

## S. 312

312
Carl Gustav was made king in 1654, he issued a medal, 
inscribed with the words ‘By God and Christina’, and show­
ing his coronation by Christina. The imagery of this medal 
very explicitly puts Gustav’s right to the throne in the hands 
of Christina, through which the will of God is articulated. 
Christina left her throne at a time of great national 
prosperity. While still queen, Jesuits came to see her, which 
suggests that her decision to abdicate was also motivated 
by a growing interest in Catholicism. The year after abdicat­
ing, Christina left Sweden and embarked on a long journey 
to Rome. Christina, with her humanistic education, would 
have been interested in visiting the classical capital of Eu­
rope, which was also the capital of Catholicism, the home of 
a religion that she found more to her liking than Protestant­
ism.
Christina, an unusual and complex person, traveled 
through Europe, where she was still considered a queen, 
though a queen without a land. She continued to occupy the 
top ranks of the social hierarchy. At the same time, as a 
queen she was imbued with Lutheranism, despite being 
clearly moved towards Catholicism. Also, because she was a 
female ruler, most countries in Europe would have consid­
ered her unusual, as she took on roles that were traditional­
ly associated with a male ruler. In almost every aspect of her 
political, religious, and social persona, therefore, Christina 
was an exception.

## S. 313

313
CHRISTINA’S ARRIVAL IN ROME
In Brussels, Christina converted to Catholicism, but her 
conversion was no private matter. Her ‘profession’ of faith, 
the document stating her intention to become a Catholic, 
was printed and made public, as a substantial piece of 
Catholic propaganda. And as part of that propagandistic 
intention, Christina’s entry into Rome in 1655 was vigorously 
celebrated. Christina, the ‘star from the north’, finally found 
her way to Rome. 
Porta del Popolo
Christina’s procession began at Porta del Popolo, one of 
the ancient entrances set along the Aurelian walls of Rome. 
At the time, the piazza in front of the gate was an oblong 
open space. The gate as it appears today was rebuilt under 
pope Pius IV (r. 1559–65) after a design by Nanni di Baccio 
Bigio (perhaps with the aid of Michelangelo), but left unfin­
ished. It was completed by Bernini in 1655, the year of 
Christina of Sweden’s visit. 
Bernini’s project, especially in the decoration of the inside 
of the gate, makes clear reference to Michelangelo’s Porta 
Pia (1561–65), both in the use of materials (brick and 
travertine) and the use of the volutes connected by a wreath 
above the central arch. Nestled into the swag of the wreath 
is the central feature of Bernini’s design: a pyramid of six 
mountains, topped by a large star, and below the wreath, 
the monumental inscription ‘Felici faustoque ingressui anno 
domini mdclv’ (‘To a happy and blessed entry in the year of 
13. The Rome of Christina of Sweden

## S. 314

314
the Lord 1655’). The star hovering over the mountains, or 
monti, visible on the exterior of the gate as well, as part of 
a coat of arms, rises high above the level of the ancient 
Roman wall, and is thus visible outside of the city, behind 
the scuptled representations of guards on the top (another 
reference to the Porta Pia). Six mountains with a star are 
the main elements of the coat of arms of Pope Alexander 
VII family, the Chigi, who commissioned the gate to com­
memorate the entrance of Queen Christina of Sweden into 
Rome in 1655. In the context of Christina’s arrival, the star 
then served as a dual metaphor, alluding to both Christina, 
‘the star of the north’, and the Chigi family, as if Providence 
itself had arranged the meeting between Christina and the 
pope.
St. Peter’s Church
Christina’s procession in 1655 ended at St. Peter’s 
Square, the heart of Western Christianity. As already seen 
previously (chapter 11), from the 14th century onwards Vati­
can City underwent a substantial process of change, in order 
to turn the place into a new capital of Christianity. From the 
late 16th century onwards the site underwent several substan­
tial changes, thanks to the initiatives of Nicholas V (1447-
55), Julius II (1503-13) and Sixtus V (1585-90). These 
included the completion of the dome of St. peter’s church, 
the replacement of its façade, the completion of the Vatican 
Palace and the addition of an elaborate system of staircases 
to the compound.

## S. 315

315
The story of the development of St. Peter’s Basilica is a 
complex one. After Nicholas V initiated the project, work 
continued in the early 16th century with Julius II. Bramante 
was put in charge to develop a complete project for both the 
church and the Vatican compound, which was scaled after 
the one proposed by Nicholas V. Bramante proposed a 
centralized building for the new church, unlike the previous 
building (called Old St. Peter’s), which was longitudinal. He 
also designed the tigurium, a protective structure to enclose 
the tomb of St. Peter during the years of construction of the 
larger church on the site of the old one, which continued to 
be used and was gradually demolished as the new building 
progressed. Around the mid-16th century, when Michelangelo 
was put in charge of the project, the church looked like an 
assemblage of parts, with the old longitudinal nave and the 
new centralized building side by side. In late 16th-century 
engravings, the church appears simplified with the addition 
of a dividing wall standing between the old and the new 
church. The dome at that point was emerging with the drum 
and the facade was a collection of different parts, old and 
new.
The next step in the progressive formalization of St. 
Peter’s and its spaces was the facade of the church, com­
pleted in the early 17th century by Carlo Maderno. As a 
result, Michelangelo’s church, which was conceived in plan 
following Bramante’s proposal, was in turn extended into an 
elongated, basilica shape. One of Maderno’s choices was to 
maintain the width of Michelangelo’s church, resulting in a 
very large nave. The facade is therefore also very broad and 
high. To counter this aspect, Maderno employed a succes­
sion of columns, providing three-dimensionality and variety 
13. The Rome of Christina of Sweden

## S. 316

316
to the system. Nevertheless, the facade is so large that, by 
standing in front of it, Michelangelo’s dome becomes almost 
invisible. 
By 1628, the church was almost completed. The process 
of progressive monumentalization of St Peter’s church was 
completed in the months following Christina’ reception in 
1655 with Bernini’s design of the square in front of the 
church (see chapter 11). The image of the two arms of the 
oval piazza embracing the faithful also mirrors very closely 
what had happened in 1655, when Christina arrived in Rome, 
even though of course the square was not yet completed. 
Christina was a heretic, but she was ‘embraced’ and wel­
comed by the Catholic church.
CHRISTINA IN ROME AS A FOREIGN BODY
The conversion of Christina, celebrated with the large 
procession to St. Peter’s Square, was captured in a painting 
by Angelo Trevisani. The female monarch is depicted kneel­
ing in front of the pope. The personification of heresy, a 
snake-like figure, crawls away in the foreground, and the em­
bodiment of the Catholic Church oversees this moment from 
above. However, the conversion of Christina did not really 
turn out as planned. In fact, Christina always remained a 
‘foreign body’: a queen in the city of the pope, a Swede in 
Rome, and a female who did not conform to expected behav­
iors in a city governed essentially by an all-male hierarchy. 
In Rome, Christina became a different center of authority, in 
a state of constant tension with the papacy. In other words, 
Christina tells a story which is parallel but to some extent in

## S. 317

317
contrast with the official architectural narrative of St. Peter’s 
explained so far, with Bernini as the heroic architect of the 
new church.
From Palazzo Farnese to Palazzo Riario
When Christina arrived in Rome, she was housed in the 
Palazzo Farnese, one of the main Renaissance palaces in 
Rome. Christina rented the palace from the Farnese family, 
who was looking for political support. Similarly, the Barber­
ini family invited Christina to their palace, which had one of 
the first theatres in Rome. Despite being courted by different 
political parties, Christina’s relationship with the pope was 
complicated from the very beginning. Alexander VIII wanted 
to parade her as a trophy and engage her in a campaign of 
recatholicization of the north, a project which Christina cate­
gorically refused. She also refused to make any public dis­
play of piety. 
A mediator, Decio Azzolino, was engaged to facilitate the 
relationship between her and the pope. Azzolino became 
Christina’s closest confidant and ended up inheriting her 
wealth after her death, which in turn generated rumors about 
their relationship. Christina also continued to foster political 
ambitions. This included an attempt to become the queen of 
Naples, with the support of France. When she visited Fon­
tainebleau, near Paris, she was depicted in the public press 
as a ‘transvestite’ knight, potentially delegitimizing her as 
queen. 
13. The Rome of Christina of Sweden

## S. 318

318
As her public image became increasingly ambiguous, 
Christina began to develop her own centers of power. She 
bought the early 16th-century Palazzo Riario, an L-shaped 
building with an extensive garden located in south side of 
Rome, between the Vatican and Trastevere, a rural and 
peripheral area of the city. Christina transformed the build­
ing substantially, as a way to establish her role in the public 
spheres of Rome. In the ground floor, she turned the court­
yard into a grand entrance. On the first floor (piano nobile), 
she removed the walls in the first room after the staircase, 
making the succession of rooms leading up to her apart­
ments conform to the traditional layout of 17th-century 
Roman palaces: staircase; a large hall for the guards (pala­
frenieri); a sequence of antechambers (anticamere); a cham­
ber for audience (udienza); private rooms. This sort of 
sequence, known as enfilade, was dictated by the rules of 
guest reception and by roles of etiquette and hierarchy. 
Social status was measured by the speed with which one 
would move through this sequence of rooms. The faster you 
are ushered through, the more important you are. The oppo­
site applies to the host. A guest is shown respect by the 
position taken by the host along this sequence of rooms. In 
other words, this spatial system is designed to articulate 
social and political relations. This spatial system had the 
same ritualistic characters than the architectural layout of 
more grandiose projects, such as Versailles (see chapter 14).
Christina’s Palazzo Riario also included the Sala delle 
Muse, a room purposely set up to display a collection of 
statues bought by Christina. The Sala dell’Alcova was the 
bedroom, where Christina received her guests. Finally, Chris­
tina kept a large collection of paintings in the Stanza dei

## S. 319

319
Quadri. She was a great admirer of Raphael, so much so 
that she wanted to build a museum dedicated to him. Palaz­
zo Riario was completely rebuilt in 1730 and renamed Palaz­
zo Corsini. The aura the Christina is still present, as it 
continues to be known as one of the most important intel­
lectual centers of 17th-century Rome.
Power Relations with the Papacy
One of the ways in which Christina could negotiate her 
relationship with the papacy and gain relevance in the Vati­
can was by getting involved in the electoral process of the 
pope. The papacy was and still is an elective monarchy, 
where a new pope is elected after the death of the current 
monarch. After the death of Alexander VII, Christina en­
dorsed Clement IX, who became pope in 1667. In turn, 
Clement had a more relaxed relationship with Christina than 
Alexander VII. In a painting from 1668, the pope is depicted 
during a banquet with Christina. The pope sits at the same 
table but on a higher level than Christina, suggesting hierar­
chy but also equivalence. The space is laid out as a throne, 
and the event is highly staged, with an audience of guards 
and guests, as well as other people ‘peeping’ from behind 
the textile walls on the sides. In the logics of representation, 
this is a highly ritualized space, part of a scenography 
staged for an event. This theatricality is key to understand 
social relationships around the papacy. It suggests that, in 
a way, you could only be yourself in the context of an enact­
ment. The truth existed only if it was staged as such. 
13. The Rome of Christina of Sweden

## S. 320

320
This was also true in the solemn rituals of canonization 
(the processes by which a saint was proclaimed), which took 
place in St. Peter’s Basilica. The physical position of Christi­
na during this highly symbolic and decorated rituals be­
comes important to understand her social and political 
position. From two successive engraving of St. Peter’s church 
during canonizations, we see that in 1665 Christina was 
seated in a box, on the same level as other members of the 
Roman nobility. In 1669, Christina has moved to her little 
own throne, in a more direct visual relationship with the 
pope but on a lower level. As with the highly ritualized 
rooms of Palazzo Riario, here again architecture was adopt­
ed to embody power relationships.

## S. 321

321
13. The Rome of Christina of Sweden
CONCLUSION 
When Christina died in 1689, she received a grand funer­
al. After four days of mourning, her body was transported to 
St Peter’s, where she would be buried, in a procession simi­
lar to the one for her arrival in Rome. At the time, Christina 
was only the fourth woman buried in St Peter’s. The ques­
tion of how we should understand Christina within the histo­
ry of Catholicism is a complicated one. A great amount of 
literature has been produced on the subject, focusing on her 
religiosity, her faith, and her ambitions. Already during the 
17th and 18th centuries, the topic of Christina was ‘adopted’ 
to discuss more important questions of religion. Over the 
20th century, literature has focused on her controversial life 
from a more academic point of view.

## S. 360

Chair for the History 
and Theory of Architecture 
Maarten Delbeke
